، اور مسبب ( خمر ) بولکر سبب ( انگور ) مراد لیا، کیونکہ یہاں مسبب سبب کے ساتھ خاص ہے یعنی شراب بغیر انگور کے نہیں بنتی ہے۔ طرفین سے استعارہ کی مثال :- مصنف اس پر ایک مسئلہ متفرع کر رہے ہیں ، اس سے پہلے بطور تمہید کے کہتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے کہا ان اشتريت عبد ا فهو حر - اگر میں غلام کو خریدوں تو وہ آزاد ہے، پس اس
Ittisal suri means that there is a connection and neighborhood between the real meaning and the figurative meaning, like saying 'water' intending 'rain'; here the form of 'sama' (cloud) is connected to the form of rain because rain comes from clouds. In this example, 'sama' means cloud, because in common usage everything above is called 'sama', and clouds are also above, as in 'or like a downpour from the sky' [Quran 2:19] where 'sama' means cloud. In any case, in ittisal suri there is a connection of adjacency between the two; that is, one of the two meanings is a cause, reason, condition, or state, or any of the twenty-four categories of majaz mursal; all these are included in ittisal suri. Therefore, the author says that ittisal sababi is also of this type, i.e., it is the category of sabab, so it is ittisal suri. And it has two types: (1) connection of sabab and musabbab, (2) connection of illa and ma'lul. The author will leave aside all categories of majaz and discuss only these two. So he begins their detailed explanation: One of them is the connection of the ruling with the cause, like the connection of ownership with purchase, and it necessitates isti'ara from both sides, because the cause was not legislated except for its ruling, and the ruling is not established except by a cause; so the connection is equal, thus the isti'ara is general. And for this we said regarding the one who said 'If I buy a slave, he is free' – then he bought half a slave and sold it, then bought the other half – this latter half becomes free. But if he said 'If I own', he does not become free until the whole slave is united in his ownership. Then if he intended by one of them the other, his intention is acted upon in both places, but in what contains a mitigation for him, he is not believed in judicial matters but is believed in religious matters. Comment: Previously it was stated that ittisal sababi has two types; here he is explaining them. The first type is the connection of ruling with cause, i.e., the connection between cause and effect. For example, purchase is the cause and ownership is the effect (ruling). The connection between cause and effect is strong, so isti'ara from both sides is permissible: you can use the cause to mean the effect and the effect to mean the cause. In sabab and musabbab, the connection is weak, so isti'ara from both sides is not valid. The reason the first is strong and the second not is because in Shari'ah, 'illa' means that which is placed for a ruling; hence if a ruling is not conceivable in a certain place, the illa would not be legislated, like if ownership is not conceivable in a certain place, sale would not be legislated. So existence and obligation of the ruling are both attributed to the illa; i.e., the ruling exists and becomes obligatory by the illa. Thus it is known that the connection between cause and effect is strong: whenever there is the illa, there is the ruling, and whenever there is the ruling, there is the illa, because the illa is legislated only for the ruling, and the ruling is established only by the illa. So they are mutually dependent, thus the connection is strong. In sabab and musabbab, this is not the case, because the musabbab depends on the sabab, but the sabab does not need the musabbab: for the existence of the musabbab, the sabab is needed, but the existence of the sabab does not necessitate the existence of the musabbab. So the need is one-sided. Therefore, using the sabab to mean the musabbab is valid, but using the musabbab to mean the sabab is not valid, because the reality of isti'ara is that the malzum (the cause) is used to mean the lazim (the effect), not the lazim to mean the malzum. So when the musabbab depends on the sabab and not vice versa, the musabbab is the dependent (lazim) and the sabab is the independent (malzum). Hence, using the malzum (sabab) to mean the lazim (musabbab) is correct, and using the musabbab to mean the sabab is incorrect, except when the musabbab is specific to the sabab, e.g., 'I see myself pressing wine' [Quran 12:36] where 'wine' is intended to mean grapes; wine is the musabbab and grapes are the sabab, and the musabbab (wine) is used to mean the sabab (grapes) because the musabbab is specific to the sabab – wine is not made without grapes. Example of isti'ara from both sides: The author is branching a ruling from this. Before that, as an introduction, he says: If someone said 'If I buy a slave, he is free', then... [the example continues].
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the usul rule regarding figurative usage (isti'ara) based on the connection between cause and effect. The author first distinguishes between two types of connection (ittisal): connection of sabab (cause) and musabbab (effect), and connection of illa (legal cause) and ma'lul (effect). The focus here is on the latter, where the relationship is mutual and strong. The key point is that when the ruling (hukm) depends on the illa in both existence and obligation, the connection is equal, making isti'ara (metaphorical usage) permissible in both directions: using the illa to refer to the hukm and vice versa. The example of the man who says 'If I buy a slave, he is free' illustrates this: 'purchase' (illa) is used to mean 'ownership' (hukm), and the ruling of freedom applies to the purchased half even if the whole slave is not yet owned. In contrast, if he says 'If I own', which is the hukm itself, the ruling only applies when full ownership is established. This demonstrates that isti'ara from cause to effect is valid, but from effect to cause is not unless the effect is specific to the cause. The commentary also clarifies the difference with sabab-musabbab connection, where the dependence is one-sided, so only one-directional isti'ara is valid. This is a standard Hanafi usul analysis, emphasizing the role of 'illa' as a legislative indicator.

Key Terms
اتصال الحكم بالعلة (Ittisal al-hukm bi al-illa): The connection of a ruling with its legal cause, where both are mutually dependent.
استعارة (Isti'ara): Figurative usage where a word is used in a non-literal sense due to a connection (ittisal) between the real and figurative meanings.
علة (Illa): A legal cause that is legislated to bring about a ruling; it is mutually dependent with the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is isti'ara permissible from both sides in the case of ittisal al-hukm bi al-illa?
A) Because the illa is stronger than the hukm.
B) Because the connection is mutual and equal.
C) Because the ruling is the cause.
D) Because the Prophet (SAW) allowed it.

Key Takeaway
When the connection between cause and ruling is mutual (illa and hukm), isti'ara is bidirectional; when one-sided (sabab and musabbab), it is unidirectional.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Generality in Majaz: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Defines ruling of haqiqah and majaz, then disputes Shafi'i denial of generality in majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on generality in majaz, identify the Shafi'i objection based on necessity, and apply the dispute to determine the scope of riba in the hadith of sa'.

Core Idea
Majaz can be general like haqiqah; necessity does not restrict it to specificity.

In usul al-fiqh, there is consensus that haqiqah (literal usage) conveys generality or specificity based on the wording. The dispute arises when we move to majaz (metaphorical usage). The Hanafi school maintains that majaz shares the same ruling: if the metaphorical word is general in its non-original meaning, it conveys generality. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that majaz is only employed out of necessity and therefore must be limited to the specific case that necessitated it. This lesson unpacks both positions, their evidences, and the practical consequence for riba rulings.

Guided Path
1
Establish the rulings of haqiqah and majaz
Start by understanding that both schools agree on haqiqah: a word in its original meaning covers all individuals it includes, whether general or specific. For majaz, the author asserts the same ruling: it follows the word in its metaphorical meaning.
The passage explicitly states there is no disagreement on haqiqah.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof from the hadith
Examine the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor one sa' for two sa''. The word 'sa'' is metaphorical (for the contents), and the definite article 'al-' indicates istighraq (totality). Therefore the metaphor is general, covering all commodities.
This shows how the Hanafi school applies the principle of generality in majaz to derive that riba applies to both food and non-food items.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that majaz is only resorted to out of necessity. Since necessity is limited, the majaz must be limited to the specific meaning that fulfills that necessity. In the hadith, necessity is met by restricting 'sa'' to food items (mut'imat), so generality does not hold.
The Shafi'i view leads to riba applying only to food items, not to non-food like lime.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The author replies that the Shafi'i premise (majaz arises only from necessity) is false because the Qur'an contains abundant majaz, and Allah is free from any necessity. Examples: 'when the water overflowed' (tagha) and 'a wall intending to collapse' (yuridu) are metaphors used without any necessity.
This refutation undermines the Shafi'i basis and supports the Hanafi position that majaz can be general.

الفيضُ الحجازي شرح اردو المنتخب الحسامي وَلَا الصَّاعَ بالصَّاعَيْنِ عَامًا فِيمَا يَحْلُهُ وَيُجَاوِرُهُ وَأَبَى الشَّافَعِنُ هذلِكَ وَقَالَ لَا عُمُوْمَ لِلْمَجَازِ لِاَنَّهُ ضَرُورِيٌّ يُصَارُ الَيْهِ تَوْسِعَةً لِلْكَلَامِ وَهَذَا بَاطِلٌ لِاَنَّ الْمَجَازَ مَوْجُودٌ فِى كِتَابِ اللهِ تَعَالَى وَاللَّهُ تَعَالٰى يَتَعَالَى عَنِ الْعَجْز وَالضَّرُورَاتِ ترجمہ:۔ اور مجاز کا حکم اس معنی کا پایا جانا ہے جو مراد لیا جائے اس سے ، مجاز خاص ہو یا عام جیسا کہ یہی حقیقت کا حکم ہے ، اور اسی وجہ سے کر دیا ہم نے لفظ صاع کو عبداللہ بن عمر کی حدیث لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعین میں عام ان تمام چیزوں میں جو اس میں سماتی ہوں اور اس کے مجاور ہوتی ہوں اور امام شافعی نے اس کا انکار کیا اور کہا کہ مجاز کے لئے عموم نہیں ہے، اس لئے کہ مجاز ضرورۃ ثابت ہے : جس کی طرف رجوع کیا جاتا ہے کلام میں وسعت پیدا کرنے کے لئے، اور یہ باطل ہے، اس لئے کہ مجاز موجود ہے اللہ تعالی کی کتاب میں اور اللہ تعالی عجز اور ضرورتوں سے بالا تر ہیں۔ شریح:- مصنف حقیقت و مجاز کا حکم بیان فرما رہے ہیں، حقیقت کا حکم ضمناً بیان کیا اور مجاز کا حکم صراحۂ بیان کر رہے ہیں، کیونکہ حقیقت کے حکم میں کسی کا اختلاف نہیں ہے اور مجاز کے حکم میں اختلاف ہے، تو حقیقت کا حکم یہ ہے کہ وہ لفظ جو معنی موضوع لہ میں مستعمل ہے، اس کے تمام افراد مراد لیں گے جن کو وہ لفظ شامل ہے، چاہے لفظ عام ہو یا خاص ہو، اور اس میں کسی کا اختلاف نہیں ہے۔ اور مجاز کا حکم یہ ہے کہ لفظ جو کسی علاقہ کی وجہ سے معنی غیر موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہو رہا ہے تو اس سے وہ تمام افراد مراد لیں گے جن کو معنی غیر موضوع لہ شامل ہے ، وہ مجازی لفظ خاص ہو یا عام یعنی اگر لفظ خاص ہے تو مجاز بھی خاص ہوگا اور اگر لفظ عام ہے تو مجاز بھی عام ہو گا ، خاص کی مثال جیسے لفظ ”اسد یہ لفظ خاص ہے اور اس کا غیر موضوع لہ معنی "رجل شجاع" بھی خاص ہے۔ عام کی مثال جیسے عبداللہ ابن عمر کی حدیث لا تبيعو الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين - یہاں صاع کا حقیقی معنی لکڑی کا وہ برتن، وہ پیمانہ جس میں سامان بھرا جاتا ہے، مگر حقیقی معنی یہاں بالاتفاق مراد نہیں ہے، لہذا مجازی معنی متعین ہو گئے ، اور مجازی معنی ہے وہ سامان جو صاع میں رہتا ہے، ما يحل فی الصاع ، تو محل ( لکڑی کا پیمانہ بولکر حال ( ما يحل فى الصاع) مراد لیا ، اب مطلب یہ ہوا کہ لکڑی کے ایک صاع میں جو سامان سماتا ہے اس کو دو صاع بھر کے سامان کے بدلے میں مت بیچو، جب کہ وہ دونوں سامان ایک جنس کے ہوں لہذا صاع کے مجازی معنی مراد لیے ، اور مجازی معنی عام ہے کیونکہ صاع پر ال استغراق کا ہے، تو صاع ان تمام چیزوں کو شامل ہوگا جو اس میں سماتی ہوں، چاہے وہ مطعوم ہوں یا غیر مطعوم، لہذا ہمارے نزدیک ربو مطعوم میں ہوگا اور غیر مطعوم جیسے چونا وغیرہ میں بھی ہوگا کیونکہ مجازی معنی عام ہے۔ تو خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ خاص و عام جیسے حقیقت میں ہوتا ہے، ایسے ہی مجاز میں ہوتا ہے اور دونوں جگہ اگر معنی موضوع لہ یا معنی غیر موضوع لہ میں خصوص ہے تو خاص مراد لیں گے اور اگر عموم ہے تو عام مراد لیں گے، اور خاص کے حکم میں تو امام شافعی بھی متفق ہیں، البتہ مجاز میں وہ عموم کا انکار کرتے ہیں ، یعنی وہ مجاز کو خاص مانتے ہیں عام نہیں مانتے ، اسی لئے حدیث میں انہوں نے صاع سے عموم مراد نہیں لیا بلکہ صرف مطعوم مراد لیا، لہذا ان کے نزدیک ربو صرف مطعوم میں ہوگا، غیر مطعوم ایک صاع بھر کے دو صاع کے بدلے اگر کوئی بیچتا ہے تو ربو نہ ہو گا، بہر حال امام شافعی مجاز میں عموم کے قائل نہیں ہیں۔ امام شافعی اس پر دلیل یہ دیتے ہیں کہ مجاز ضرورۃ ثابت ہوتا ہے، اصل تو کلام میں حقیقت ہے یعنی لفظ کا موضوع لہ معنی مراد لینا، لیکن اگر کبھی حقیقی معنی مراد لینا مشکل ہو رہا ہو تو کلام کو مجاز کی طرف پھیر دیا جاتا ہے، تا کہ لوگوں کے لئے آسانی پیدا ہو جائے ، پس جب مجاز ضرورۃ ثابت ہوا تو یہ اصول مسلم ہے کہ وہ بقدر ضرورت ہی رہے گا ، اور حدیث میں مطعوم مراد لینے سے یعنی خصوص مراد لینے سے ضرورت پوری ہو جاتی ہے، لہذا خواہ مخواہ اس میں عموم روا نہیں رکھا جائے گا۔ مصنف اس کا جواب دیتے ہیں کہ حضرت امام شافعی کا مجاز کو ضرورۃ ثابت ماننا باطل ہے، غلط ہے، کیونکہ قرآن کریم میں مجاز کا کثرت سے استعمال ہے اور اللہ تعالی عجز اور ضرورت سے بالاتر ہیں، پس حضرت امام کا یہ قول باطل ہو گیا۔ قرآن میں مجاز کی مثال جیسے إِنَّا لَمَّا طَغَى الْمَاء - یہاں طغی کے حقیقی معنی ظلم اور گناہوں میں حد سے بڑھنا ہے ، مگر یہ معنی مراد نہیں ہے، بلکہ مجازی معنی مراد ہے یعنی پانی کا بلند ہونا اور حد سے بڑھنا، آیت طوفان نوح سے متعلق ہے اسی طرح فَوَجَدَا فِيهَا جِدَارًا يُرِيدُ أَنْ يَنْقَضَّ - یہاں یرید کے حقیقی معنی چاہنا اور ارادہ کرنا، مگر یہ معنی مراد نہیں ہے، اس لئے کہ اس کی نسبت دیوار کی طرف ہو رہی ہے ، یہاں مجازی معنی یعنی مشارفت اور قریب ہونے کے معنی مراد ہے، یعنی دیوار گرنے کے قریب ہوگئی۔ وَمِنْ حُكْم الْحَقِيقَةِ وَالْمَجَازِ اسْتِحَالَةُ اجْتِمَاعِهِمَا مُرَادَيْنِ بِلَفْظِ وَاحِدٍ كَمَا اسْتَحَالَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الثَّوْبُ الْوَاحِدُ عَلَى اللَّابِسِ مِلْكًا وَعَارِيَةً فِي زَمَانٍ وَاحِدٍ
And [the prohibition] 'nor one sa' for two sa'' is general regarding what is contained in it and what is adjacent to it. Al-Shafi'i denied this and said: There is no generality for majaz, because it is established by necessity, resorted to for broadening speech. And this is false, because majaz exists in the Book of Allah, and Allah Almighty is exalted above incapacity and necessities. Translation: And the ruling of majaz is that it applies to the meaning intended from it, whether the majaz is specific or general, just as this is the ruling of haqiqah. And for this reason we made the word sa' in the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor one sa' for two sa'' general for all things that are contained in it and that are adjacent to it. Imam al-Shafi'i denied this and said: There is no generality for majaz, because majaz is established by necessity: it is resorted to in order to create breadth in speech. And this is false, because majaz exists in the Book of Allah, and Allah Almighty is exalted above incapacity and necessities. Sharh: The author is stating the ruling of haqiqah and majaz; he mentioned the ruling of haqiqah implicitly and the ruling of majaz explicitly, because there is no disagreement on the ruling of haqiqah while there is disagreement on the ruling of majaz. So the ruling of haqiqah is that a word used in its original meaning will be taken to include all individuals that the word covers, whether the word is general or specific, and there is no disagreement on this. And the ruling of majaz is that a word, due to some relation, used in a non-original meaning will be taken to include all individuals that the non-original meaning covers, whether the metaphorical word is specific or general: if the word is specific, the majaz is also specific; if the word is general, the majaz is also general. An example of the specific: the word 'asad' (lion) is specific, and its non-original meaning 'brave man' is also specific. An example of the general: the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor one sa' for two sa''. Here the real meaning of sa' is the wooden vessel, the measure in which goods are filled, but the real meaning is not intended by consensus, so the metaphorical meaning is determined. And the metaphorical meaning is the goods that reside in the sa': 'what is contained in the sa''. So the container (wooden measure) is mentioned but the contained (what is contained in the sa') is intended. Now the meaning is: do not sell what one sa' contains of goods in exchange for the goods of two sa', when both goods are of the same type. Hence the metaphorical meaning of sa' is intended, and the metaphorical meaning is general because the al in sa' is for istighraq (totality). So sa' includes all things that are contained in it, whether they are food items or non-food items. Therefore, according to us, riba applies to food items and also to non-food items like lime etc., because the metaphorical meaning is general. So the summary is: specificity and generality occur in majaz just as they occur in haqiqah. In both cases, if the original or non-original meaning is specific, the specific is intended; if it is general, the general is intended. Imam al-Shafi'i agrees on the ruling of specific cases, but he denies generality in majaz: he considers majaz to be specific, not general. Therefore, in the hadith he did not intend generality from sa' but only intended food items. Hence according to him, riba applies only to food items; if someone sells a non-food item such as one sa' for two sa', there is no riba. In any case, Imam al-Shafi'i does not affirm generality in majaz. Imam al-Shafi'i presents as his evidence that majaz is established by necessity; the original in speech is haqiqah, i.e., intending the original meaning of the word. But if sometimes intending the real meaning becomes difficult, the speech is turned to majaz so that ease is created for people. So when majaz is established by necessity, it is an accepted principle that it will remain to the extent of necessity. And in the hadith, intending food items, i.e., intending specificity, fulfills the necessity, so generality is not allowed to be maintained unnecessarily. The author replies that Imam al-Shafi'i's considering majaz as established by necessity is false, because in the Qur'an majaz is used abundantly, and Allah is exalted above incapacity and necessity. So Imam al-Shafi'i's statement becomes false. Examples of majaz in the Qur'an: 'Indeed when the water overflowed' (11:25) – here the real meaning of tagha is transgression in oppression and sins, but that meaning is not intended; rather the metaphorical meaning is intended, i.e., the rising and exceeding of bounds of water. The verse pertains to the flood of Noah. Similarly, 'And they found in it a wall intending to collapse' (18:77) – here the real meaning of yuridu is wanting and intending, but that meaning is not intended because it is attributed to a wall. Here the metaphorical meaning of approaching and being near is intended, i.e., the wall was about to collapse. And from the ruling of haqiqah and majaz is the impossibility of both being intended simultaneously by a single word, just as it is impossible for a single garment on a wearer to be both owned and borrowed at the same time.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the ruling of generality in majaz. The author first establishes that haqiqah (literal usage) applies to all individuals the word covers, whether general or specific, with no disagreement. Then he states that the same ruling applies to majaz (metaphorical usage): if the metaphorical word is general in its non-original meaning, it conveys generality; if specific, it conveys specificity. The Hanafi school supports this by analyzing the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar prohibiting the sale of one sa' for two sa' of the same commodity. The word 'sa'' (a measure) is used metaphorically to mean the commodity measured. Since 'al' in 'al-sa'' indicates istighraq (totality), the metaphor is general, covering all commodities – food and non-food. Therefore, according to Hanafis, riba applies to both food and non-food items. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that majaz arises only out of necessity and therefore is limited to the extent of that necessity. In the hadith, necessity is satisfied by taking sa' to mean food items only, so generality is not required. The Hanafi refutation points out that majaz exists abundantly in the Qur'an, where Allah is free from any necessity, so the premise that majaz is only due to necessity is false. Quranic examples are given: 'when the water overflowed' (tagha) and 'a wall intending to collapse' (yuridu). Both use majaz without necessity. Finally, the passage asserts that haqiqah and majaz cannot both be intended simultaneously by a single word, analogous to impossibility of a garment being both owned and borrowed at once.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): A word used in its originally assigned meaning (al-ma'na al-mawdu' lahu). Its ruling is that it covers all individuals that the word includes, regardless of generality or specificity.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original meaning due to a relation (al-alaqah). Its ruling is that it covers all individuals that the non-original meaning includes, parallel to haqiqah in terms of generality/specificity.
عموم (umum): A word encompasses all individuals of its meaning without restriction. Here, the dispute is whether this applies to majaz as it does to haqiqah.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that compels resorting to majaz. Shafi'is claim majaz is only employed when necessary; Hanafis reject this as it is found in the Qur'an without necessity.
استغراق (istighraq): The definite article 'al' indicating totality (all individuals of the meaning). Used in the hadith 'al-sa'' to show generality of the metaphor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school presented in this passage, what is the ruling of generality in majaz?
A) Majaz cannot be general; it is always specific.
B) Majaz follows the same ruling as haqiqah: it can be general or specific depending on the wording.
C) Majaz is general only if the original meaning is general.
D) Majaz is general only for food items.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, majaz shares the ruling of haqiqah regarding generality and specificity; the Shafi'i restriction based on necessity is refuted by the presence of majaz in the Qur'an.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Hanafi Position on General Meaning in Figurative Language
Analyzing the Shafi'i objection to 'amm in majaz and the Hanafi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that figurative language (majaz) can carry general ('amm) meaning, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection based on necessity.

Core Idea
Majaz can be 'amm — the definite article for istighraq applies even figuratively, as the Quran uses majaz abundantly without necessity.

This passage delves into a key usul al-fiqh dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is: can figurative language (majaz) bear a general meaning? The Hanafi school affirms this, using the example of the word 'sa''' in a hadith on riba. Imam al-Shafi'i restricts majaz to specific meanings due to its origin in necessity. Let's examine the proofs and their practical impact on fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on 'amm in majaz
Hanafis hold that the definite article 'al' in 'al-sa''' indicates istighraq (comprehensiveness), so even in figurative usage, the term includes all items measured, edible or non-edible.
The Hanafi view is that 'amm in haqiqah and majaz follow the same rules.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that majaz is only used out of necessity, so it should be limited to the minimal meaning required to fulfill that necessity. In the hadith, taking 'sa''' to mean only edible items suffices.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author refutes al-Shafi'i by pointing to the Quran's abundant use of majaz (e.g., 11:41, 18:77), showing that Allah, who is above any need, uses majaz broadly. Therefore, the premise that majaz is only due to necessity is false.
4
Examine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute affects riba: Hanafis apply riba to both edible and non-edible items measured by sa'; Shafi'is restrict riba to edible items only.
This shows how usul principles directly impact fiqh rulings.
5
Reflect on the tarjih
The text favors the Hanafi position, declaring al-Shafi'i's argument as false (bāṭil) based on Quranic evidence.
The mufta bihi position is the Hanafi view.

الفيضُ الحجازي شرح اردو المنتخب الحسامي لیے ، اور مجازی معنی عام ہے کیونکہ صاع پر ال استغراق کا ہے، تو صاع ان تمام چیزوں کو شامل ہوگا جو اس میں سماتی ہوں، چاہے وہ مطعوم ہوں یا غیر مطعوم، الہذا ہمارے نزدیک ربو مطعوم میں ہوگا اور غیر مطعوم جیسے چونا وغیرہ میں بھی ہوگا کیونکہ مجازی معنی عام ہے۔ تو خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ خاص و عام جیسے حقیقت میں ہوتا ہے، ایسے ہی مجاز میں ہوتا ہے اور دونوں جگہ اگر معنی موضوع لہ یا معنی غیر موضوع لہ میں خصوص ہے تو خاص مراد لیں گے اور اگر عموم ہے تو عام مراد لیں گے، اور خاص کے حکم میں تو امام شافعی بھی متفق ہیں، البتہ مجاز میں وہ عموم کا انکار کرتے ہیں ، یعنی وہ مجاز کو خاص مانتے ہیں عام نہیں مانتے ، اسی لئے حدیث میں انہوں نے صاع سے عموم مراد نہیں لیا بلکہ صرف مطعوم مراد لیا، لہذا ان کے نزدیک ربو صرف مطعوم میں ہوگا، غیر مطعوم ایک صاع بھر کے دو صاع کے بدلے اگر کوئی بیچتا ہے تو ریو نہ ہو گا، بہر حال امام شافتی مجاز میں عموم کے قائل نہیں ہیں۔ امام شافعی اس پر دلیل یہ دیتے ہیں کہ مجاز ضرورۃ ثابت ہوتا ہے، اصل تو کلام میں حقیقت ہے یعنی لفظ کا موضوع لہ معنی مراد لینا، لیکن اگر کبھی حقیقی معنی مراد لینا مشکل ہو رہا ہو تو کلام کو مجاز کی طرف پھیر دیا جاتا ہے، تا کہ لوگوں کے لئے آسانی پیدا ہو جائے ، پس جب مجاز ضرورۃ ثابت ہوا تو یہ اصول مسلم ہے کہ وہ بقدر ضرورت ہی رہے گا ، اور حدیث میں مطعوم مراد لینے سے یعنی خصوص مراد لینے سے ضرورت پوری ہو جاتی ہے، لہذا خواہ مخواہ اس میں عموم روا نہیں رکھا جائے گا۔ مصنف اس کا جواب دیتے ہیں کہ حضرت امام شافعی کا مجاز کو ضرورۃ ثابت مان باطل ہے، غلط ہے، کیونکہ قرآن کریم میں مجاز کا کثرت سے استعمال ہے اور اللہ تعالی عجز اور ضرورت سے بالاتر ہیں، پس حضرت امام کا یہ قول باطل ہو گیا۔ قرآن میں مجاز کی مثال جیسے اِنَّا لَمَّا طَغَى الْمَاء - یہاں طفی کے حقیقی معنی ظلم اور گناہوں میں حد سے بڑھنا ہے ، مگر یہ معنی مراد نہیں ہے، بلکہ مجازی معنی مراد ہے یعنی پانی کا بلند ہونا اور حد سے بڑھنا، آیت طوفان نوح سے متعلق ہے اسی طرح فَوَجَدَا فِيهَا جِدَارًا يُرِيدُ أَنْ يَنْقَضَ - یہاں یرید کے حقیقی معنی چاہنا اور ارادہ کرنا، مگر یہ معنی مراد نہیں ہے، اس لئے کہ اس کی نسبت دیوار کی طرف ہو رہی ہے ، یہاں مجازی معنی یعنی مشارفت اور قریب ہونے کے معنی مراد ہے، یعنی دیوار گرنے کے قریب ہوگئی۔
This is the commentary. Figurative meaning is general because 'al' on 'sa''' indicates istighraq (comprehensiveness). So 'sa''' will include all things that fit in it, whether edible or non-edible. Therefore, according to us, riba will apply to edible items and also to non-edible items like lime etc. because the figurative meaning is general. So the conclusion is that specific and general are in haqiqah, similarly in majaz. In both cases, if the meaning (what the word is coined for or not coined for) is specific, we take the specific; if it is general, we take the general. Imam al-Shafi'i agrees with us in the case of haqiqah, but in majaz he denies generality, i.e., he considers majaz to be specific, not general. For this reason, in the hadith he did not take generality from 'sa''', but rather took only edible items. So in his view, riba will only apply to edible items; if someone sells two measures of non-edible items for one measure, there is no riba. In any case, al-Shafi'i does not accept generality in majaz. Al-Shafi'i gives as evidence that majaz is established out of necessity. The original in speech is haqiqah, i.e., taking the meaning for which the word was coined. But if ever taking the literal meaning is difficult, the speech is turned to majaz to ease things for people. Since majaz is established out of necessity, the accepted principle is that it will only remain to the extent of necessity. In the hadith, taking the edible meaning (i.e., taking the specific) fulfills the necessity. Therefore, unnecessarily, generality should not be maintained in it. The author replies that Imam al-Shafi'i's view that majaz is established out of necessity is false, wrong, because in the Quran majaz is used abundantly, and Allah is above weakness and necessity. So his statement is false. Examples of majaz in the Quran: 'Indeed, when the water overflowed' (11:41) – here 'tagha' literally means oppression and exceeding limits in sins, but that meaning is not intended; rather the figurative meaning is intended: water rising and exceeding its limits, related to Noah's flood. Similarly, 'And they found therein a wall that wanted to collapse' (18:77) – here 'yurid' literally means to desire and intend, but that meaning is not intended because it is attributed to the wall. Here the figurative meaning of being on the verge and nearness is intended, i.e., the wall was about to fall.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on whether figurative language (majaz) can carry a general ('amm) meaning. The Hanafi position is illustrated with the example of the word 'sa''' (a measure) in a hadith concerning riba. Hanafis argue that because the definite article 'al' in 'al-sa''' indicates istighraq (comprehensiveness), even in figurative usage the term includes all things measured, edible or not. Thus, riba applies to both edible and non-edible items. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees: he holds that majaz is only used out of necessity, so it should be restricted to the minimal meaning needed. In the hadith, the necessity is fulfilled by taking 'sa''' to mean only edible items, so the figurative meaning is specific, not general. The author of this commentary refutes al-Shafi'i by pointing to the Quran, which uses majaz extensively, and Allah is free from any need or incapacity. Therefore, majaz is not confined to necessity. Quranic examples are given: in verse 11:41, 'tagha' (overflowed) is used figuratively for the water rising, and in verse 18:77, 'yurid' (wants) is used figuratively for the wall being about to collapse. These prove that majaz can be general. Thus, the Hanafi position that majaz can carry generality is upheld.

Key Terms
عام ('amm): General meaning that includes all individuals or instances falling under a category.
خاص (khass): Specific meaning that refers to a particular individual or subset.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative language where a word is used for a meaning other than its original coinage.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal language where a word is used for its original meaning.
استغراق (istighraq): Comprehensiveness, where the definite article 'al' indicates that the term includes all instances.
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarah al-khilaf): The practical consequence or ruling that results from a scholarly difference.
ربا (riba): Usury or interest, prohibited in Islamic law; includes certain exchanges of commodities.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Shafi'i, why should majaz be restricted to khass (particular meaning)?
A) Because majaz is only used out of necessity.
B) Because the Quran uses majaz broadly.
C) Because the Hanafis say so.
D) Because it is a consensus.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that figurative language can carry general meaning, supported by Quranic usage, leading to a broader application of riba to all measured goods.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Combining Haqiqah and Majaz: Two Objections Answered
Raises two objections against the prohibition of combining haqiqah and majaz, then answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on combining haqiqah and majaz in oaths and vows, and apply the concept of general metaphor to justify the combination.

Core Idea
When usage makes a phrase a general metaphor, applying it fully does not 'combine' haqiqah and majaz—it follows the metaphor.

In this passage, the author addresses two objections that appear to contradict the principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined. The first involves an oath about placing a foot in a house; the second involves a statement that serves as both a vow and an oath. Both objections are resolved by showing that the phrases have become general metaphors or that the context demands simultaneous application. The key is understanding that what seems like a combination is actually the application of a unified metaphorical meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle being challenged
The principle is that haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative) cannot be combined in interpreting a single utterance. Note the origin of this principle.
2
Understand the first objection
The oath 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' can be interpreted literally (barefoot entry, owned house) or figuratively (riding, borrowed house). The Hanafi ruling includes all forms of entry and all types of possession. This appears to combine haqiqah and majaz, which is prohibited.
3
Analyze the Hanafi answer
The phrase 'placing the foot' has become a common metaphor for entering. The idafa 'house' denotes dwelling. Thus, the general metaphor applies uniformly. This is not combining two separate meanings but applying one general meaning.
4
Examine the Qiyas analogy
The author compares to manumission conditional on 'the day' so-and-so arrives. 'Day' with an action means absolute time, including night and day. Similarly, the metaphor for entry includes all modes and dwellings.
5
Understand the second objection and its resolution
The statement 'For Allah I must fast Rajab' can be a vow (literal) or an oath (figurative). Hanafis treat it as both. The answer relies on the speaker's intention and the nature of the utterance as a single act carrying dual obligations. (Note: the full answer is beyond this excerpt but intention is key.)

تشریح:- سابق جواب پر پھر اعتراض ہوا کہ جس طرح آپ نے ابناء اور موالی میں صورت اور شبہ کا اعتبار کر کے ابناء الابناء اور موالی الموالی کو شامل کر لیا، تو اسی طرح اگر کوئی اپنے آباء اور امہات پر امان طلب کرے ، تو اس میں بھی اور ا اجداد اور جدات کو داخل کر لینا چاہیے، کیونکہ وہی شبہ یہاں بھی ہے ، لہذا یہاں بھی صورت اور شبہ کا اعتبار کرنا چاہیے حالانکہ آپ کہتے ہیں کہ جب آباء اور امہات پر کوئی امان طلب کرے، تو صرف باپ اور ماں کو امان ملے گا دادا اور دادی اس میں داخل نہ ہوں گے کیوں؟ مصنف اس کا جواب دے رہے ہیں، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ صورت اور شبہ کا اعتبار کر کے کسی محل میں جو حکم لگتا ہے وہ تبعیت کے طریقہ پر لگتا ہے، یعنی اس کو تابع بنا کر حکم لگایا جاتا ہے، اور تابع بنانا فروع میں تو ہوسکتا ہے اصول میں نہیں ہو سکتا ہے، لہذا پہلے مسئلہ میں ابناء اور موالی میں پوتوں کو اور موالی کے موالی کو شامل کر لیا کیونکہ پوتے اور موالی الموالی ابناء اور موالی کے تابع ہیں، لفظ کے اطلاق میں بھی اور حقیقت میں بھی۔ اور دوسرے مسئلہ میں اجداد اور جدات کو تابع بنا کر آباء اور امہات میں داخل نہیں کر سکتے ، اس لئے کہ دادا اور دادی تو اصول ہیں، آباء و امہات کے تابع نہیں ہیں۔ خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ دونوں مسئلوں میں فرق ہے، پہلے مسئلہ میں تبعیت کے طریقہ پر شمول ہو سکتا ہے دوسرے میں نہیں۔
فَإِنْ قِيْلَ قَدْ قَالُوْا فِيْمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَضَعُ قَدَمَهُ فِي دَارِفُلَانِ إِنَّهُ يَقَعُ عَلَى الْمِلْكِ وَالْعَارِيَةِ وَالْإِجَارَةِ جَمِيعًا وَيَحْنَثُ إِذَا دَخَلَهَا رَاكِبًا أَوْمَاشِيًا وَكَذَلِكَ قَالَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ فِيمَنْ قَالَ لِلَّهِ عَلَى أَنْ أَصُومَ رَجَبًا وَنَوَى بِهِ الْيَمِيْنَ كَانَ نَذْرًا وَيَمِينًا وَفِيهِ جَمْع بَيْنَ الْحَقِيقَةِ وَالْمَجَاز ترجمہ :۔ پس اگر اعتراض کیا جائے کہ فقہاء نے کہا ہے اس شخص کے بارے میں جس نے قسم کھائی کہ وہ اپنا قدم فلاں کے گھر میں نہیں رکھے گا تو یہ قسم ملکیت، اجارہ اور عاریت سب پر واقع ہوگی اور حانث ہو جائے گا جب وہ گھر میں داخل ہوگا سوار یا پیدل، اور ایسے ہی امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد نے کہا اس شخص کے بارے میں جس نے کہا کہ اللہ کے لئے میرے ذمہ ہے کہ میں رجب کا روزہ رکھوں اور نیت کی اس سے یمین کی تو یہ نذر اور یمین ہوگی اور اس میں حقیقت اور مجاز کے درمیان جمع کرنا ہے۔ تشریح:۔ مصنف سابق میں بیان کردہ ضابطہ پر کہ حقیقت و مجاز کو جمع کرناممنوع ہے دو اعتراض اور ان کے

جواب نقل کر رہے ہیں ۔
پہلا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی آدمی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں فلاں کے گھر میں قدم نہیں رکھونگا تو قدم رکھنے کے حقیقی معنی ہیں ننگے پیر داخل ہونا ، پیدل داخل ہونا، اور مجازی معنی ہیں جوتے پہن کر داخل ہونا سوار ہو کر داخل ہونا۔ اور گھر میں داخل ہونے کی قسم کھائی تو گھر کے حقیقی معنی ہیں اس کی ملکیت کا گھر ، اور مجازی معنی ہیں عاریت کا یا کرایہ کا گھر تو حقیقی اور مجازی دونوں میں سے ایک معنی مراد ہونا چاہیے ، حالانکہ آپ کہتے ہیں کہ وہ ننگے پیر داخل ہو، پیدل داخل ہو یا جوتے پہن کر سوار ہو کر داخل ہو ہر صورت میں حانث ہو جائے گا، یہ حقیقت و مجاز کو جمع کرنا ہوا۔ ایسے ہی وہ گھر چاہے اس کی ملکیت کا ہو یا عاریت کا ، کرایہ کا ہو چاہے جو بھی ہو ، جب بھی وہ گھر میں داخل ہو جائے گا تو ہر صورت میں حانث ہو جائے گا تو یہ بھی حقیقت و مجاز کو آپ نے جمع کر دیا۔ دوسرا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ جب کسی نے کہا اللہ علی ان اصوم رجب اللہ کے لیے میرے ذمہ ہے کہ میں رجب کا روزہ رکھوں ، تو اس کلام کے حقیقی معنی یہ ہیں کہ نذر ہو، کیونکہ اس کے نذر منعقد ہونے کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت نہیں پڑتی ہے، معلوم ہوا کہ نذر اس کے حقیقی معنی ہیں، اور مجازی معنی یمین کے ہے، یمین مجازی معنی اس لئے ہے کہ اس میں یمین مراد لینے کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے اور نیت کی احتیاج یہ مجاز کی علامت ہے۔ بہر حال حقیقی معنی نذر ہے اور مجازی معنی یمین ہے، تو دونوں میں سے کوئی ایک معنی مراد ہونا چاہیے، حالانکہ امام ابو حنیفہ ومحمد کے نزدیک یہ کلام نذر اور یمین دونوں ہوتا ہے، تو یہ حقیقت و مجاز کا جمع کرنا ہوا ، آگے مصنف دونوں اعتراض کے جواب نقل کر رہے ہیں ۔ ندر اور یمین میں فرق: - نذر کو پورا کرنا ضروری ہے اگر پوری نہ ہوسکی تو اس کی قضا ضروری ہے، اس میں کفارہ واجب نہیں ہے، اور یمین میں حکم یہ ہے کہ اس کو پورا کیا جائے اگر نہ کر سکا تو قضا نہیں بلکہ کفارہ واجب ہے۔
قُلْنَا وَضْعُ الْقَدَم صَارَ مَجَازًا عَنِ الدُّخُولِ وَإِضَافَةُ الدَّارِ يُرَادُ بِهَا نِسْبَةُ السُّكْنى فَاعْتُبِرَ عُمُومُ الْمَجَازِ وَهُوَ نَظِيرُ مَا لَوْ قَالَ عَبْدُهُ حُرِّ يَوْمَ يَقْدَمُ فَلَانْ فَقَدِمَ لَيْلًا اَوْنَهَارًا عَتَقَ لِاَنَّ الْيَوْمَ مَتى قَرَنَ بِفِعْلٍ لَا يَمْتَذُ حُمِلَ عَلَى مُطْلَقِ الْوَقْتِ ثُمَّ الْوَقْتُ يَدْخُلُ فِيهِ اللَّيْلُ وَالنَّهَارُ
ترجمہ :۔ ہم کہیں گے کہ وضع قدم ( قدم رکھنا ) دخول سے مجاز ہو گیا اور دار کی اضافت اس سے مراد ہے سکنی کی نسبت تو عموم مجاز کا اعتبار کیا گیا، اور یہ اس کی نظیر ہے کہ اگر کسی نے کہا کہ اس کا غلام آزاد ہے جس دن فلاں آئے ا
Explanation: Then an objection was raised on the previous answer that just as you included grandchildren and clients' clients in 'abna' and 'mawali' by consideration of form and doubt, similarly if someone seeks aman for his fathers and mothers, then grandfathers and grandmothers should also be included therein, because the same doubt exists here. Therefore consideration of form and doubt should also be applied here, whereas you say that when someone seeks aman for fathers and mothers, only the father and mother receive aman; grandfather and grandmother are not included. Why? The author is answering this, the gist of which is that the ruling applied in any case by consideration of form and doubt is applied on the basis of tab'iyyah (subordination), i.e., it is made subordinate. Making something subordinate is possible in furu' (branches) but not in usul (roots). Therefore in the first issue, grandchildren in 'abna' and clients of clients in 'mawali' were included because grandchildren and clients of clients are subordinate to 'abna' and 'mawali' both in linguistic application and in reality. And in the second issue, grandfathers and grandmothers cannot be made subordinate and included in 'aba' and 'ummahat' because grandfather and grandmother are usul (roots), not subordinate to fathers and mothers. The summary is that there is a difference between the two issues: in the first issue inclusion is possible on the basis of tab'iyyah, in the second not.
If it is said: They have said concerning one who swore, 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house,' that it applies to ownership, borrowing, and leasing all together, and he violates the oath if he enters it riding or walking. Similarly, Abu Hanifah and Muhammad said concerning one who said, 'For Allah I must fast Rajab,' intending thereby an oath, it is both a nadhr (vow) and a yamin (oath), and in that there is combination of haqiqah and majaz. 
Translation: So if it is objected that the jurists have said regarding a person who swore that he will not place his foot in so-and-so's house, then this oath applies to ownership, leasing, and borrowing all together, and he becomes a violator when he enters the house riding or walking. And similarly, Imam Abu Hanifah and Imam Muhammad said regarding a person who said, 'For Allah, upon me is that I fast Rajab,' and he intended by it an oath, then it is a nadhr and a yamin, and in that there is combination of haqiqah and majaz.
Explanation: The author is transmitting two objections and their answers to the previously mentioned principle that combining haqiqah and majaz is prohibited.
The first objection is that if a person swore, 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house,' then the literal meaning of placing the foot is entering barefoot, walking; and the figurative meaning is entering wearing shoes, riding. And he swore about entering the house; the literal meaning of the house is his owned house, and the figurative meaning is a borrowed or rented house. So one of the two meanings, literal or figurative, should be intended, whereas you say that whether he enters barefoot, walking, or wearing shoes, riding, in every case he violates the oath. That is combining haqiqah and majaz. Similarly, whether the house is his ownership, borrowed, or rented, whatever it is, whenever he enters the house, in every case he violates the oath. So this too is combining haqiqah and majaz. The second objection is that when someone says 'For Allah, upon me is that I fast Rajab,' the literal meaning of this speech is that it is a nadhr, because for it to be a valid nadhr, intention (niyyah) is not needed; thus nadhr is its literal meaning. The figurative meaning is yamin, and it is figurative because to intend yamin from it, intention is needed, and the need for intention is a sign of majaz. In any case, the literal meaning is nadhr and the figurative meaning is yamin; so one of the two meanings should be intended, whereas according to Imam Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, this speech is both a nadhr and a yamin. That is combining haqiqah and majaz. Next, the author is transmitting the answers to both objections.
Difference between nadhr and yamin: In nadhr, it is necessary to fulfill it; if it cannot be fulfilled, its qada is necessary; there is no expiation. In yamin, the ruling is that it is to be fulfilled; if he cannot fulfill it, there is no qada but expiation is wajib.
We say: The phrase 'placing the foot' has become metaphorical for entering, and the idafa (construct) of 'dar' (house) is intended to denote the relation of dwelling. So the general application of the majaz is considered. This is analogous to if someone said, 'His slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives,' and he arrives at night or day, the slave is freed because when 'day' is connected to an action that does not have a specific time, it is taken to mean absolute time; and time includes night and day.
Translation: We say that 'placing the foot' has become metaphorical for entering, and the idafa of 'dar' is intended to denote the relation of dwelling. So the general application of the majaz is considered. This is analogous to if someone said, 'His slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives,' and he arrives at night or day, the slave is freed because when 'day' is connected to an action that does not have a specific time, it is taken to mean absolute time; and time includes night and day.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the principle that combining haqiqah and majaz is prohibited. It presents two objections and their answers. The first objection involves an oath about placing a foot in a house. The literal meanings (barefoot, owned house) and figurative meanings (riding, borrowed/rented house) are both considered in the juristic ruling, which seems to combine them. The answer explains that the phrase has become a common metaphor for entering, and the house is understood in terms of dwelling, so the general application of the metaphor is used. The second objection involves a statement that can be both a nadhr (literal) and a yamin (figurative). The answer is not fully given in this excerpt but will follow. The author uses an analogy from manumission conditional on a day to show that when a word like 'day' is connected to an action without specific time, it refers to absolute time, allowing inclusion of both day and night. Thus, the combination is justified.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal, original meaning of a word as per its linguistic origin.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning derived from the literal by a relation (e.g., metaphor).
تبعية (tab'iyyah): Subordination; inclusion of a thing under a general term due to its dependency on the primary referent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the text, why does the oath about placing a foot in a house include both owned and rented houses?
A) Because the word 'house' is ambiguous.
B) Because the phrase 'placing the foot' has become a common metaphor for entering, and the idafa of 'house' denotes dwelling, so the general metaphor applies.
C) Because the oath is invalid otherwise.
D) Because Imam Abu Hanifah and Imam Muhammad differed from Abu Yusuf.

Key Takeaway
Combining haqiqah and majaz is prohibited only when there are two distinct meanings; when a phrase becomes a general metaphor, applying it broadly does not violate the principle.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Refuting the Objection on Oaths: The Principle of 'Umum al-Majaz
Hanafi refutation of first objection using 'umum al-majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'umum al-majaz to analyze oaths involving literal and figurative meanings.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a metaphor can be so general that it includes the literal meaning as one of its instances — this is 'umum al-majaz.

The first objection claims that the Hanafi ruling on oaths — that entering a house in any manner breaks the oath — combines literal and figurative meanings, which is impossible. The Hanafi response does not combine both; rather, it uses the principle of 'umum al-majaz (general metaphorical meaning), where the metaphorical meaning is so general that it encompasses the literal as a subset. This lesson unpacks that principle through the analogy of 'yawm' in a conditional freeing of a slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing objection
State the opponent's claim: that in an oath not to 'set foot' in a house, the Hanafi ruling that any entry (barefoot, with shoes, riding) breaks the oath combines the literal and figurative meanings of both 'setting foot' and 'house', which is logically impossible.
The opponent assumes that haqiqah and majaz cannot coexist in one interpretation.
2
Locate the Hanafi response: 'Umum al-Majaz
The Hanafi response does not combine haqiqah and majaz simultaneously. Instead, it uses 'umum al-majaz: the metaphorical meaning (e.g., 'entering') is taken as a general category that includes the literal instance (barefoot entry) as one of its particulars.
The term 'umum al-majaz is a key Hanafi concept. The literal meaning is not treated as separate but as a subset of the broader metaphor.
3
Follow the analogy with 'yawm'
The text provides an analogy: if someone says 'my slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives', and the person arrives at night, the slave is freed. The word 'yawm' (day) literally means daytime, but when coupled with a non-extending action like 'arrival', it is interpreted as absolute time, including night. This is 'umum al-majaz applied to time.
The analogy shows that 'yawm' is not used in both literal and figurative senses; rather, its metaphorical sense (absolute time) includes the literal sense (daytime) as a subset.
4
Apply the principle to the oath
Similarly, 'setting foot' becomes a metaphor for 'entering' in general, and 'house' becomes a metaphor for 'dwelling place'. The metaphorical meaning of 'entering' includes literal entry (barefoot) and figurative entry (with shoes, riding). The metaphorical meaning of 'dwelling' includes owned, rented, and borrowed houses. Thus, any entry breaks the oath through 'umum al-majaz, not through combining separate meanings.
The result: the oath is broken in all cases, but the reasoning is based on general metaphor, not combination.
5
Summarise the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi fiqh, an oath not to 'set foot' in a house is interpreted broadly, protecting the oath's integrity. Opponents would restrict it to literal meanings, allowing evasive actions like entering with shoes or entering a rented house. The Hanafi ruling ensures the oath is not circumvented.
This difference impacts many cases in oaths and vows.

قُلْنَا وَضْعُ الْقَدَم صَارَ مَجَازًا عَنِ الدُّخُولِ وَإِضَافَةُ الدَّارِ يُرَادُ بِهَا نِسْبَةُ السُّكْنى فَاعْتُبِرَ عُمُومُ الْمَجَازِ وَهُوَ نَظِيرُ مَا لَوْ قَالَ عَبْدُهُ حُرِّ يَوْمَ يَقْدَمُ فَلَانْ فَقَدِمَ لَيْلًا اَوْنَهَارًا عَتَقَ لِاَنَّ الْيَوْمَ مَتى قَرَنَ بِفِعْلٍ لَا يَمْتَذُ حُمِلَ عَلَى مُطْلَقِ الْوَقْتِ ثُمَّ الْوَقْتُ يَدْخُلُ فِيهِ اللَّيْلُ وَالنَّهَارُ
ترجمہ :۔ ہم کہیں گے کہ وضع قدم ( قدم رکھنا ) دخول سے مجاز ہو گیا اور دار کی اضافت اس سے مراد ہے سکنی کی نسبت تو عموم مجاز کا اعتبار کیا گیا، اور یہ اس کی نظیر ہے کہ اگر کسی نے کہا کہ اس کا غلام آزاد ہے جس دن فلاں آئے ا
(پس) وہ فلاں آیارات میں یا دن میں تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا، اس لئے کہ یوم جب ملا ہوا ہوا ایسے فعل کے ساتھ جس میں امتداد نہ ہو تو وہ محمول کیا جاتا ہے مطلق وقت پر ، پھر وقت اس میں رات اور دن دونوں داخل ہو جاتے ہیں۔ تشریح:- مصنف " پہلے اعتراض کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ حضرت ! ہم نے حقیقت و مجاز کو جمع نہیں کیا بلکہ عموم مجاز مراد لیا ہے یعنی مجازی معنی ایسے عام مراد لئے کہ حقیقی معنی بھی اس کا ایک فرد بن کر داخل ہو گیا ، پس وضع قدم یعنی قدم رکھنے سے عموم مجاز کے طریقہ پر دخول مراد لیا اور دخول میں حقیقی و مجازی دونوں معنی آگئے ، اب چاہے ننگے پیر داخل ہو ، جوتے پہن کر، پیدل ، سوار ہو کر جس طرح بھی داخل ہو گا حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح دار یعنی گھر کی جو نسبت کی گئی تو اس سے عموم مجاز کے طریقہ پر اس کا سکنی (اس کی رہائش ) مراد ہے یعنی جس گھر میں وہ رہتا ہے، اب چاہے وہ ملکیت کا گھر ہو یا کرایہ کا یا عاریت کا ، ہر صورت میں گھر میں داخل ہونے سے حانث ہو جائے گا، اور عموم مجاز ہمارے نزدیک جائز ہے، تو خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ اس میں حقیقت و مجاز کا جمع ہونا بطریق عموم مجاز ہے نہ کہ جمع بین الحقیقہ والمجاز کے قبیل سے، لہذا اب آپ اعتراض نہیں کر سکتے ۔ اس کی نظیر سمجھو، کسی نے کہا کہ عبدى حريوم يقدم فلان جس دن فلاں آئے میرا غلام آزاد ہے، تو یہاں یوم کے حقیقی معنی دن اور مجازی معنی رات ہے، تو دونوں میں سے کوئی ایک معنی مراد ہونا چاہیے، حالانکہ یہاں بھی دونوں معنی مراد ہیں، اس لئے وہ رات میں آئے ، چاہے دن میں آئے ، بہر صورت غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔۔تو یہاں بھی وہی بات ہے کہ یوم سے بطریق عموم مجاز مطلق وقت مراد ہے، کیونکہ یوم جب فعل غیر ممتند سے ملا ہوا ہو یعنی ایسے فعل سے جسکی حد بندی نہ ہو سکے، تو اس سے مطلق وقت مراد ہوتا ہے، یہاں قدوم فعل غیر ممتد ہے کیونکہ آنے کی حد بندی نہیں ہوسکتی ، لہذا یہاں مطلق وقت مراد ہے، اور جب مطلق وقت مراد لیا تو اس میں رات اور دن دونوں داخل ہو گئے تو دن اور رات دونوں کا جمع ہونا عموم مجاز کے طریقہ پر ہوا، نہ یہ کہ حقیقت و مجاز کو جمع کیا گیا۔ وَأَمَّا مَسْئَلَةُ النَّذْرِ فَلَيْسَ بِجَمْعِ أَيْضًا بَلْ هُوَ نَذْرٌ بِصِيغَتِهِ يَمِيْنٌ بِمُوْجَبِهِ وَهُوَ الْإِيجَابُ لِاَنَّ ايْجَابَ الْمُبَاحَ يَصْلُحُ يَمِينًا كَتَحْرِيمِ الْمُبَاحَ وَهَذَا كَشِرَاءِ الْقَريْب فَإِنَّهُ تَمَلُكَ بِصِيْغَتِهِ تَحْرِيْرٌ بِمُوْجَبِهِ
۔
ترجمہ :۔ اور بہر حال نذر کا مسئلہ پس وہ بھی جمع (بین الحقیقہ والمجاز) نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ کلام نذر ہے اپنے صیغہ کے اعتبار سے اور یمین ہے اپنے حکم کے اعتبار سے اور وہ حکم ایجاب ( واجب کرنا) ہے، اس لئے کہ مباح کو واجب کرنا یمین بن سکتا ہے، جیسے مباح کو حرام کرنا اور یہ ایسا ہی ہے جیسے قریبی رشتہ دار کو خریدنا تملک (مالک بننا ) ہے اپنے صیغہ
We said: 'wad' al-qadam' (placing the foot) has become a metaphor (majaz) for entering (al-dukhul), and the idafah (construct) of 'dar' (house) is intended to mean the relationship of dwelling (sukna). So the generality of the metaphor ('umum al-majaz) is considered. And this is analogous to if someone said, 'His slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives,' and he arrives at night or daytime, the slave becomes free. Because 'yawm' (day), when coupled with an action that does not have extension (imtidad), is interpreted as absolute time, and then time includes night and day. And as for the issue of nadhr (vow), it is not a combination (of haqiqah and majaz) either. Rather, it is a nadhr by its wording (sighah) and a yamin (oath) by its ruling (hukm), which is obligation (ijab). Because making permissible obligatory can serve as a yamin, just as making permissible forbidden. And this is like purchasing a close relative: it is ownership (tamalluk) by its wording and emancipation (tahreer) by its ruling.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Hanafi position on two objections regarding the combination of literal and figurative meanings (haqiqah and majaz) in legal language. The first objection concerns oaths. The opponent argues that when someone swears not to set foot in a house, the literal meaning of 'setting foot' is entering barefoot, while the figurative includes wearing shoes or riding. The house itself has literal (owned) and figurative (borrowed/rented) meanings. The Hanafi ruling that the oath is broken in all cases seems to combine both meanings, which the opponent claims is invalid. The author responds using the principle of 'umum al-majaz: the metaphor is taken broadly so that the literal meaning becomes one of its instances. He illustrates with the example of 'yawm' in a conditional freeing of a slave. Although 'yawm' literally means daytime, when coupled with an action like arrival that has no extension, it is interpreted as absolute time, including night. The second objection concerns a person saying 'upon me to fast Rajab.' The opponent says the literal meaning is a nadhr (vow), and the figurative is a yamin (oath), and they cannot be combined. The author argues it is not a combination: it is a nadhr by wording and a yamin by ruling. Making permissible obligatory (ijab) can serve as a yamin, similar to how buying a close relative results in ownership by wording but emancipation by ruling. Thus, the Hanafi position is consistent with usul principles.

Key Terms
عموم المجاز ('umum al-majaz): The general metaphorical meaning that includes the literal meaning as one of its particular instances.
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship, which becomes obligatory and requires fulfillment; if broken, qada' is required, not kaffarah.
یمین (yamin): An oath that, if broken, requires expiation (kaffarah) but not qada'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the first objection, how is entering a house with shoes considered a breach of an oath where the literal meaning is to enter barefoot?
A) It is a combination of literal and figurative meanings simultaneously.
B) The literal meaning is considered the only valid one, so wearing shoes does not break the oath.
C) 'Umum al-majaz is used, taking the metaphorical meaning broadly to include literal as one instance.
D) The oath is invalid because of ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
The principle of 'umum al-majaz allows Hanafis to give a broad interpretation to oaths, ensuring they are not evaded by figurative actions.

intermediate

Refuting the Objection on Vows: Nadhr by Wording, Yamin by Ruling
Hanafi refutation of second objection using distinction between wording and ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between nadhr (vow) and yamin (oath) in Hanafi fiqh and explain how a single statement can be a nadhr by wording and a yamin by ruling.

Core Idea
One statement can be a valid nadhr by its wording and simultaneously a yamin by its ruling, without combining haqiqah and majaz.

The second objection concerns a person saying 'upon me to fast Rajab'. The opponent argues that this statement is literally a nadhr (vow) and figuratively a yamin (oath), and that combining them is impossible. The Hanafi response clarifies that it is not a combination but a single statement that functions as a nadhr in wording (sighah) and a yamin in ruling (hukm). This lesson explains this distinction and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
State the opponent's objection regarding the vow
The opponent claims that when someone says 'upon me to fast Rajab', the literal meaning is a nadhr (because a nadhr does not require intention to be valid) and the figurative meaning is a yamin (because an oath requires intention). The Hanafi view that it is both a nadhr and a yamin seems to combine literal and figurative meanings, which is impossible.
The objection centers on the necessity of intention: nadhr is valid without intention, yamin requires intention.
2
Identify the Hanafi response: not a combination
The Hanafi response states that this is not a combination of haqiqah and majaz. Rather, the statement is a nadhr by its wording (sighah) and a yamin by its ruling (hukm). The ruling of a yamin is obligation (ijab): making a permissible act obligatory can serve as a yamin, just as making a permissible act forbidden can be a yamin.
The key distinction is between the form (sighah) and the legal effect (hukm).
3
Follow the analogy with purchasing a close relative
The text gives an analogy: purchasing a close relative is ownership (tamalluk) by wording, but emancipation (tahreer) by ruling. Similarly, the statement 'upon me to fast' is a nadhr by wording, but a yamin by ruling because it imposes an obligation.
The analogy illustrates that a single act can have different classifications from different perspectives.
4
Distinguish nadhr from yamin in Hanafi fiqh
In a nadhr, fulfillment is obligatory; if the act is not done, qada' (making up) is required, not kaffarah. In a yamin, if the oath is broken, kaffarah (expiation) is required, not qada'. The statement 'upon me to fast Rajab' is both: if the person fasts, he fulfills the nadhr; if he does not, he must both make up the fast (qada' for nadhr) and pay kaffarah (for yamin) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This dual ruling is unique to the Hanafi school.
5
Summarise the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi fiqh, a person who says 'upon me to fast Rajab' and fails to fast must both make up the fast (qada') and pay expiation (kaffarah). Opponents would treat it only as a nadhr, requiring qada' only, or only as an oath, requiring kaffarah only.
This difference affects the legal obligations of individuals in vows and oaths.

قُلْنَا وَضْعُ الْقَدَم صَارَ مَجَازًا عَنِ الدُّخُولِ وَإِضَافَةُ الدَّارِ يُرَادُ بِهَا نِسْبَةُ السُّكْنى فَاعْتُبِرَ عُمُومُ الْمَجَازِ وَهُوَ نَظِيرُ مَا لَوْ قَالَ عَبْدُهُ حُرِّ يَوْمَ يَقْدَمُ فَلَانْ فَقَدِمَ لَيْلًا اَوْنَهَارًا عَتَقَ لِاَنَّ الْيَوْمَ مَتى قَرَنَ بِفِعْلٍ لَا يَمْتَذُ حُمِلَ عَلَى مُطْلَقِ الْوَقْتِ ثُمَّ الْوَقْتُ يَدْخُلُ فِيهِ اللَّيْلُ وَالنَّهَارُ
ترجمہ :۔ ہم کہیں گے کہ وضع قدم ( قدم رکھنا ) دخول سے مجاز ہو گیا اور دار کی اضافت اس سے مراد ہے سکنی کی نسبت تو عموم مجاز کا اعتبار کیا گیا، اور یہ اس کی نظیر ہے کہ اگر کسی نے کہا کہ اس کا غلام آزاد ہے جس دن فلاں آئے ا

۔
ترجمہ :۔ اور بہر حال نذر کا مسئلہ پس وہ بھی جمع (بین الحقیقہ والمجاز) نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ کلام نذر ہے اپنے صیغہ کے اعتبار سے اور یمین ہے اپنے حکم کے اعتبار سے اور وہ حکم ایجاب ( واجب کرنا) ہے، اس لئے کہ مباح کو واجب کرنا یمین بن سکتا ہے، جیسے مباح کو حرام کرنا اور یہ ایسا ہی ہے جیسے قریبی رشتہ دار کو خریدنا تملک (مالک بننا ) ہے اپنے صیغہ
We said: 'wad' al-qadam' (placing the foot) has become a metaphor (majaz) for entering (al-dukhul), and the idafah (construct) of 'dar' (house) is intended to mean the relationship of dwelling (sukna). So the generality of the metaphor ('umum al-majaz) is considered. And this is analogous to if someone said, 'His slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives,' and he arrives at night or daytime, the slave becomes free. Because 'yawm' (day), when coupled with an action that does not have extension (imtidad), is interpreted as absolute time, and then time includes night and day. And as for the issue of nadhr (vow), it is not a combination (of haqiqah and majaz) either. Rather, it is a nadhr by its wording (sighah) and a yamin (oath) by its ruling (hukm), which is obligation (ijab). Because making permissible obligatory can serve as a yamin, just as making permissible forbidden. And this is like purchasing a close relative: it is ownership (tamalluk) by its wording and emancipation (tahreer) by its ruling.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Hanafi position on two objections regarding the combination of literal and figurative meanings (haqiqah and majaz) in legal language. The first objection concerns oaths. The opponent argues that when someone swears not to set foot in a house, the literal meaning of 'setting foot' is entering barefoot, while the figurative includes wearing shoes or riding. The house itself has literal (owned) and figurative (borrowed/rented) meanings. The Hanafi ruling that the oath is broken in all cases seems to combine both meanings, which the opponent claims is invalid. The author responds using the principle of 'umum al-majaz: the metaphor is taken broadly so that the literal meaning becomes one of its instances. He illustrates with the example of 'yawm' in a conditional freeing of a slave. Although 'yawm' literally means daytime, when coupled with an action like arrival that has no extension, it is interpreted as absolute time, including night. The second objection concerns a person saying 'upon me to fast Rajab.' The opponent says the literal meaning is a nadhr (vow), and the figurative is a yamin (oath), and they cannot be combined. The author argues it is not a combination: it is a nadhr by wording and a yamin by ruling. Making permissible obligatory (ijab) can serve as a yamin, similar to how buying a close relative results in ownership by wording but emancipation by ruling. Thus, the Hanafi position is consistent with usul principles.

Key Terms
عموم المجاز ('umum al-majaz): The general metaphorical meaning that includes the literal meaning as one of its particular instances.
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship, which becomes obligatory and requires fulfillment; if broken, qada' is required, not kaffarah.
یمین (yamin): An oath that, if broken, requires expiation (kaffarah) but not qada'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the second objection, how can a statement be both a nadhr and a yamin?
A) It is a combination of the literal meaning (nadhr) and figurative meaning (yamin).
B) It is a nadhr by its wording and a yamin by its ruling, not a semantic combination.
C) It depends on the intention of the speaker.
D) It is only possible if the act is sinful.

Key Takeaway
A single statement can be a nadhr in form and a yamin in effect, illustrating that legal classifications can be based on different aspects of an utterance.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Umum al-Majaz: Combining Literal and Metaphorical Meanings
Responding to an objection about combining haqiqah and majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define umum al-majaz, explain how it allows combining literal and metaphorical meanings, and apply it to interpret an oath in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Umum al-majaz: the metaphorical meaning is so general that the literal becomes a subset—no forbidden combination.

A common objection in usul al-fiqh is that a single word cannot simultaneously carry its literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings. The Hanafi school responds by introducing the concept of umum al-majaz: the metaphorical meaning is taken in such a broad sense that it includes the literal meaning as one of its instances. This lesson unpacks this concept using the example of an oath involving 'day' (yawm) and explains why it is valid in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify the hypothetical objection: that interpreting 'placing a foot' (wad' al-qadam) as entry (dukhul) and 'house' (dar) as dwelling combines literal and metaphorical meanings in one word, which some schools forbid.
The objection is based on the principle that a word cannot have two simultaneous meanings.
2
Define umum al-majaz
Learn the definition: a general metaphorical usage where the metaphorical meaning is so broad that the literal meaning becomes one of its individuals. This is not a direct combination but an inclusive interpretation.
The Hanafi school permits this as a valid linguistic and legal tool.
3
Analyze the example
Examine the given example: 'My slave is free on the day when Zayd arrives' (عبدي حر يوم يقدم فلان). Here, yawm (day) is interpreted via umum al-majaz as absolute time, so arrival at night also triggers freedom.
The phrase 'yawm' is combined with a non-extended action (qudum), which forces the interpretation of absolute time.
4
Recognize the Hanafi ruling
Conclude that the Hanafi school accepts umum al-majaz as valid, allowing broader interpretations in oaths and other texts, while other schools may restrict to the literal meaning.
This illustrates a key difference in usul between Hanafi and other madhhabs.

(پس) وہ فلاں آیارات میں یا دن میں تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا، اس لئے کہ یوم جب ملا ہوا ہوا ایسے فعل کے ساتھ جس میں امتداد نہ ہو تو وہ محمول کیا جاتا ہے مطلق وقت پر ، پھر وقت اس میں رات اور دن دونوں داخل ہو جاتے ہیں۔ تشریح:- مصنف " پہلے اعتراض کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ حضرت ! ہم نے حقیقت و مجاز کو جمع نہیں کیا بلکہ عموم مجاز مراد لیا ہے یعنی مجازی معنی ایسے عام مراد لئے کہ حقیقی معنی بھی اس کا ایک فرد بن کر داخل ہو گیا ، پس وضع قدم یعنی قدم رکھنے سے عموم مجاز کے طریقہ پر دخول مراد لیا اور دخول میں حقیقی و مجازی دونوں معنی آگئے ، اب چاہے ننگے پیر داخل ہو ، جوتے پہن کر، پیدل ، سوار ہو کر جس طرح بھی داخل ہو گا حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح دار یعنی گھر کی جو نسبت کی گئی تو اس سے عموم مجاز کے طریقہ پر اس کا سکنی (اس کی رہائش ) مراد ہے یعنی جس گھر میں وہ رہتا ہے، اب چاہے وہ ملکیت کا گھر ہو یا کرایہ کا یا عاریت کا ، ہر صورت میں گھر میں داخل ہونے سے حانث ہو جائے گا، اور عموم مجاز ہمارے نزدیک جائز ہے، تو خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ اس میں حقیقت و مجاز کا جمع ہونا بطریق عموم مجاز ہے نہ کہ جمع بین الحقیقہ والمجاز کے قبیل سے، لہذا اب آپ اعتراض نہیں کر سکتے ۔ اس کی نظیر سمجھو، کسی نے کہا کہ عبدى حريوم يقدم فلان جس دن فلاں آئے میرا غلام آزاد ہے، تو یہاں یوم کے حقیقی معنی دن اور مجازی معنی رات ہے، تو دونوں میں سے کوئی ایک معنی مراد ہونا چاہیے، حالانکہ یہاں بھی دونوں معنی مراد ہیں، اس لئے وہ رات میں آئے ، چاہے دن میں آئے ، بہر صورت غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔۔تو یہاں بھی وہی بات ہے کہ یوم سے بطریق عموم مجاز مطلق وقت مراد ہے، کیونکہ یوم جب فعل غیر ممتند سے ملا ہوا ہو یعنی ایسے فعل سے جسکی حد بندی نہ ہو سکے، تو اس سے مطلق وقت مراد ہوتا ہے، یہاں قدوم فعل غیر ممتد ہے کیونکہ آنے کی حد بندی نہیں ہوسکتی ، لہذا یہاں مطلق وقت مراد ہے، اور جب مطلق وقت مراد لیا تو اس میں رات اور دن دونوں داخل ہو گئے تو دن اور رات دونوں کا جمع ہونا عموم مجاز کے طریقہ پر ہوا، نہ یہ کہ حقیقت و مجاز کو جمع کیا گیا۔
(So) if he says 'on such-and-such days' or 'on the day', the slave will be freed, because when 'yawm' is combined with an action that has no extension, it is interpreted as absolute time, then time includes both night and day. Explanation: The author is answering the first objection: O sir! We have not combined literal and metaphorical meanings, but we intended general metaphor (umum al-majaz), i.e., we intended the metaphorical meaning so broadly that the literal meaning becomes one of its individuals. So by 'placing a foot', we intended entry (dukhul) by way of umum al-majaz, and entry includes both literal and metaphorical meanings. Now whether he enters barefoot, with shoes, walking, or riding, in any way he enters, he will be sinful. Similarly, with 'house' (dar), the attribution to him is interpreted by umum al-majaz as his dwelling (sukna), i.e., the house he lives in, whether owned, rented, or borrowed. In all cases, entering the house makes him sinful. And umum al-majaz is permissible in our school. So the conclusion is that this is combining literal and metaphorical via umum al-majaz, not of the category of combining literal and metaphorical (jama bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz), so you cannot object. Understand its example: someone said, 'My slave is free on the day when so-and-so arrives (abdi hurr yawm yaqdam fulan).' Here, the literal meaning of yawm is day, and the metaphorical meaning is night. One of the two meanings should be intended, yet here both meanings are intended. Therefore, whether he comes at night or day, in any case the slave is freed. So here too, from yawm by way of umum al-majaz, absolute time is intended, because when yawm is combined with a non-extended action, i.e., an action whose limit cannot be determined, then absolute time is intended. Here 'qudum' (arrival) is a non-extended action because its limit cannot be determined. So absolute time is intended, and when absolute time is intended, both night and day enter. So the combination of night and day occurs by way of umum al-majaz, not a combining of literal and metaphorical.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a teacher's explanation responding to a hypothetical objection: that the earlier interpretation (of 'placing a foot' and 'house' in an oath) wrongly combines literal and metaphorical meanings in one word, which is supposedly forbidden. The commentator clarifies that they are not combining haqiqah and majaz directly; rather, they are using 'umum al-majaz' — general metaphorical usage. In this method, the metaphorical meaning is taken so broadly that the literal meaning becomes one of its inclusive cases. So, for example, when a person swears 'I will not place my foot in his house', the word 'foot' (placed) is interpreted metaphorically as 'entry' (dukhul) in a general sense, which includes all modes of entry (barefoot, shoed, etc.). Similarly, 'house' is interpreted as 'dwelling place', including owned, rented, or borrowed houses. Since the Hanafi school permits 'umum al-majaz', this is not a prohibited combination. The commentator then gives a parallel example: someone says 'My slave is free on the day when Zayd arrives' (عبدي حر يوم يقدم فلان). Here 'yawm' (day) literally means daytime but metaphorically can mean night. If we interpret it as absolute time via 'umum al-majaz', then arrival at any time (night or day) triggers the freeing of the slave. Thus, both literal and metaphorical meanings are included without directly combining them in a single usage. This illustrates the permissibility and mechanism of 'umum al-majaz' in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning of a word; the original meaning for which it was coined.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical meaning of a word; a secondary meaning based on a relationship to the literal meaning.
عموم مجاز (ʿumūm al-majāz): A general metaphorical usage where the metaphorical meaning is so broad that it includes the literal meaning as one of its instances.
يوم (yawm): In this context, 'yawm' literally means day (daytime) but is metaphorically used for time in general, including night.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'عبدي حر يوم يقدم فلان', if Zayd arrives at night, is the slave freed according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, because 'yawm' literally means daytime only.
B) Yes, because 'yawm' is interpreted via umum al-majaz as absolute time, including night.
C) It depends on the intention of the speaker.
D) The vow is invalid because it combines haqiqah and majaz.

Key Takeaway
Umum al-majaz allows a word to encompass both its literal and metaphorical meanings when the metaphorical is made general enough, and the Hanafi school accepts this as valid.

Preference for Haqiqah over Majaz in Oaths
Stating the rule and exceptions for haqiqah vs. majaz in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that haqiqah (literal meaning) takes precedence over majaz (metaphorical meaning) in oaths, and identify the two exceptions (impossibility and abandonment).

Core Idea
Literal meaning is mandatory in oaths unless impossible or abandoned – only then is metaphor used.

In Hanafi usul, when interpreting words in oaths and similar linguistic acts, the literal meaning (haqiqah) is the default and takes priority over metaphorical meaning (majaz). This lesson explains that rule, its rationale, and its exceptions. You will learn to identify when literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, and when to resort to metaphorical interpretation, using classic examples.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Articulate the principle: 'The literal meaning (haqiqah) is to be acted upon whenever possible, and the metaphorical (majaz) is dropped.' Memorize this foundational rule.
The rule is absolute – there is no choice between literal and metaphorical when literal is possible.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given in the text: 'because the borrowed (musta‛ār) does not compete with the original.' Majaz is a borrowed meaning; it cannot override the original literal meaning.
This rationale underscores the strength of haqiqah in Hanafi thought.
3
Identify the two exceptions
Literal meaning is set aside only when it is: (1) impossible (mut‛adhdhirah) – e.g., 'I will not eat from this palm tree' (literally impossible to eat the tree itself), or (2) abandoned (mahjūrah) – e.g., 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' (literal meaning of 'placing foot' is possible but idiomatically abandoned in favor of 'enter').
In both cases, the metaphorical meaning is used. The distinction between impossibility and abandonment affects later furū‛.
4
Apply to the examples
For each example given, determine whether the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, then state the correct interpretation. Practice classifying new oaths.
This trains the skill of tashkhīṣ (diagnosing the condition of the literal meaning).
5
Generalize the principle
Recognise that this rule applies beyond oaths to all contexts where a word has both a literal and a common metaphorical meaning, though the examples here focus on oaths.
This principle is part of the broader Hanafi approach to linguistic interpretation in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ حُكْمِ هَذَا الْبَابِ أَنَّ الْعَمَلَ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ مَتى أَمْكَنَ سَقَطَ الْمَجَازُ لِاَنَّ الْمُسْتَعَارَ لَا يَزَاحِمُ الْاَصْلَ فَإِنْ كَانَتِ الْحَقِيقَةُ مُتَعَذِّرَةً كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَاكُلُ مِنْ هَذِهِ النَّخْلَةِ أَوْ مَهْجُوْرَةً كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَضَعُ قَدَمَهُ فِي دَارِفَلَانٍ صِيْرَ إِلَى الْمَجَازِ
ترجمہ :۔ اور اس باب کا حکم یہ ہے کہ حقیقت پر عمل کرنا جب تک ممکن ہو تو مجاز ساقط ہو جائے گا ،اس لئے کہ
And from the ruling of this chapter is that acting by the literal meaning (haqiqah) whenever possible causes the metaphorical (majaz) to drop, because the borrowed (musta'ar) does not compete with the original. If the literal meaning is impossible, as when he swears 'I will not eat from this palm tree', or abandoned, as when he swears 'I will not place his foot in so-and-so's house', then resort is made to the metaphorical. Translation: And the ruling of this chapter is that acting by the literal meaning is done as long as it is possible; then the metaphorical is dropped, because the borrowed does not compete with the original.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle in the interpretation of oaths (and similar linguistic acts) in Hanafi usul: when a literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word is possible, it takes precedence, and the metaphorical meaning (majaz) is not used. The rationale is that the metaphorical meaning, being a loan (musta'ar) from the original, does not compete with the original meaning. However, there are exceptions: if the literal meaning is impossible (mut'azzirah) or abandoned (mahjurah), then one must resort to the metaphorical meaning. Two examples are given: (1) If someone swears 'I will not eat from this palm tree', the literal meaning (eating from the tree itself) is impossible, so the metaphorical meaning (eating its fruit) is intended. (2) If someone swears 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house', the literal meaning (placing the foot) is normally possible but is considered abandoned (mahjurah) in common usage because the phrase idiomatically means 'to enter'; thus, the metaphorical meaning (entering) is used. This principle helps determine the scope of oaths.

Key Terms
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning that is applicable; the original meaning of a word.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical meaning that deviates from the original.
متعذرة (mut'addhirah): Impossible; when the literal meaning cannot be applied at all.
مهجورة (mahjūrah): Abandoned; when the literal meaning is possible but not commonly used or intended in that context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an oath 'I will not eat from this palm tree', which interpretation is applied according to this passage?
A) The literal meaning (not eating from the tree itself) because it is possible.
B) The metaphorical meaning (not eating its dates) because literal is impossible.
C) Both meanings are possible; the choice is arbitrary.
D) The oath is invalid because of ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning (haqiqah) is always applied in oaths unless it is impossible or abandoned; only then is the metaphorical meaning (majaz) used.
