Haqiqah and Majaz in One Utterance: The Hanafi Defence
Refutation of the impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqah (literal meaning from the sighah) and the hukm (legal ruling) derived from an utterance, and apply the Hanafi argument that haqiqah and majaz can coexist in one utterance via different aspects.

Core Idea
Haqiqah and majaz are not combined in the same word – one is from the expression, the other from the ruling.

In Hanafi usul, an utterance can carry both a literal meaning (haqiqah) and a legal effect that is not directly expressed but derived from the ruling of the speech. The objector claims this would combine haqiqah and majaz in one word, which is impossible. The Hanafi school responds by showing that the literal meaning comes from the expression (sighah), while the metaphorical effect comes from the ruling (hukm) of the speech – they are two different aspects. This lesson unpacks that argument through the example of nathr (vow) and yamin (oath).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector argues that in the nathr formula 'lillahi alayya an asuma rajaba', the word 'nathr' cannot simultaneously mean nathr (haqiqah) and yamin (majaz) because haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined in one word.
The objector is likely a Mutakallim or grammarian.
2
Examine the Hanafi Response – Two Aspects
The author states that nathr is proven from the literal expression (sighah) of the speech – that is haqiqah. Yamin is proven not from the word's metaphorical meaning, but from the ruling (hukm) of the speech: making a permissible act wajib is the definition of a yamin. Thus haqiqah and majaz are not combined in the same word; rather, haqiqah is from the lafz, and majaz is from the hukm.
This is the core of the Hanafi position.
3
Understand the Analogical Proof (Qiyas)
The author draws an analogy: if someone buys a mahram relative, the word 'buy' (shira') literally means ownership (haqiqah), but the ruling (hukm) of owning a mahram relative is emancipation (tahrir) due to the hadith. Here, the literal meaning (ownership) and the derived effect (emancipation) come from different aspects, not from the same word. Similarly, in nathr, the literal nathr is from the sighah, and the effect (yamin) is from the hukm.
The hadith: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he is free.'
4
Apply the Principle to Other Cases
The principle is: haqiqah and majaz can be present in one utterance as long as they arise from different aspects – one from the literal expression and the other from the legal ruling or necessary implication of the speech. This is not a violation of the rule against combining haqiqah and majaz in the same word.
This principle is used in many Hanafi usul arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مصنف دوسرے اعتراض کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ حضرت! نذر والا مسئلہ سمجھے ، لله على ان اصوم رجبا، اس میں ہم نے حقیقت و مجاز کو جمع نہیں کیا ہے بلکہ اس کلام کے حقیقی معنی ہی مراد لئے ہیں، اور اس کے حقیقی معنی نذر ہیں جو اس کے صیغہ سے ثابت ہو رہے ہیں اور رہ گئی یمین تو یمین بھی اس کلام سے ثابت ہو جاتی ہے لیکن اس لئے نہیں کہ اس کے مجازی معنی ہیں بلکہ یمین کا ثبوت اس کلام کے حکم اور مقتضی سے ہو رہا ہے، اور اس کلام کا حکم ایجاب ہے یعنی کسی چیز کو واجب کرنا، چونکہ قاتل ایک مباح چیز کو اپنے اوپر واجب کر رہا ہے، کیونکہ رجب کا روزہ اس پر واجب نہیں ہے، پس مباح چیز کو واجب کرنا یہ یمین ہے جیسے مباح چیز کو حرام کرنا یمین ہے، قد فرض الله لكم تحلة ایمانکم دلیل ہے، تو یمین کا ثبوت اس کلام کے حکم سے ہوا، نہ کہ ایک ہی لفظ سے حقیقت و مجاز کا اجتماع ہو رہا ہے جو ممنوع ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ نذر تو اس کلام کے صیغہ سے ثابت ہوئی جو اس کے حقیقی معنی ہیں، اور یمین کا ثبوت اس کلام کے حکم اور مقتضی سے ہوا، اور اس میں کوئی قباحت نہیں ہے تو اس طرح نذر اور یمین دونوں جمع ہوئے ، لہذا آپ جمع بین الحقیقت والمجاز کا اعتراض نہیں کر سکتے۔ اس کی نظیر سمجھو، جیسے کسی نے اپنے قریبی رشتہ دار کو خریدا تو وہ فوراً آزاد ہو جائے گا حالانکہ شراء (خریدنے) سے ملک ثابت ہوتی ہے نہ کہ تحریر ( آزادی)۔ تو یہاں بھی وہی بات ہے کہ شراء کے صیغہ سے تو ملک کا ثبوت ہو رہا ہے اور شراء کے حکم سے تحریر ثابت ہو رہی ہے، شراء کا حکم ہے ملک مع القرابۃ یعنی قریبی رشتہ دار کا مالک بننا اور قریبی رشتہ دار کے ملک سے آزادی ثابت ہو جاتی ہے حدیث کی رو سے من ملک زارحم محرم منه عتق - تو شراء کے صیغہ سے تملک ہوا اور اس کے حکم سے تحریر ہوئی۔
The author is answering the second objection: Sir! Understand the issue of nathr: 'For Allah upon me that I fast Rajab.' In this we have not combined haqiqah and majaz, rather we intend the literal meanings of this speech, and its literal meanings are nathr, which are proven by its expression. As for yamin, it is also proven from this speech, but not because it has metaphorical meanings, rather the proof of yamin comes from the ruling and requirement of this speech. And the ruling of this speech is obligation, i.e., making something wajib. Since the speaker is making a permissible thing wajib upon himself, because fasting Rajab is not wajib upon him, making a permissible thing wajib is yamin, just as making a permissible thing haram is yamin. The proof is: 'Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths.' So yamin is proven from the ruling of the speech, not that the conjunction of haqiqah and majaz from one word is occurring, which is prohibited. The summary is that nathr is proven from the expression of this speech, which is its literal meaning, and yamin is proven from the ruling and requirement of this speech. There is no objection in this; thus nathr and yamin are both combined. Therefore you cannot object to the combination of haqiqah and majaz. Understand its analogy: Just as if someone buys his close relative, he will become free immediately, even though purchase (shira') proves ownership, not emancipation (tahrir). So here too, the expression of purchase proves ownership, and from the ruling of purchase, emancipation is proven. The ruling of purchase is ownership with kinship, and from ownership of a close relative, emancipation is proven by the hadith: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he is free.' So from the expression of purchase, ownership occurred, and from its ruling, emancipation occurred.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute defending the view that haqiqah and majaz can be present in one utterance without being combined in the same word. The objection was that in the nathr formula 'lillahi alayya an asuma rajaba', both nathr (literal) and yamin (metaphorical) cannot be intended simultaneously because that would require combining haqiqah and majaz in one word, which is prohibited. The author responds that nathr comes from the literal sighah (expression), while yamin comes not from the word's metaphorical meaning but from the ruling (hukm) of the speech: making a permissible act wajib is a yamin. They are two different aspects—one from the lafz, the other from the hukm. The author strengthens this with a qiyas: buying a mahram relative results in emancipation, even though the word 'buy' means ownership, not emancipation; emancipation follows from the ruling of owning a mahram. Thus haqiqah (ownership) and its effect (emancipation) are not combined in one word. Similarly, nathr (literal) and yamin (ruling) are not combined in one word. The hadith 'man malaka zā rahim mahram minhu ‘utaq' supports this.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): Literal or original meaning of a word, as established by its primary usage.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or secondary meaning of a word, used when haqiqah is impossible or abandoned.
صیغہ (sighah): The linguistic form or expression of a word.
حکم (hukm): The legal ruling or effect derived from a word or action; here the ruling of obligation.
یمین (yamin): An oath; here, making a permissible thing obligatory or forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, how is yamin proven in the nathr formula?
A) From the metaphorical meaning of the word 'nathr'.
B) From the literal meaning of the sighah.
C) From the ruling (hukm) of the speech, which is obligation of a permissible act.
D) From the context of Rajab being a sacred month.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah and majaz can coexist in one utterance when they arise from different aspects: the literal meaning from the expression and the metaphorical effect from the legal ruling.

Haqiqah Precedes Majaz: The Rule and Its Exceptions
Stating the usul rule and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of precedence between haqiqah and majaz, identify the two exceptions (muta'azzir and mahjur), and apply the rule to oath and agency scenarios.

Core Idea
Haqiqah is the original; majaz is used only when haqiqah is impossible or abandoned.

This lesson explains a fundamental usul al-fiqh principle: the literal meaning (haqiqah) is the default in interpreting legal speech. Majaz (metaphorical meaning) only takes over when the literal meaning is impossible to apply or has been abandoned by custom or shariah. The passage uses examples from oaths to illustrate the rule, and extends it to the appointment of an agent for litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its rationale
State the rule: as long as acting upon haqiqah is possible, majaz is set aside. The rationale is that haqiqah is the original (asl) and majaz is like a borrowed thing or deputy; the deputy cannot compete with the original.
This is a key usul principle: al-haqiqah tuqaddam 'ala al-majaz.
2
Define the two exceptions
Recognize two cases where haqiqah is abandoned: (a) muta'azzir (impossible) – literal meaning cannot be performed without extreme difficulty; (b) mahjur (abandoned) – literal meaning is no longer used in custom or shariah.
These exceptions are not arbitrary; they are based on the inability or impracticality of applying the literal meaning.
3
Apply the exceptions to oath examples
Work through the two examples: (1) oath not to eat from a date palm – literal meaning impossible if tree is fruitless; (2) oath not to place foot in a house – literal meanings (barefoot, on foot, as owner) are customarily abandoned. In both, majaz is intended.
Pay attention to the distinction: one is physically impossible, the other is conventionally abandoned.
4
Extend to the agency case
Understand how the author applies the rule to tawkīl bi'l-khuṣūmah: the haqiqah of litigation (specific court proceedings) is abandoned by shariah (since litigation can be delegated), so the agent's authority goes to absolute answering (mujarrad al-jawāb).
This shows that shariah abandonment (mahjur sharan) is equivalent to customary abandonment.
5
Test with the child example
Examine the example of swearing not to speak to a child: the literal meaning restricted to childhood is abandoned by shariah, so the oath is not time-limited. Confirm your understanding of how the rule operates.
This reinforces that the exception applies even when the literal meaning is technically possible but shariah disregards the restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ حُكْمِ هَذَا الْبَابِ أَنَّ الْعَمَلَ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ مَتى أَمْكَنَ سَقَطَ الْمَجَازُلِاَنَّ الْمُسْتَعَارَ لَا يَزَاحِمُ الْاَصْلَ فَإِنْ كَانَتِ الْحَقِيقَةُ مُتَعَذِّرَةً كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَاكُلُ مِنْ هَذِهِ النَّخْلَةِ أَوْ مَهْجُوْرَةً كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَضَعُ قَدَمَهُ فِي دَارِفَلَانٍ صِيْرَ إِلَى الْمَجَازِ ترجمہ :۔ اور اس باب کا حکم یہ ہے کہ حقیقت پر عمل کرنا جب تک ممکن ہو تو مجاز ساقط ہو جائے گا ،اس لئے کہ مستعار اصل کا مقابل نہیں ہو سکتا ، پس اگر حقیقی معنی متعذر ہوں جیسے جب قسم کھائی کہ اس کھجور کے درخت سے نہیں کھائے گا، یا حقیقی معنی متروک ہوں ( عرف میں ) جیسے جب قسم کھائی کہ اپنا قدم نہیں رکھے گا فلاں کے گھر میں تو کلام کو مجاز کی طرف پھیر دیا جائے گا۔ تشریح:- مصنف اللہ یہاں حقیقت و مجاز کا حکم بیان فرمارہے ہیں کہ حقیقت و مجاز کا حکم یہ ہے کہ جب تک حقیقت پر عمل ممکن ہو، مجاز پر عمل نہیں کیا جائے گا ، اس لئے کہ حقیقت اصل ہے اور مجاز مستعار ہے یعنی عاریت پر لیا ہوا ہے، دوسرے لفظوں میں یوں کہو کہ حقیقت اصل ہے اور مجاز خلیفہ ہے تو جب تک اصل موجود ہے مستعار اور خلیفہ مراد نہیں لیا جاسکتا ہے، کیونکہ مستعار اور خلیفہ اصل کے مقابل نہیں ہوسکتا ہے۔ ہاں اگر حقیقت پر عمل متعد ر ہو یعنی بغیر تکلف اور مشقت کے اس تک نہ پہنچا جا سکے یا حقیقت مہجورہ ہو یعنی اس پر عمل تو ہو سکتا ہو مگر عرف میں یہ معنی چھوڑ دیے گئے ہوں ، تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں کلام سے حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں لیں گے بلکہ کلام کو مجازی معنی کی طرف پھیر دیا جائے گا۔ حقیقت متعذرہ کی مثال جیسے کسی نے قسم کھائی - لا أكل من هذه النخله - اس درخت سے نہیں کھاؤنگا، تو اس کے حقیقی معنی اس درخت کو کھانا ، اور مجازی معنی اس درخت کے پھل کھانا ، اگر درخت پھل دار نہ ہو تو اس کی قیمت کھانا، تو یہاں حقیقی معنی متعذر اور مشکل ہے، لہذا اس کے مجازی معنی مراد ہو نگے یعنی پھل یا قیمت ، لہذا تکلف کر کے اگر اس درخت کو کھائے گا تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ حقیقت مہجورہ کی مثال جیسے کسی نے قسم کھائی - لا يضع قدمه فی دار فلان - فلاں کے گھر میں وہ قدم نہیں رکھے گا، تو اس کے حقیقی معنی ننگے پیر داخل ہونا، پیدل داخل ہونا، ملکیت کے گھر میں داخل ہونا ہے، اور مجازی معنی مطلق داخل ہونا جیسے پہلے اس کی تفصیل گذر چکی تو یہاں حقیقی معنی عرفاً متروک ہیں، لہذا مجازی معنی مراد ہوں گے۔ وَعَلَى هَذَا قُلْنَا اِنَّ التَّوْكِيْلَ بِالْخُصُومَةِ يَنْصَرِفْ إِلى مُطْلَقِ الْجَوَابِ لِاَنَّ الْحَقِيْقَةَ مَهْجُوْرَةٌ شَرْعًا وَالْمَهْجُورُ شَرْعًا بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمَهْجُوْرِ عَادَتَا أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ مَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُ هَذَا الصَّبِيَّ لَمْ يَتَقَيَّدُ بِزَمَانِ صِبَاهُ لِأَنَّ هِجْرَانَ الصَّبِيِّ مَهْجُورٌ شَرُعًا
And from the ruling of this chapter is that acting upon the haqiqah, whenever possible, cancels the majaz, because the borrowed term cannot compete with the original. If the haqiqah is impossible, like when he swore 'I will not eat from this date palm,' or is abandoned, like when he swore 'I will not place his foot in the house of so-and-so,' then one turns to majaz. Translation: And the ruling of this chapter is that acting upon haqiqah, as long as it is possible, will drop the majaz, because the borrowed term cannot oppose the original. So if the literal meaning is impossible, like when he swore that he will not eat from this date palm, or the literal meaning is abandoned (in custom), like when he swore that he will not place his foot in so-and-so's house, then the speech will be turned to majaz. Elaboration: The author is stating the ruling of haqiqah and majaz: as long as acting upon haqiqah is possible, acting upon majaz will not be done, because haqiqah is the original and majaz is borrowed. In other words, haqiqah is the original and majaz is the deputy; so as long as the original exists, the borrowed and the deputy cannot be intended, because the borrowed and deputy cannot compete with the original. Yes, if acting upon haqiqah is impossible, i.e., cannot be reached without difficulty and hardship, or the haqiqah is mahjurah, i.e., it is possible to act upon it but in custom these meanings have been abandoned, then in both cases the literal meanings will not be intended from the speech, rather the speech will be turned to metaphorical meaning. Example of impossible haqiqah: if someone swore 'La akulu min hadhihi al-nakhlah' - 'I will not eat from this tree,' its literal meaning is eating the tree itself, and its metaphorical meaning is eating its fruit. If the tree has no fruit, then its value is eaten. So here the literal meaning is impossible and difficult, so the metaphorical meaning (fruit or value) is intended. Therefore, if he forces himself to eat the tree, he will not be an oath-breaker. Example of abandoned haqiqah: if someone swore 'La yada'u qadamahu fi dari fulan' - 'He will not place his foot in the house of so-and-so,' its literal meanings are entering barefoot, entering on foot, or entering as owner, and its metaphorical meaning is absolute entering. As the detail has passed earlier, here the literal meanings are customarily abandoned, so metaphorical meaning is intended. And upon this we said that appointment as an agent for litigation turns to absolute answering, because the haqiqah is abandoned by shariah, and what is abandoned by shariah is like what is abandoned by custom. Do you not see that he who swore he will not speak to this child is not restricted to the time of his childhood, because the avoidance of the child is abandoned by shariah.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage states a core usul principle: haqiqah takes precedence over majaz. As long as the literal meaning is feasible, the metaphorical meaning is not used. This is because haqiqah is the original (asl) and majaz is like a borrowed thing or a deputy; the deputy cannot replace the original as long as the original is present. Two exceptions: (1) When haqiqah is impossible (muta'azzirah) – e.g., swearing not to eat from a date palm tree. The literal meaning is to eat the wood itself, which is impossible; so majaz (eating its fruit or value) is intended. (2) When haqiqah is abandoned (mahjurah) by custom – e.g., swearing not to place one's foot in a house. Literally, it could mean entering barefoot or as owner, but common custom has abandoned those specifics, so majaz (simple entry) is intended. The author then applies this rule to a fiqh issue: when someone appoints an agent for litigation (tawkīl bi'l-khuṣūmah), the haqiqah of litigation (which requires actual court presence) is abandoned by shariah, so it goes to absolute answering (mujarrad al-jawāb)—i.e., the agent can respond generally. This is supported by the example of swearing not to speak to a child: the haqiqah limited to childhood is abandoned by shariah, so it is unrestricted.

Key Terms
متعذر (muta'azzir): Impossible to act upon literally without extreme difficulty or impossibility.
مهجور (mahjur): Abandoned in custom or shariah; the literal meaning is no longer commonly used.
توكيل بالخصومة (tawkīl bi'l-khuṣūmah): Appointing an agent to handle litigation; its haqiqah is specific to court proceedings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not place my foot in Zaid's house,' according to the Hanafi rule, what is the intended meaning?
A) He must enter barefoot to avoid breaking his oath.
B) He must enter as an owner to avoid breaking his oath.
C) The literal meaning is mahjurah, so it refers to any kind of entry.
D) He cannot enter even if invited.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah is the default; majaz is resorted to only when haqiqah is impossible or abandoned.

Five Indicators for Preferring Majaz over Haqiqah
Lists five indicators for preferring majaz over haqiqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five indicators (dilalat) that cause the literal meaning to be abandoned in favor of metaphorical meaning in Hanafi usul, and apply each to an example.

Core Idea
Context, custom, speaker's state, textual sequence, and word scope — five indicators that override the literal meaning.

This passage from al-Husami presents a foundational list in Hanafi usul: five indicators (dilalat) that necessitate abandoning the literal meaning (haqiqah) and adopting the metaphorical (majaz). Memorizing this taxonomy arms the student with the interpretive tools to understand why classical scholars often departed from apparent meanings. Each indicator is illustrated with concrete examples, making this a crucial memorization anchor for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Context of Speech (dalālat maḥall al-kalām)
Identify when the situation or context of the speech renders the literal meaning impossible or false; in such cases, majaz is intended.
Example: a command in a context of impossibility becomes metaphorical.
2
Custom (dalālat al-ʿādah)
Examine common usage; if custom consistently uses a word figuratively, that interpretation overrides the literal.
Hanafis strongly consider ʿurf in interpretation.
3
Speaker's Intention (dalālat maʿnan yarjiʿu ilā al-mutakallim)
Consider the speaker's state or known intention; for example, in an oath of promptness (yamīn al-fawr), the intended meaning is immediate action.
This indicator contextualizes oaths and promises.
4
Textual Sequence (dalālat siyāq al-naẓm)
Analyze the surrounding composition; the immediate context can show that the apparent meaning is not intended, e.g., a threat disguised as permission.
Quranic example: 'So whoever wills, let him believe…' followed by threat of Fire.
5
Word Itself (dalālat al-lafẓ fī nafsihi)
Assess the intrinsic linguistic scope of the word; some referents are excluded by the word's conventional domain, as in oaths.
Hanafi: 'meat' excludes fish; 'fruit' excludes grapes in oath contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ جُمْلَةً مَا تُتْركُ بِهِ الْحَقِيقَةُ خَمْسَةٌ قَدْ تُتُرَكْ بِدَلَالَةِ مَحَلَّ الْكَلَامِ وَبِدَلَالَةِ الْعَادَةِ كَمَا ذَكَرْنَا وَبِدَلَالَةِ مَعْنًى يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْمُتَكَلِّمِ كَمَا فِيْ يَمِيْنِ الْفَوْرِ وَبِدَلَالَةِ سِيَاقِ النَّظْمِ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْانًا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّلِمِينَ نَارًا وَبِدَلَالَةِ اللُّفْظِ فِى نَفْسِهِ كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَاكُل لَحْمًا فَأَكَلَ لَحْمَ السَّمَكِ لَمْ يَحْنَثُ وَكَذَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَاكُلُ فَاكِهَةٌ فَأَكَلَ الْعِنَبَ لَمْ يَحْنَثُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةِ لِقُصُورِ فِي الْمَعْنَى الْمَطْلُوبِ فِي الْأَوَلِ وَزِيَادَهِ فِي الثَّانِي
ترجمہ :۔ پھر وہ تمام مقامات جہاں حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دئے جاتے ہیں پانچ ہیں کبھی حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دئے جاتے ہیں محل کلام کی دلالت کی وجہ سے اور عادت کی دلالت کی وجہ سے جیسا کہ ہم ذکر کر چکے ، اور ایسے معنی کی دلالت کی وجہ سے جو متکلم کی طرف لوٹتے ہیں جیسا کہ یمین فور میں ، اور سیاق نظم کی دلالت کی وجہ سے جیسے اللہ تعالی کے اس فرمان میں ” پس جس کا جی چاہے ایمان لائے اور جس کا جی چاہے کفر کرے ہم نے ظالموں کے لئے جہنم تیار کر رکھی ہے اور لفظ کی اپنی ذات کے اعتبار سے دلالت کی وجہ سے جیسے کہ جب قسم کھائی کہ وہ گوشت نہیں کھائے گا ، پس اس نے مچھلی کا گوشت کھا یا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا، اور اسی طرح جب قسم کھائی میوہ نہیں کھائے گا، پس اس نے انگور کھالی تو حانث نہیں ہوگا امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک معنی مطلوب میں کمی کی وجہ سے اول میں اور زیادتی کی وجہ سے ثانی میں ۔
تشریح:- یہاں مصنف ہے ان مقامات کو بیان کر رہے ہیں جہاں حقیقی معنی چھوڑ کر مجازی معنی مراد لئے جاتے ہیں ، وہ پانچ جگہیں ہیں ۔ پہلی جگہ محل کلام کی دلالت کی وجہ سے حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دئے جاتے ہیں یعنی جس محل میں کلام واقع ہورہا ہے و محل حقیقی معنی قبول کرنے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا ہے، حقیقی معنی مراد لینے سے یا تو کذب لازم آتا ہے یا اور کوئی وجہ سے محل حقیقی معنی کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا ہے، حالانکہ اس کا وقوع قرآن وحدیث میں ہے لہذا حقیقی معنی چھوڑ کر مجازی معنی
Then the totality of what causes the literal to be abandoned is five: sometimes it is abandoned by the indication of the context of speech (dilālat maḥall al-kalām), by the indication of custom (dilālat al-ʿādah) as we mentioned, by the indication of a meaning that refers back to the speaker (dilālat maʿnan yarjiʿu ilā al-mutakallim) as in the oath of promptness (yamīn al-fawr), by the indication of the sequence of composition (dilālat siyāq al-naẓm) as in the statement of Allah Most High: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a Fire', and by the indication of the word itself (dilālat al-lafẓ fī nafsihi) as when someone swears 'I will not eat meat' and then eats fish meat, he does not break his oath; similarly, if he swears 'I will not eat fruit' and then eats grapes, he does not break his oath according to Abu Hanifa, due to a deficiency in the intended meaning in the first and an excess in the second.
Translation: Then all the places where the literal meaning is abandoned are five: Sometimes the literal meaning is abandoned due to the indication of the context of speech, and due to the indication of custom as we have mentioned, and due to the indication of a meaning that returns to the speaker as in the oath of promptness, and due to the indication of the sequence of composition as in the saying of Allah Most High: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. We have prepared for the wrongdoers a Fire', and due to the indication of the word itself as when someone swears he will not eat meat, then he eats fish meat, he does not become a breaker of oath; and similarly, when he swears he will not eat fruit, then he eats grapes, he does not become a breaker of oath according to Imam Abu Hanifa, due to a deficiency in the intended meaning in the first and an excess in the second.
Explanation: Here the author (musannif) is explaining those places where the literal meaning is abandoned and the metaphorical meaning is intended. They are five places. The first place: Due to the indication of the context of speech, the literal meaning is abandoned. That is, the place in which the speech occurs does not have the capacity to accept the literal meaning. Taking the literal meaning would entail either a lie or some other reason that the place does not have the capacity for the literal meaning, even though its occurrence is in the Qur'an and Hadith. Therefore, the literal meaning is abandoned and the metaphorical meaning is [adopted].
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage from the hadāyah (referring to al-Husāmī) enumerates five categories of indicators (dilālāt) that cause the literal meaning (haqīqah) to be abandoned in favor of metaphorical meaning (majāz). The author presents these as a memorization list for students. The first indicator is the context or situation of speech (maḥall al-kalām) – if the literal meaning does not fit the situation, majāz is taken. The second is custom (ʿādah) – common usage may dictate a figurative interpretation. The third is a meaning that refers back to the speaker (maʿnan yarjiʿu ilā al-mutakallim), exemplified by an oath of promptness (yamīn al-fawr) where the intended meaning is immediate action. The fourth is the sequence or composition (siyāq al-naẓm), as in the Quranic verse that apparently gives permission to believe or disbelieve, but the context shows it is a threat. The fifth is the word itself (al-lafẓ fī nafsihi), as in oaths, where the word 'meat' normally excludes fish, and 'fruit' normally excludes grapes according to Imam Abu Hanifa due to linguistic conventions. The Urdu commentary begins to explain the first reason but cuts off mid-sentence. The passage is foundational for understanding when and why scholars depart from literal interpretations.

Key Terms
دلالة محل الكلام (dalālat maḥall al-kalām): Indication from the context or situation in which the speech occurs; if the literal meaning is inappropriate, majaz is used.
دلالة العادة (dalālat al-ʿādah): Indication from custom or common usage; if custom uses a word metaphorically, that is preferred.
دلالة معنى يرجع إلى المتكلم (dalālat maʿnan yarjiʿu ilā al-mutakallim): Indication from a meaning pertaining to the speaker's state, as in an oath of promptness where the intended meaning is immediate.
دلالة سياق النظم (dalālat siyāq al-naẓm): Indication from the sequence or composition of the text; the surrounding text shows the intended meaning is not literal.
دلالة اللفظ في نفسه (dalālat al-lafẓ fī nafsihi): Indication from the word itself; the word's linguistic scope may exclude certain referents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which indicator leads to abandoning the literal meaning in the Quranic verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe...'?
A) Indication of the context of speech
B) Indication of custom
C) Indication of the sequence of composition
D) Indication of the word itself

Key Takeaway
The five indicators of abandoning haqiqah are essential tools for interpreting texts beyond their literal shell.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

Two Reasons for Abandoning Literal Meaning
Explaining the fourth and fifth reasons for abandoning literal meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify context (siyaq) and root derivation (maddah) as reasons for departing from the literal meaning, and apply them to oath scenarios to determine whether the oath is broken.

Core Idea
Both context and a word's root derivation can restrict its scope, overriding the literal meaning.

In Hanafi usul, the literal (haqiqi) meaning of a word is the default, but it may be abandoned when there is a clear indicator. This passage from a sharh on Al-Husami introduces two such indicators: the context of speech (siyaq kalam) and the word's own root derivation (maddah). Through Quranic and oath examples, you will see how these indicators operate to exclude certain individuals from a word's scope, and how custom can influence the final ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reasons
Locate the fourth and fifth reasons in the passage: context (siyaq kalam) and word derivation (maddah). Note that both cause the literal meaning to be abandoned in favor of a figurative or restricted meaning.
The fourth reason uses a verbal qarina; the fifth uses the word's root meaning.
2
Analyze the context example
Examine the Quranic verse about choice in faith followed by the threat of fire. Understand why the literal meaning (actual choice) is abandoned: the following clause is a qarina indicating deterrence, not permission.
The qarina here is a verbal (lafzi) indicator within the same speech.
3
Analyze the derivation example (lahm)
Study the example of an oath not to eat 'lahm' (meat). The root derivation of 'lahm' implies intensity due to blood; fish (bloodless) is therefore excluded. See how the root meaning restricts the word's application despite general coinage.
The principle: if the intended meaning of the word (by derivation) is deficient or excessive in an individual, that individual is excluded.
4
Note the dispute and custom
Examine the example of 'fakiha' (fruit) where Abu Hanifa excludes grapes (because they have nutritional value, exceeding the derivation's implication of mere pleasure food), while the Sahibayn include grapes based on common custom. Understand that custom can override derivation.
The later tanbih confirms that oaths are governed by local custom.

Source Text

چوتھی جگہ : سیاق کلام کی دلالت کی وجہ سے حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دئے جاتے ہیں، یعنی کلام میں ایسا کوئی لفظی قرینہ ہوتا ہے جو دلالت کرتا ہے کہ یہاں حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں ہے جیسے اللہ تعالی کا ارشاد ” جو چاہے ایمان لائے اور جو چاہے کفر کرے، بے شک ہم نے ظالموں کے لئے آگ تیار کر رکھی ہے اس کلام کے حقیقی معنی ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ ایمان و کفر میں اختیار دے رہے ہیں، یعنی چا ہو تو ایمان لاؤ، چاہو تو کفر کرو، دونوں کا اختیار ہے۔ مگر یہ معنی مراد نہیں ہے کیونکہ کلام میں اگلا جملہ انا اعتدنا للكافرين قرینہ ہے، جو بتلا رہا ہے کہ یہاں حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں ہے، بلکہ مجازی معنی مراد ہے یعنی زجر و تو یخ مقصود ہے۔ پانچویں جگہ: کبھی لفظ کی ذاتی دلالت کی وجہ سے حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دئے جاتے ہیں یعنی لفظ اپنے مادہ اور ماخذ اشتقاق کے لحاظ سے جس معنی پر دلالت کرتا ہے وہ معنی اس میں نہیں پائے جاتے ، اس لئے حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دیے ، تو یہ لفظ کی ذاتی دلالت ہوئی مثلاً لفظ اپنے مادے اور اشتقاق کے لحاظ سے ایسے معنی کے لئے وضع ہوا ہے، جس میں قوت اور شدت کے معنی ہیں، تو اس کے افراد میں سے وہ نکل جائینگے جس میں یہ معنی ناقص ہیں ، ایسے ہی کوئی لفظ ایسے معنی کے لئے وضع کیا گیا ہے جس میں ضعف اور نقصان کے معنی ہیں، تو اس کے افراد میں سے وہ نکل جائیں گے جن میں یہ معنی زیادتی کے ساتھ پائے جاتے ہیں۔ الحاصل لفظ اپنی وضع کے اعتبار سے تو اپنے تمام افراد کو شامل ہے مگر اپنے مادے اور ماخذ اشتقاق کی وجہ سے صرف ان ہی افراد کو شامل رہے گا جن میں یہ معنی جس پر یہ لفظ اپنے مادے اور ماخذ اشتقاق سے دلالت کرتا ہے برابر درجہ کے ہوں ، اور جن افراد میں وہ معنی کمی کے ساتھ یا زیادتی کے ساتھ پائے جائینگے وہ افراد خارج ہو جائیں گے۔ مثال سے سمجھو، کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ وہ گوشت نہیں کھائے گا ، اگر اس نے مچھلی کا گوشت کھالیا، تو حانث نہ ہوگا کیونکہ خود لفظ کی دلالت سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ مچھلی کا گوشت اس میں داخل نہیں ہے، کیونکہ تم التحام سے بنا ہے اور اس میں شدت کے معنی ہیں، اور مچھلی کے گوشت میں خون نہ ہونے کہ سبب شدت نہیں ہے، لہذا مچھلی خارج ہو جائے گی حالانکہ حکم میں وضع کے اعتبار سے مچھلی بھی داخل ہے مگر لفظ کے مادے کی دلالت کی وجہ سے وہ خارج ہو جائے گی ، اور مچھلی کے علاوہ باقی خون والے حلال جانوروں کا گوشت اس میں داخل رہے گا۔ دوسری مثال کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ وہ فاکھہ نہیں کھائے گا ، اگر اس نے انگور کھالی تو حانث نہ ہوگا کیونکہ لفظ فاکہہ مادہ کے اعتبار سے دلالت کرتا ہے ایسی چیز پر جس میں فرحت ولذت ہو، یعنی جو چیز فرحت ولذت کے طور پر کھائی جائے ، اس میں غذائیت اور بدن کا قوام نہ ہو اور انگور میں فرحت ولذت کے ساتھ غذائیت بھی ہے، تو فا کہہ کے معنی انگور میں زیادتی کے ساتھ پائے گئے ، لہذا امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک حانث نہ ہوگا ،صاحبین کے نزدیک حانث ہو جائے گا، تفصیل کے لئے ہدایہ کی مراجعت کریں۔ بہر حال مچھلی میں لحم کے معنی کمی کے ساتھ پائے جارہے ہیں اور انگور میں فاکہہ کے معنی زیادتی کے ساتھ پائے جارہے ہیں، لہندا مچھلی تم میں اور انگور فاکہہ میں داخل نہ ہوگی ، لہذا گوشت نہ کھانے کی قسم کھانے والا مچھلی کا گوشت کھانے سے اور فاکہہ نہ کھانے کی قسم کھانے والا انگور کھانے سے امام صاحب کے نزدیک حانث نہ ہوگا ، اور صاحبین کے نزدیک حانث ہو جائے گا۔ مشائخ نے لکھا ہے کہ امام صاحب اور صاحبین کا یہ اختلاف عرف وزمان کے اختلاف کی وجہ سے ہے، امام صاحب نے اپنے زمانے اور عرف کے اعتبار سے فتویٰ دیا ہے، اور صاحبین نے اپنے زمانے کے عرف کے لحاظ سے، صاحبین کے زمانے میں عرف بدل گیا تھا۔
تنبیہ : یمین کا مدار عرف پر ہے، لہذا ہر علاقہ کے عرف کے اعتبار سے حکم ہوگا ، اگر کسی علاقہ میں مچھلی کے گوشت پر گوشت کا اطلاق ہوتا ہے، اور عام عرف یہی ہے تو مچھلی کے گوشت سے حانث ہو جائے گا۔
Specification. Fourth place: Due to the indication of the context of speech, the literal meaning is abandoned. That is, there is some verbal qarina in the speech which indicates that the literal meaning is not intended here, like Allah's statement: 'Whoever wills let him believe, and whoever wills let him disbelieve; indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire.' The literal meaning of this speech is that Allah is giving choice in faith and disbelief; i.e., if you want, believe, if you want, disbelieve; both are optional. But this meaning is not intended because the next sentence in the speech 'Indeed, We have prepared for the disbelievers' is a qarina indicating that the literal meaning is not intended, but rather the figurative meaning is intended, which is deterrence and threat. Fifth place: Sometimes due to the intrinsic indication of the word, the literal meaning is abandoned. That is, the word, by virtue of its root and derivation, indicates a meaning which is not present in [what would have been its literal extension]; therefore the literal meaning is abandoned. So this is the word's intrinsic indication. For example, a word is coined for a meaning in which there is strength and intensity; then those individuals in which this meaning is deficient will be excluded. Similarly, a word coined for a meaning in which there is weakness and deficiency; then those individuals in which this meaning is found in excess will be excluded. In short, the word by its coinage includes all its individuals, but due to its root and derivation, it will only include those individuals in which the meaning that the word indicates by its root and derivation is of equal degree; those individuals in which that meaning is found deficiently or excessively will be excluded. Understand by example: Someone swore that he will not eat meat (lahm). If he ate fish meat, he does not become a hanith because from the word's own indication it is known that fish meat is not included, because [lahm] is derived from [the root] with the meaning of intensity, and fish meat does not have intensity due to lack of blood. Therefore, fish is excluded, although in ruling by coinage fish is included, but due to the indication of the word's root it will be excluded, and the meat of other blooded halal animals besides fish remains included. Second example: Someone swore that he will not eat fruit (fakiha). If he ate grapes, he does not become a hanith according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the word fakiha by derivation indicates something in which there is joy and pleasure, i.e., something eaten for joy and pleasure, with no nutrition and bodily sustenance. Grapes have nutrition along with joy and pleasure, so the meaning of fakiha is found in grapes in excess. Therefore, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, he does not become a hanith; according to the Sahibayn, he becomes a hanith. For details, refer to Hidaya. In any case, in fish the meaning of lahm is found deficiently, and in grapes the meaning of fakiha is found excessively; therefore fish is not included in lahm and grapes are not included in fakiha. Hence, the one who swore not to eat meat eating fish meat and the one who swore not to eat fruit eating grapes do not become hanith according to Imam Sahib, but become hanith according to the Sahibayn. The mashayikh wrote that this difference between Imam Sahib and the Sahibayn is due to difference in custom and time. Imam Sahib gave fatwa according to his time and custom, and the Sahibayn according to the custom of their time; the custom had changed by the time of the Sahibayn. Notice: An oath depends on custom; therefore the ruling will be according to the custom of each region. If in a region fish meat is called meat, and that is common custom, then eating fish meat will make him a hanith.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul commentary explaining two reasons why the literal (haqiqi) meaning of a word may be abandoned in favor of a figurative (majazi) meaning. The fourth reason is the context of speech (siyaq kalam) providing a verbal qarina (indication) that the literal meaning is not intended, and the fifth reason is the intrinsic indication (zati dalalat) of the word's root/derivation restricting its application. For the fourth reason, the author gives the Quranic verse about choice in faith followed by the threat of fire as a qarina showing deterrence is intended, not literal permission. For the fifth reason, the word's root meaning imposes a condition: for 'lahm' (meat), the root implies strength and intensity due to blood, so fish (bloodless) is excluded; for 'fakiha' (fruit), the root implies something eaten for pleasure without being staple food, so grapes (which have nutritional value) are excluded. However, the passage notes that Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differed on the fruit example, attributing the difference to changing customs. The passage then clarifies that in oaths, custom (urf) determines the scope, so if local custom calls fish 'meat', the oath would be broken. This is a typical Hanafi approach: giving weight to the literal derivation but also considering custom.

Key Terms
حقیقی (haqiqi): Literal meaning of a word as per its original coinage.
مجازی (majazi): Figurative meaning, not the originally coined meaning.
قرینہ (qarina): A contextual indicator that shows which meaning is intended.
مادہ (maddah): The root letters of a word from which its meaning is derived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears not to eat 'lahm' (meat), does eating fish break the oath according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) Yes, because fish is a type of meat.
B) No, because 'lahm' derives from a root meaning intensity/blood, and fish lacks blood.
C) Yes, because the Quran uses 'lahm' for fish.
D) No, because oaths are always interpreted figuratively.

Key Takeaway
Context (siyaq) and word derivation (maddah) can both override the literal meaning; in oaths, local custom ultimately determines the ruling.

Sarih and Kinayah: Intention and Legal Effect
Defining sarih and kinayah and their legal effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih and kinayah and state the Hanafi ruling on the necessity of intention for each.

Core Idea
Sarih needs no intention; kinayah needs intention to produce legal effect.

In Hanafi usul, words are not all alike: some carry their meaning so clearly that intention is irrelevant, while others are ambiguous and require the speaker's intention to be effective. This passage defines these two categories—sarih and kinayah—and lays out their legal consequences, especially in contracts and divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Find where the passage defines sarih and kinayah. Note the examples given for each.
Sarih examples: 'I sold,' 'I bought,' 'I gifted' in contracts. Kinayah is defined indirectly.
2
Understand the ruling for sarih
The passage states that for sarih, the legal effect attaches to the very utterance and it stands in place of its meaning, so no intention is needed. Confirm this with the example of divorce.
The commentator gives the example: if someone says 'You are divorced' without intention, divorce still occurs.
3
Understand the ruling for kinayah
For kinayah, action is not obligatory except with intention because the intended meaning is hidden. The passage adds that kinayah resembles majaz before it becomes conventional.
Kinayah requires intention or a contextual clue to resolve ambiguity.
4
Apply to divorce expressions
The passage explains that words like 'al-bain' and 'al-haram' are kinayat al-talaq; they require intention because of ambiguity in what they connect to and operate upon.
Once intention is present, the divorce becomes effective and is irrevocable (bain).
5
Relate to the concept of majaz
Note the analogy: majaz before becoming conventional is like kinayah; after conventionalization, it becomes like sarih.
This illustrates that the classification can shift over time with usage.

Source Text

وَأَمَّا الصَّرِيحُ فَمِثْلُ قَوْلِهِ بِعْتُ وَاشْتَرَيْتُ وَوَهَبْتُ وَحُكْمُهُ تَعَلُّقُ الْحُكْمِ بِعَيْنِ الْكَلَامِ وَقِيَامُهُ مَقَامَ مَعْنَاهُ حَتَّى اسْتَغْنَى عَنِ الْعَزِيْمَةِ لِأَنَّهُ ظَاهِرُ الْمُرَادِ وَحُكْمُ الْكِنَايَهِ أَنَّهُ لَا يَجِبُ الْعَمَلُ  ِبهِ إِلَّا بِالنّيَةِ لأَنَّهُ مُسْتَرَ الْمُرَادِ وَذَلِكَ مِثْلُ الْمَجَاز قَبْلَ أَنْ يَصِيْرَ مُتَعَارَفًا ترجمہ : اور بہر حال صریح پس جیسے اس کا قول بعت، اشتریت اور وهبت اور صریح کا حکم حکم کا متعلق ہوتا ہے عین کلام کے ساتھ اور کلام کا اپنے معنی کے قائم مقام ہونا ہے، یہاں تک کہ صریح نیت سے بے نیاز ہوگا اس لئے کہ اس کی مراد ظاہر ہے، اور کنایہ کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس پر عمل واجب نہیں ہو گا مگر نیت سے ، اس لئے کہ اس کی مراد پوشیدہ ہے اور کنایہ مجاز کی طرح ہے اس کے متعارف ہونے سے پہلے ۔ تشریح:- مصنف ہے یہاں سے صریح اور کنایہ کی بحث شروع کر رہے ہیں ، صریح کی تعریف نہیں کی کیونکہ اس کی تعریف ظاہر تھی ، صرف اشارہ کر دیا کہ صریح اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی مراد بالکل ظاہر ہو، جیسے بعت ، اشتریت، وهبت ، ان الفاظ میں بیع ، شراء اور صبہ کے ثبوت کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ الفاظ صریح ہیں ۔ صریح کا حکم یہ ہے کہ صریح میں متکلم کی نیت اور ارادہ کی ضرورت نہیں ہے، کیونکہ اس کے معنی ایسے ظاہر ہیں کہ لفظ خود معنی کی جگہ پر ہے، اس میں کوئی دوسرا احتمال ہوتا ہی نہیں ، جیسے کسی نے بلا ارادہ بھی انت طالق کہا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ، چاہے وہ کہے کہ میری مراد طلاق کی نہیں ہے، کیونکہ لفظ صریح ہے۔ مصنف نے کنایہ کی تعریف بھی نہیں کی ، صرف اشارہ کیا کہ کنایہ اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی مراد پوشیدہ ہو بغیر نیت کے ظاہر نہ ہو سکے۔  الله. کنایہ کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس پر عمل بغیر نیت کے واجب نہیں ہے، کیونکہ اس کے معنی میں تردد ہے، لہذا اس میں نیت یا اس کے قائم مقام چیز ( مثلا کوئی قرینہ) کی ضرورت پڑے گی ، جب نیت سے اس کا خفا دور ہو جائے گا تو اس پر عمل واجب ہوگا۔ وذلك مثل المجاز:- مصنف اللہ کہتے ہیں کہ کنایہ میں چونکہ خفا اور پوشیدگی ہوتی ہے، اس کی مراد ظاہر نہیں ہوتی ہے، تو یہ ایسا ہی ہے جیسے مجاز جب وہ متعارف نہ ہو، کیونکہ متعارف ہونے سے پہلے مجاز میں بھی خفا اور پوشیدگی ہوتی ہے، ہاں جب مجاز متعارف ہو جائے تو خفا اور تر در ختم ہو جاتا ہے ،لہذا پھر مجاز کنا ہی نہیں رہے گا بلکہ صریح ہو جائے گا، تو گو یا مجاز جب تک متعارف نہ ہو وہ کنایہ ہی سمجھا جاتا ہے مصنف کی عبارت سے اس طرف اشارہ ہے کہ کنا یہ جیسے حقیقت میں ہوتا ہے ایسے ہی مجاز میں بھی ہوتا ہے۔  وَسُمِيَ الْبَائِنُ وَالْحَرَامَ وَنَحْوُهُمَا كِنَايَاتِ الطَّلَاقِ مَجَازَ الاَنَّهَا مَعْلُومَةُ الْمَعَانِئ لَكِنَّ الْأَبْهَامَ فِيْمَا يَتَّصِلُ بِهِ وَتَعْمَلُ فِيْهِ فَلِذْلِكَ شَابَهَتِ الْكِنَايَاتِ فَسْقِيَتْ بِذَلِكَ مَجَازَاوَلِهَذَا الْأَبْهَامِ أَحْتِيجَ إِلَى النِّيَّةِ فَإِذَازَالَ الْإِبْهَامُ بِالنِّيَّةِ وَجَبَ الْعَمَلُ بِمَوْجِبَاتِهَا مِنْ غَيْرِانْ تُجْعَلَ عِبَارَةَ عَنِ الصَّرِيحِ وَلِذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَاهَا بَوَائِنَ ترجمہ: اور نام رکھا گیا بائن ، حرام اور ان دونوں الفاظ کے مانند کا کنایات طلاق مجازاً، اس لئے کہ ان الفاظ
And as for sarih (explicit), it is like his saying 'I sold', 'I bought', 'I gifted'; its ruling is that the legal effect attaches to the very utterance and it stands in place of its meaning, so that it dispenses with intention, because its intended meaning is apparent. And the ruling of kinayah (implicit) is that action is not obligatory by it except with intention, because its intended meaning is hidden. And that is like majaz (figurative) before it becomes conventionally recognized. Translation: And anyway, sarih is like his saying 'I sold', 'I bought', 'I gifted'; the ruling of sarih is that the ruling attaches to the very word and the word stands in place of its meaning, so that it is independent of intention because its intended meaning is apparent. And the ruling of kinayah is that action is not obligatory by it except with intention, because its intended meaning is hidden. And kinayah is like majaz before it becomes conventional. Commentary: The author begins here the discussion of sarih and kinayah. He did not define sarih because its definition was apparent; he only indicated that sarih is that whose intended meaning is completely apparent, like 'I sold', 'I bought', 'I gifted'—in these words, for the establishment of sale, purchase, and gift, no intention is needed because these words are sarih. The ruling of sarih is that the intention and will of the speaker is not needed, because its meanings are so apparent that the word itself stands in place of the meaning; there is no other possibility in it. For example, if someone says 'You are divorced' without intention, divorce occurs, even if he says 'I did not intend divorce', because the word is sarih. The author also did not define kinayah, only indicated that kinayah is that whose intended meaning is hidden and cannot become apparent without intention. The ruling of kinayah is that action is not obligatory without intention, because there is hesitation in its meaning. Hence, it requires intention or something that stands in its place (like a contextual clue). When its concealment is removed by intention, then action becomes obligatory. 'And that is like majaz': The author says that because kinayah involves concealment and hiddenness, and its intended meaning is not apparent, it is like majaz when it is not conventional, because before becoming conventional, majaz also has concealment and hiddenness. Yes, when majaz becomes conventional, the concealment ends, so then majaz will no longer be kinayah but becomes sarih. So as long as majaz is not conventional, it is considered kinayah. The author's words indicate that kinayah occurs both in haqiqah and in majaz. 'And al-bain and al-haram and the like are called kinayat al-talaq (implicit expressions of divorce) figuratively, because they are known in meaning but there is ambiguity in what they connect to and act upon. Therefore, they resemble kinayat and are thus called majaz. Due to this ambiguity, intention is needed. When the ambiguity is removed by intention, action becomes obligatory according to their implications, without making them expressions of sarih. And for that reason we make them bain (irrevocable divorce). Translation: And names such as al-bain and al-haram and the like are called kinayat al-talaq figuratively, because these words' meanings are known, but there is ambiguity in what they connect to and operate upon. Therefore they resemble kinayat and are called majaz. Due to this ambiguity, intention is needed. When the ambiguity is removed by intention, action becomes obligatory according to their implications, without making them expressions of sarih. And for that reason we make them bain (irrevocable).
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of two key terms in Hanafi usul: sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit). The author (the commentator) introduces the discussion from the matn. He explains that sarih words are those whose intended meaning is immediately clear from the word itself; no additional intention (niyyah) is needed. Examples include 'I sold', 'I bought', 'I gifted' in contracts, and 'You are divorced' in divorce. Even if the speaker had no intention, the legal effect occurs because the word is sarih. Kinayah words, on the other hand, are ambiguous; their intended meaning is hidden. Therefore, for a legal effect to occur, intention (or a clear context) is required. The author then draws an analogy with majaz (figurative language): before a figurative expression becomes conventionally recognized (muta'araf), it is like kinayah in requiring interpretation. Once it becomes conventional, it becomes like sarih. The passage concludes with an application: words like 'you are bain' or 'you are haram' are kinayah for divorce; they are called majaz because their meanings are known but there is ambiguity about the specific action. Thus, intention resolves the ambiguity and makes the divorce effective, resulting in an irrevocable (bain) divorce.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): A word that is clear in meaning without needing intention, such that legal effect attaches to the utterance itself.
کنایة (kinayah): A word whose intended meaning is hidden and cannot be acted upon without intention or contextual clarification.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative language that, before becoming conventional (muta'araf), resembles kinayah in requiring interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'You are bain' without any intention of divorce, does a divorce occur according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) No, because bain is not a word of divorce.
B) Yes, because bain is sarih for divorce.
C) No, because bain is kinayah and requires intention.
D) It depends on whether the couple is in a state of anger.

Key Takeaway
Sarih words need no intention; kinayah words need intention to produce legal effect.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Kināyah Words in Ṭalāq and Their Legal Effect
Explains three kināyah words for ṭalāq, their need for niyyah, and resulting rajʿī ṭalāq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three kināyah words for ṭalāq, explain why they require intention, and derive the ruling that they effect a revocable (rajʿī) ṭalāq in the Hanafī school.

Core Idea
Kināyah in ṭalāq requires intention; with it, it is like ṣarīḥ and yields rajʿī ṭalāq.

The Hanafī school distinguishes between explicit (ṣarīḥ) and allusive (kināyah) words in ṭalāq. While explicit words effect divorce without intention, kināyah words require the husband's intention to be legally effective. This lesson explores three specific kināyah words—iʿtaddī, istabriʿī, and anti wāḥidah—and demonstrates through the ḥadīth of Sawdah (ra) that with proper intention, they result in a revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī). We will also trace the underlying uṣūl principle that ṣarīḥ is the default in speech, a rule with broader implications for criminal confessions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept of kināyah
Understand that kināyah is indirect speech that requires intention (niyyah) to clarify its legal meaning, unlike ṣarīḥ which is explicit. Note the uṣūlī principle: al-aṣl fī al-kalām huwa al-ṣarīḥ.
Kināyah is ambiguous; intention resolves ambiguity.
2
Examine the three kināyah words
Study the three words: (1) iʿtaddī (observe your ʿiddah), (2) istabriʿī raḥimak (purify your womb), (3) anti wāḥidah (you are one). Each has multiple possible meanings, creating ambiguity that requires intention to be resolved.
For istabriʿī, the two possibilities are purifying for the current husband or for a future husband. For anti wāḥidah, possibilities include unique in beauty, religion, or one divorce.
3
Analyze the dalīl (proof)
The ḥadīth of Sawdah (ra) shows the Prophet ﷺ said 'Iʿtaddī' to her and later took her back (rajʿah). This proves that iʿtaddī, when intended for ṭalāq, effects a revocable divorce.
The Prophet's act of returning (rajʿah) confirms the divorce was rajʿī, not bāʾin.
4
Apply the principle: kināyah with intention = ṣarīḥ
When intention removes ambiguity, the kināyah word functions like an explicit word. For all three words, if the husband intends ṭalāq, the resulting divorce is rajʿī, whether the wife is consummated or not (with a slight technical distinction for non-consummated wives).
For non-consummated wives, istabriʿī is interpreted metaphorically as divorce.
5
Evaluate the uṣūlī implication for punishments
The passage concludes with a broader uṣūl principle: ṣarīḥ is the default in speech. Kināyah is deficient in that it fails to convey meaning without intention. This difference is crucial in matters repelled by doubts (shubuhāt), such as criminal confessions: a kināyah confession does not incur punishment.
This reflects the Hanafī maxim: al-ḥudūd tudraʾ bi al-shubuhāt.

جانبین سے درست نہیں ہے، بلکہ ایک طرف سے استعارہ درست ہے، سبب بولکر مسبب مراد لینا درست ہے مگر مسبب بولکر سبب مراد لینا درست نہیں ہے تو یہاں آپ نے کیسے مسبب بولکر سبب مراد لیا ؟

جواب یہ ہے کہ وہ بات عام حالات میں تھی لیکن اگر مسبب ایسا ہے جو سبب کے ساتھ خاص ہے تو استعارہ ہے اور یہاں مسبب یعنی عدت گزارنا سبب یعنی طلاق کے ساتھ خاص ہے بغیر طلاق کے عدت گزارنا نہیں ہوتا ہے، لہذا وَكَذَلِكَ قَوْلُهُ إِسْتَبْرِئِيْ رَحِمَكِ وَقَدْ جَاءَتْ بِهِ السُّنَّةُ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ قَالَ لِسَوْدَةَ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهَا إِعْتَدِئُ ثُمَّ رَاجَعَهَا وَكَذَلِكَ أَنْتِ وَاحِدَةٌ يَحْتَمِلُ نَعْنَّا لِلطَّلَقَةِ وَيَحْتَمِلُ صِفَةً لِلْمَرْأَةِ فَإِذَازَالَ الْإِبْهَامُ بِالنِّيَةِ كَانَ دَلَالَةً عَلَى الصَّرِيحَ لَا عَامِلًا بِمُوْجَبِهِ ترجمہ :۔ اور ایسے ہی شوہر کا قول " استبرئی رحمک ، اور تحقیق کہ اس کے بارے میں حدیث وارد ہوئی ہے کہ نبی کریم صلی یا پیام نے حضرت سودہ رضی اللہ عنہا سے فرمایا اعتدی پھر آپ صلی اینم نے ان سے رجوع فرما لیا ،اور ایسے ہی انت واحدة “ ہے ، یہ لفظ احتمال رکھتا ہے طلقہ کی صفت کا ، اور احتمال رکھتا ہے عورت کی صفت کا ، پس جب نیت سے ابہام دور ہو گیا تو یہ لفظ دلیل ہو گا طلاق صریح پر نہ کہ عمل کرنے والا اپنے منہ تشریح :۔ دوسرا کنائی لفظ جس سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے، استبرئی رحمک ہے،اس کے معنی ہیں تو اپنے رحم کو پاک کر لے ، اس میں دو احتمال ہے (۱) تو بچہ کے لئے رحم پاک کر یعنی حیض سے رحم پاک کر ، تا کہ میں وطی کروں اور بچہ کی امید ہو، (۲) دوسرے شوہر کے لئے رحم پاک کرے تو اس میں ابہام آگیا، اب نیت کی ضرورت پڑے گی ، جب شوہر نے نیت کر لی کہ دوسرے شوہر کے لئے رحم پاک کرنا میری مراد ہے، تو یہاں بھی وہی دو صورتیں ہوں گی، مدخول بھا اور غیر مدخول بھا جو اعتدی میں تھی بالکل وہی تقریر یہاں بھی ہے کہ اگر عورت مدخول بھا ہے تو استبرکی سے پہلی طلاق کو مقدر مانا جائے گا، تو گو یا شوہر نے یوں کہا کہ میں نے تجھے طلاق دی تو دوسرے شوہر کے لیے رحم پاک کر ، اور جب طلاق کو مقدر مانا جو صریح لفظ ہے تو طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی اور اگر عورت غیر مدخول بھا ہے تو استبرائی سے مجاز اطلاق مراد لیں گے ، تو یہاں بھی طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ مصنف سے " اعتدی“ سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہونے پر دلیل میں ایک حدیث لائے کہ ایک مرتبہ
حضور صلی این ایام حضرت سودہ رضی اللہ عنہا کے پاس آئے ، تو آپ نے دیکھا کہ وہ جنگ بدر میں قتل ہونے والے بعض اپنے رشتہ داروں پر رو رہی ہیں، اور مشرکین مکہ کے اشعار سے مرثیہ پڑھ رہی ہیں ، آپ سالی می بینم کو ناگوار گذرا، تو آپ سالی شما اینم نے اعتدی سے طلاق دی ، حضرت سودہ رضی اللہ عنہا کو ندامت ہوئی اور درخواست کی کہ یا رسول اللہ! مجھے اپنے نکاح میں باقی رکھیں، میں اپنی باری حضرت عائشہ رضی اللہ عنہا کو دے رہی ہوں، میری تو ایک ہی آرزو ہے کہ قیامت کے دن مجھے آپ مالی یا یہ تم کی بیوی ہونے کا شرف ملے۔ تو آپ صلی ہم نے رجوع کر لیا ، معلوم ہوا کہ اعتدی سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے۔ حدیث اعتدی“ کے بعد ذکر کرنی چاہیے مگر مصنف اللہ نے "استبرئی “ کے بعد ذکر کی ،شاید اس میں یہ بات ملحوظ ہو کہ استبر کی اور اعتدی دونوں کا حکم یکساں ہے، یہ بتانے کے لئے استبرئی کے بعد لائے۔ واللہ اعلم تیسرا کنائی لفظ ”انت واحدة “ ہے، اس میں کئی احتمال ہیں ، تو یکتا ہے حسن و جمال میں ، تو یکتا ہے دینداری میں، یا تو ایک طلاق والی ہے، لہذا ابہام پیدا ہو گیا، اب نیت کی ضرورت پڑے گی ، جب شوہر نے نیت سے معنی واضح کر دئے کہ میری مراد تو ایک طلاق والی ہے، تو ابہام دور ہو گیا، تو یہاں طلاق مضمر اور پوشیدہ ہے اور صریح ہے، لہذا طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی، جیسے شوہر صریح لفظ ذکر کرتا تو بھی طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ، تو اسی طرح جب طلاق مضمر ہے تو بھی طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ لاعاملا بموجبہ سے کہہ رہے ہیں کہ یہ واحدۃ کا لفظ صریح طلاق پر دلیل بن گیا نہ یہ کہ یہ لفظ اپنے تم مقتضی اور موجب پر عامل ہے، کیونکہ اس کا مقتضی تو تو حد ہے، جس کا طلاق سے کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے۔ ثُمَّ الْأَصْلُ فِي الْكَلَامِ هُوَ الصَّرِيحُ فَاَمَّا الْكِنَايَةُ فَفِيْهَا ضَرْبُ قُصُورٍ مِنْ حَيْثُ أَنَّهُ يَقْصُرُ عَنِ الْبَيَانِ بِدُونِ النّيَةِ وَظَهَرَ هَذَا التَّفَاوُتُ فِيْمَا يُدْرَأُ بِالشُّبُهَاتِ حَتَّى أَنَّ الْمُقِرَ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِبَعْضِ الْأَسْبَابِ الْمُوْجِبَةِ لِلْعَقُوْبَةِ مَا لَمْ يَذْكُرِ اللَّفْظَ الصَّرِيحَ لَا يَسْتَوْجِبُ الْعُقُوبَةَ
ترجمہ :۔ پھر اصل کلام میں وہ صریح ہے ، پس بہر حال کنا یہ تو اس میں ایک طرح کا قصور ہے، اس حیثیت سے کہ وہ قاصر ہے بیان سے بغیر نیت کے، اور یہ تفاوت ظاہر ہو گا ان احکام میں جو شبہات سے دور ہو جاتے ہیں، یہاں تک کہ وہ شخص جو اقرار کرنے والا ہوا اپنی ذات پر ان بعض اسباب کا جو سزا کو واجب کرتے ہیں تو جب تک وہ نہیں ذکر کرے گا صریح لفظ ، وہ سزا کا مستحق نہیں ہوگا۔
Al-Faiz al-Hijazi, Urdu commentary of al-Muntakhab al-Husami. [It] is not correct from both sides, rather from one side metaphor is correct: saying the cause intending the effect is correct, but saying the effect intending the cause is not correct. So here how did you say the effect intending the cause? The answer is that that matter was in general circumstances, but if the effect is specific to the cause, then it is a metaphor. And here the effect, i.e., observing the waiting period ('iddah), is specific to the cause, i.e., divorce; without divorce, observing 'iddah does not occur. Therefore the metaphor is correct, like 'Indeed I see myself pressing wine' (Quran 12:36); here 'wine' means grapes, grapes are the cause and wine is the effect. Here also the effect is said intending the cause because the effect is specific to the cause: wine is not made without grapes. And similarly his saying 'Istabri'i rahimaki' (purify your womb), and indeed the Sunnah has brought it that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said to Sawda رضي الله عنها: 'I'taddi' (observe your 'iddah) then he took her back. And similarly 'Anti wahidah' (you are one): this word can be a description of the divorce and can be a description of the woman. So when the ambiguity is removed by intention, it becomes an indication of explicit (sarih) divorce, not acting according to its own implication. Translation: And similarly the husband's saying 'Istabri'i rahimaki', and indeed it is established regarding it that a hadith has come that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said to Sawda رضي الله عنها: 'I'taddi', then the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم took her back. And similarly 'Anti wahidah' is such that this word can be a description of the divorce and can be a description of the woman. So when the ambiguity is removed by intention, this word becomes a proof of explicit divorce, not an actor according to its own requirement. Explanation: The second kinayah word by which revocable divorce occurs is 'Istabri'i rahimaki'. Its meaning is: purify your womb. In it there are two possibilities: (1) purify your womb for a child, i.e., purify from menstruation so that I may have intercourse and hope for a child, (2) purify your womb for another husband. So ambiguity arises; now intention is needed. When the husband intends that purifying the womb for another husband is my intention, then here also the same two cases will apply: whether the wife is dukhool or not, exactly the same discussion as in 'I'taddi'. If the wife is dukhool (consummated), then from 'Istabri'i' the first divorce is considered implied, so as if the husband said: I divorce you, so purify your womb for another husband. And when the implied divorce is taken as an explicit word, then revocable divorce occurs. If the wife is non-consummated, then from 'Istabri'i' a metaphorical application of divorce is intended, so here also revocable divorce occurs. The author brought a hadith as proof that revocable divorce occurs from 'I'taddi': Once the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم came to Sawda رضي الله عنها and saw her crying over some of her relatives killed in Badr and reciting elegies from the poems of the polytheists of Makkah. The Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم found it disagreeable, so he divorced her with 'I'taddi'. Sawda رضي الله عنها felt regret and asked: O Prophet of Allah! Keep me in your marriage; I give my turn to Aisha رضي الله عنها. My only wish is to have the honor of being your wife on the Day of Judgment. So the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم took her back. Thus it is known that revocable divorce occurs from 'I'taddi'. The hadith should have been mentioned after 'I'taddi', but the author mentioned it after 'Istabri'i'. Perhaps his consideration was that the ruling of both 'Istabri'i' and 'I'taddi' is the same, so he brought it after 'Istabri'i' to indicate this. And Allah knows best. The third kinayah word is 'Anti wahidah'. In it there are several possibilities: you are unique in beauty, unique in religiosity, or you are one who has one divorce. So ambiguity arises; now intention is needed. When the husband clarifies the meaning by intention that his intention is 'one divorce', the ambiguity is removed. So here the divorce is implicit and hidden, and it is explicit, therefore revocable divorce occurs. Just as if the husband had mentioned an explicit word, revocable divorce would have occurred; similarly when the divorce is implicit, revocable divorce occurs. By 'lā ʿāmilan bi-mūjabihi' he means that the word 'wahidah' became a proof of explicit divorce, not that this word acts according to its own demand and requirement, because its own demand is 'singularity', which has no relation to divorce. Then the original in speech is the explicit (ṣarīḥ). As for kināyah, in it there is a kind of deficiency in that it fails to clarify without intention. And this difference is evident in matters that are repelled by doubts, such that a person who confesses against himself regarding some causes that necessitate punishment, as long as he does not mention the explicit word, he does not deserve punishment.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of kināyah (allusive) words in the context of ṭalāq (divorce) according to the Ḥanafī school. The author first addresses a potential objection regarding metaphorical usage (istiʿārah): normally, using the effect (musabbab) to refer to the cause (sabab) is invalid, but here in the phrase 'istabriʿī raḥimak' (purify your womb), the effect (ʿiddah) is used to mean the cause (ṭalāq). The author explains this is permissible because the effect is specific to the cause—ʿiddah only occurs after ṭalāq. This is similar to the Qur'anic example 'innī arānī aʿṣiru khamran' (I see myself pressing wine) where wine (effect) refers to grapes (cause), since wine cannot exist without grapes. Thus, when the effect is exclusive to the cause, such metaphorical usage is valid.

The main discussion then covers three kināyah words for divorce:
1. 'Iʿtaddī' (observe your ʿiddah) – This is explicitly a kināyah. The author cites a ḥadīth where the Prophet ﷺ said this to Sawdah (ra) and later returned to her (rajʿah), proving that this word effects a revocable (rajʿī) divorce.
2. 'Istabriʿī raḥimak' (purify your womb) – Similar to iʿtaddī, this word has two possibilities: purifying for the current husband (intercourse) or purifying for a future husband. With intention (niyyah) of divorce, it becomes effective. The ruling is the same: if the wife has been consummated, an implicit divorce is understood; if not consummated, the word is used metaphorically for divorce. In both cases, the resulting ṭalāq is rajʿī.
3. 'Anti wāḥidah' (you are one) – This word can mean unique in beauty, religion, or one divorced. With intention of divorce, it becomes a proof (dalālah) of explicit divorce, not acting according to its own literal meaning (which is singularity). Hence, it also results in rajʿī ṭalāq.

The passage concludes with a fundamental uṣūl principle: the default (aṣl) in speech is ṣarīḥ (explicit). Kināyah has a deficiency in that it requires intention to clarify meaning. This difference is significant in matters where punishments (ʿuqūbāt) are repelled by doubts (shubuhāt). For example, a person who confesses to an act that necessitates punishment does not incur the punishment unless he uses an explicit word (ṣarīḥ); with kināyah, punishment is not imposed. This uṣūl rule (dafʿ al-ḥudūd bi al-shubuhāt) is frequently applied by the Ḥanafīs.

Key Terms
الكناية (al-kināyah): A word or phrase that is not explicit (ṣarīḥ) but alludes to a meaning, requiring intention (niyyah) for legal effect in contexts like ṭalāq.
الصريح (al-ṣarīḥ): An explicit word that clearly indicates its meaning without need for intention; in ṭalāq, words like 'ṭāliq' (divorced) are ṣarīḥ.
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the end of marriage, during which she cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ḥanafī school, what type of ṭalāq results from a husband saying 'Anti wāḥidah' (you are one) with the intention of divorce?
A) A single irrevocable (bāʾin) ṭalāq.
B) A single revocable (rajʿī) ṭalāq.
C) No ṭalāq at all because it is not explicit.
D) Three ṭalāq at once.

Key Takeaway
Kināyah words for ṭalāq require intention; with intention, they effect a revocable (rajʿī) ṭalāq in the Hanafī school.

Sarih and Kinayah in Hanafi Usul
Explains the usul principle of sarih vs kinayah with divorce example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) words, and apply the Hanafi rule that sarih is effective without intention while kinayah requires intention.

Core Idea
The explicit word speaks; the implicit word hints—only with intention does it speak.

In Hanafi legal theory, words are not equal. Some words are so clear they need no interpretation, while others are ambiguous and require the speaker's intention to specify meaning. This lesson unpacks the distinction between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) using the example of divorce from the Prophet's own practice. Understanding this distinction is foundational for deriving rulings from texts, as it affects the binding force of statements in matters like divorce, confession, and hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Name the two textual categories being defined: sarih and kinayah.
2
Grasp the defining feature
Understand that sarih is clear by conventional usage; kinayah requires intention or context to clarify meaning.
3
Apply to the divorce example
Analyze the hadith of Sawda where the Prophet used 'i'tiddi' (a kinayah word) and later returned to her, showing that a kinayah divorce is effective with intention.
4
Examine the 'anti wahidah' example
See how multiple possible meanings create ambiguity, and intention resolves it, making the divorce implicit yet effective.
5
Understand the legal consequence
In matters averted by doubt (hudud, kaffarat), only sarih suffices because kinayah carries inherent ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حضور صلی الله علیه وآله وسلم حضرت سودہ رضی اللہ عنہا کے پاس آئے، تو آپ نے دیکھا کہ وہ جنگ بدر میں قتل ہونے والے بعض اپنے رشتہ داروں پر رو رہی ہیں، اور مشرکین مکہ کے اشعار سے مرثیہ پڑھ رہی ہیں، آپ صلی الله علیه وآله وسلم کو ناگوار گذرا، تو آپ صلی الله علیه وآله وسلم نے اعتدی سے طلاق دی، حضرت سودہ رضی اللہ عنہا کو ندامت ہوئی اور درخواست کی کہ یا رسول اللہ! مجھے اپنے نکاح میں باقی رکھیں، میں اپنی باری حضرت عائشہ رضی اللہ عنہا کو دے رہی ہوں، میری تو ایک ہی آرزو ہے کہ قیامت کے دن مجھے آپ صلی الله علیه وآله وسلم کی بیوی ہونے کا شرف ملے۔ تو آپ صلی الله علیه وآله وسلم نے رجوع کر لیا، معلوم ہوا کہ اعتدی سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے۔ حدیث "اعتدی" کے بعد ذکر کرنی چاہیے مگر مصنف نے "استبرئی" کے بعد ذکر کیا، شاید اس میں یہ بات ملحوظ ہو کہ استبرا اور اعتدی دونوں کا حکم یکساں ہے، یہ بتانے کے لئے استبرئی کے بعد لائے۔ واللہ اعلم تیسرا کنائی لفظ "انت واحدة" ہے، اس میں کئی احتمال ہیں، تو یکتا ہے حسن و جمال میں، تو یکتا ہے دینداری میں، یا تو ایک طلاق والی ہے، لہذا ابہام پیدا ہو گیا، اب نیت کی ضرورت پڑے گی، جب شوہر نے نیت سے معنی واضح کر دئے کہ میری مراد تو ایک طلاق والی ہے، تو ابہام دور ہو گیا، تو یہاں طلاق مضمر اور پوشیدہ ہے اور صریح ہے، لہذا طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی، جیسے شوہر صریح لفظ ذکر کرتا تو بھی طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی، تو اسی طرح جب طلاق مضمر ہے تو بھی طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ لا عمل بموجبه سے کہہ رہے ہیں کہ یہ واحدۃ کا لفظ صریح طلاق پر دلیل بن گیا نہ یہ کہ یہ لفظ اپنے تم مقتضی اور موجب پر عامل ہے، کیونکہ اس کا مقتضی تو توحد ہے، جس کا طلاق سے کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے۔ ثُمَّ الْأَصْلُ فِي الْكَلَامِ هُوَ الصَّرِيحُ فَاَمَّا الْكِنَايَةُ فَفِيْهَا ضَرْبُ قُصُورٍ مِنْ حَيْثُ أَنَّهُ يَقْصُرُ عَنِ الْبَيَانِ بِدُونِ النّيَةِ وَظَهَرَ هَذَا التَّفَاوُتُ فِيْمَا يُدْرَأُ بِالشُّبُهَاتِ حَتَّى أَنَّ الْمُقِرَ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِبَعْضِ الْأَسْبَابِ الْمُوْجِبَةِ لِلْعَقُوْبَةِ مَا لَمْ يَذْكُرِ اللَّفْظَ الصَّرِيحَ لَا يَسْتَوْجِبُ الْعُقُوبَةَ
تشریح:- مصنف سے کہتے ہیں کہ صریح و کنایہ میں اصل صریح ہے، کنایہ اصل کے خلاف ہے، کیونکہ کلام سے مقصود سمجھنا سمجھانا ہوتا ہے، اور صریح میں یہ بات کامل طریقہ پر ہوتی ہے، کیونکہ اس میں نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہوتی ہے، اور کنایہ کلام کو سمجھنے سمجھانے میں کامل نہیں ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ اس میں نیت کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے، بغیر نیت یا قائم مقام نیت (دلالت حال وغیرہ) کے کنایہ اپنے معنی کو واضح نہیں کر سکتا ہے، تو گویا اس میں ایک گونہ قصور ہوتا ہے۔ یہ فرق صریح اور کنایہ کا کہ صریح کی مراد واضح ہوتی ہے اور کنایہ کی مراد پوشیدہ ہوتی ہے۔ ان احکام میں ظاہر ہوگا جو شبہات سے ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں، جیسے حدود و کفارات، تو یہ احکام صریح سے ثابت ہو جا ئینگے اور کنایہ سے ثابت نہ ہوں گے، کیونکہ کنایہ میں خود ایک طرح کا شبہ موجود ہے، اس میں مختلف احتمالات ہونے کی وجہ سے، لہذا اگر کوئی شخص حدود و کفارات میں صریح لفظ کے بجائے کنایہ لفظ سے اپنے اوپر کوئی اقرار کرے جیسے کہے کہ میں نے فلاں عورت سے جماع کیا یا اس سے ناجائز صحبت کی وغیرہ تو چونکہ یہ لفظ زنا پر صریح نہیں ہے، لہذا اس سے زنا کا ثبوت نہ ہوگا، جب تک صریح لفظ نہ کہے جیسے کہے کہ میں نے زنا کیا۔
The Prophet (peace be upon him) came to Sawda (may Allah be pleased with her) and saw her crying over some relatives killed in Badr and reciting elegies from the poems of the Makkan polytheists. He disapproved and divorced her with 'i'tiddi'. She regretted and requested to remain in his marriage, giving her turn to Aisha. He returned to her, showing that divorce with 'i'tiddi' is raj'i. The hadith should have been mentioned after 'i'tiddi', but the author mentioned it after 'istabri'i', perhaps because both have the same ruling. ... Third kinayah word: 'anti wahidah' has multiple possibilities: unique in beauty, in piety, or one divorce. Thus ambiguity arises, requiring intention. When the husband clarifies by intention that he meant one divorce, the ambiguity is removed. Thus divorce is implicit and explicit, so raj'i divorce occurs... Then the principle: the origin in speech is the explicit (sarih). As for kinayah, it has a kind of deficiency because it falls short of clarity without intention. This difference appears in matters that are warded off by doubts, such that a person who confesses to causes of punishment, if he does not use the explicit word, he does not incur punishment.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition and illustration. It begins with a hadith showing that the Prophet (peace be upon him) divorced Sawda using the word 'i'tiddi' (meaning 'stay away'), which is kinayah, and then returned to her, proving that such a divorce is raj'i. The author then discusses the kinayah word 'anti wahidah' (you are one), which has multiple possible meanings and thus requires intention. The core usul principle is then stated in Arabic: 'The origin in speech is the explicit (sarih). As for kinayah, it has a deficiency because it cannot clarify without intention or contextual indication.' This difference is manifested in matters that are averted by doubts, such as hudud and kaffarat. For example, if someone confesses to a crime using kinayah (like 'I had intercourse with her' instead of 'I committed zina'), the punishment is not established because kinayah carries doubt. The commentary expands on this, explaining that sarih is the norm, while kinayah is deficient because it requires intention to be clear. Thus, in legal matters that require certainty, only explicit language suffices.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): A clear, unambiguous word that directly indicates its intended meaning without need for intention or context, e.g., 'taliq' (you are divorced).
كناية (kinayah): An implicit word that does not directly indicate its intended meaning; it carries multiple possibilities and requires intention or context to specify the meaning, e.g., 'anti wahidah' (you are one).

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, why does a confession using kinayah (e.g., 'I had intercourse with her') not establish the hadd penalty for zina?
A) Kinayah is not a valid form of confession in Islamic law.
B) Kinayah carries doubt due to multiple possible meanings, and hudud are averted by doubts.
C) The hadd for zina requires four witnesses, not confession.
D) Kinayah words are only valid in divorce, not in criminal matters.

Key Takeaway
Sarih words speak for themselves; kinayah words require intention to speak.
