Equality and Conflict of 'Ibaarah and Ishaarah
Explains the equality and conflict resolution of 'ibaarah and ishaarah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between 'ibaarah al-nass and ishaarah al-nass and apply the Hanafi rule that 'ibaarah takes precedence in case of conflict.

Core Idea
Both explicit and implied meanings are definitive, but explicit meaning wins in conflict.

In Hanafi usul, the textual indications of the Quran and Sunnah are categorized by how they convey meaning. This lesson examines two types: 'ibaarah (explicit intent) and ishaarah (implied meaning). While both are considered qat'i (definitive) in establishing rulings, they can sometimes conflict. The Hanafi school has a clear rule for such situations, which we will explore through the example of the maximum duration of menstruation.

Guided Path
1
Define 'ibaarah and ishaarah
Understand the distinction: 'ibaarah is the literal intended meaning; ishaarah is a secondary implied meaning that is still definitive.
Both are qat'i but differ in directness.
2
Recognize their equal binding force
In Hanafi usul, both make a ruling wajib (obligatory) – they are equally authoritative in establishing a hukm.
No hierarchy in authority; both demand compliance.
3
Identify the conflict scenario
A conflict occurs when the explicit meaning of one text contradicts the implied meaning of another. The passage gives the example of menstruation maximum duration.
One hadith explicitly says 10 days; another implies 15 days.
4
Apply the precedence rule
When 'ibaarah and ishaarah conflict, the explicit meaning ('ibaarah) is given precedence (tarjih). This is a settled Hanafi principle.
The implied meaning is set aside in that specific case.
5
See the practical outcome
In the example, the explicit hadith (10 days) overrules the implied 15 days, so the Hanafi ruling is that the maximum menstruation is 10 days.
The Shafi'i school follows the ishaarah, leading to a difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پھر فرماتے ہیں کہ عبارة النص اور اشارۃ النص دونوں حکم کو واجب کرنے میں برابر ہیں ، یعنی دونوں قطعی ہیں البتہ اگر ان دونوں میں تعارض ہو جائے تو عبارۃ النص کو ترجیح حاصل ہوگی۔ تعارض کی مثال :- جیسے حدیث میں عورتوں کو ناقصات عقل و دین کہا گیا ہے، کہ ان کی عقل بھی آدھی اور دین بھی آدھا۔ پوچھا گیا کہ عقل اور دین آدھا کیوں؟ تو وجہ بتائی کہ عقل تو آدھی اس لئے ہے کہ دوعورتوں کی گواہی ایک مرد کے برابر ہے۔۔ اور آدھا دین اس لئے ہے کہ آدھی عمر میں وہ بیٹھی رہتی ہیں نماز روزہ نہیں کرتی ہیں تو حدیث کو چلانے کا مقصد تو عورتوں کا دین و عقل میں ناقص ہونا بتلانا ہے، لہذا یہ بات تو عبارۃ النص سے معلوم ہوئی۔ مگر اس حدیث کے اشارۃ النص سے معلوم ہوا کہ اکثر مدت حیض پندرہ دن ہے جیسا کہ امام شافعی کا مذہب ہے۔ کیونکہ آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا آدھی عمر بیٹھی رہتی ہیں ، حیض کی وجہ سے نماز ، روزہ نہیں کرتی ہیں ، اور زندگی کا حساب سال سے ہوتا ہے، تو آدھا سال حیض کا زمانہ ہوگا، اور سال کا حساب مہینوں سے ہوتا ہے تو آدھا مہینہ یعنی پندرہ دن حیض کا زمانہ ہوگا، تو اس طرح اشارة النص سے حیض کی اکثر مدت پندرہ دن ثابت ہوئی۔ اور ایک دوسری حدیث ہے کہ حیض کی اقل مدت تین دن ہیں، اور اکثر مدت دس دن ہیں ، اس حدیث سے حیض کی اکثر مدت دس دن ہونا عبارة النص سے معلوم ہوا، کیونکہ یہ حدیث یہ حکم بتانے کے لئے لائی گئی ہے۔ لہذا عبارة النص اور پہلی حدیث کے اشارۃ النص میں تعارض ہو گیا تو عبارۃ النص کو ترجیح ہوگی اور حیض کی اکثر مدت دس دن ہوگی۔
Then he says: 'Ibaarah al-nass and ishaarah al-nass are equal in making the ruling obligatory, meaning both are definitive. However, if they conflict, precedence is given to 'ibaarah al-nass. Example of conflict: In the hadith, women are called deficient in intellect and religion, that their intellect is half and their religion is half. It was asked why intellect and religion are half? The reason was given that intellect is half because the testimony of two women equals one man; and religion is half because half of their life they sit (during menstruation) not praying or fasting. So the intent of the hadith is to show women's deficiency in religion and intellect; this is known from 'ibaarah al-nass. But from the ishaarah of this hadith, it is understood that the maximum duration of menstruation is fifteen days, as is the Shafi'i school. Because the Prophet ﷺ said they sit half their life, i.e., due to menstruation they do not pray or fast; and life is measured by years, so half a year would be the time of menstruation; and a year is measured by months, so half a month means fifteen days. Thus from ishaarah al-nass, the maximum period of menstruation is established as fifteen days. And there is another hadith that the minimum period of menstruation is three days and the maximum is ten days; from this hadith, the maximum of ten days is known by 'ibaarah al-nass, because this hadith is brought to convey this ruling. So when 'ibaarah al-nass and the ishaarah of the first hadith conflict, 'ibaarah al-nass is given precedence, and the maximum period of menstruation is ten days.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between two types of textual indication: 'ibaarah al-nass (explicit meaning) and ishaarah al-nass (alluded meaning). Both are considered definitive (qat'i) in establishing rulings, but when they conflict, the explicit meaning takes priority. The example given is from two ahadith about menstruation: one hadith states women are deficient in intellect and religion, from which ishaarah suggests the maximum menstruation is fifteen days (since half a year of missed prayers). Another hadith explicitly says the maximum is ten days. Since the explicit text ('ibaarah) is given precedence, the ruling is ten days. This principle is fundamental in Hanafi usul: the explicit wording of a text is preferred over inferred meanings in cases of contradiction.

Key Terms
عبارة النص (‘ibaarah al-nass): The explicit, literal meaning of the text that the text was intended to convey.
إشارة النص (ishārah al-nass): A meaning implied by the text but not the primary intent, understood through contextual clues.
تعارض (ta'aarud): Conflict or contradiction between two proofs or indications.
ترجیح (tarjīḥ): Giving precedence to one proof over another due to a stronger indicator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this lesson, what is the Hanafi ruling on the maximum duration of menstruation?
A) Three days
B) Ten days
C) Fifteen days
D) No fixed maximum

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, both 'ibaarah and ishaarah are definitive, but in case of conflict, the explicit meaning ('ibaarah) prevails.

Defining Dalaalah al-Nass: Necessary Linguistic Implication
Defines dalaalah al-nass and compares it to ishaarah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dalaalah al-nass, explain how it differs from 'ibaarah and ishaarah, and apply it to derive a ruling from a Quranic text.

Core Idea
Dalaalah al-nass is the immediate necessary implication of a text, not requiring ijtihaad.

This lesson introduces dalaalah al-nass, one of the primary methods of extracting rulings from the Quran and Sunnah. Unlike 'ibaarah al-nass (literal meaning) and ishaarah al-nass (alluded meaning), dalaalah is a necessary consequence understood by any native Arabic speaker. We will see how this principle operates through the famous example of the verse prohibiting saying 'uff' to parents.

Guided Path
1
State the definition
Dalaalah al-nass is the ruling established by the necessary linguistic meaning of the text, not by deduction through opinion.
It is immediate, without reflection or ijtihaad.
2
Distinguish from 'ibaarah
'Ibaarah gives the literal meaning the text directly states; dalaalah gives the logical consequence that any native speaker understands.
Both are qat'i, but 'ibaarah is the primary meaning.
3
Apply the example
From 'Do not say to them uff' (Quran 17:23), the literal prohibition is saying 'uff'. The necessary implication is that all forms of harm, like hitting, are prohibited.
This is derived by dalaalah, not qiyas.
4
Compare with ishaarah
Both dalaalah and ishaarah are qat'i and can establish hudud and kaffarah. However, in case of conflict, ishaarah is given precedence.
The passage states 'except in conflict, it is lower than ishaarah'.
5
Identify the authority
The ruling from dalaalah is definitive (qat'i) and can establish even severe punishments like hudud.
This is specific to the Hanafi school.

الْإِشَارَةِ . ترجمہ : اور بہر حال دلالتہ النص پس وہ ہے جو ثابت ہو نص کے معنی سے لغتہ نہ کہ رائے سے استنباط کے طریقہ پر، جیسے اُف کہنے سے روکنا، مطلع ہوا جاتا ہے اس سے ضرب کی حرمت پر تامل اور اجتہاد کے واسطہ کے بغیر، اور وہ حکم جو دلالۃ النص سے ثابت ہے وہ اس حکم کی طرح ہے جو اشارۃ النص سے ثابت ہے، یہاں تک کہ صحیح ہے حدود اور کفارات کو ثابت کرنا دلالات النصوص سے، مگر تعارض کے وقت (دلالۃ النص) اشارة النص سے کم درجہ ہے۔ تشریح :- دلالت النص اس کو کہتے ہیں کہ کوئی حکم نص کے معنی سے لغتہ ثابت ہو، یعنی نص کے معنی سے مراد اس کا لغوی اور موضوع لہ معنی نہیں ہے، بلکہ اس کا لازمی معنی مراد ہے، کیونکہ اگر اس کا موضوع لہ معنی مراد لینگے تو یہ عبارۃ النص ہو جائے گا۔ بہر حال جو حکم نص کے معنی لازمی سے ثابت ہو لغتہ یعنی لغت عرب کا جاننے والا یہ لازمی معنی اس اصل معنی کے ساتھ فوراً سمجھ لے اس میں تامل اور استنباط کی ضرورت نہ پڑے تو اس لازمی معنی کو جو لغۃ مفہوم ہورہا ہے دلالت النص کہیں گے۔ مثال سے سمجھو، اللہ تعالی کا ارشاد ہے لَا تَقُلْ لَهُمَا أُفٍّ، ماں باپ کو اف نہ کہو تو اس کا موضوع لہ معنی تو ہے اف نہ کہنا، یہ تو ثابت ہوا عبارۃ النص سے اور اف نہ کہو اس کلام سے ہر لغت کا جاننے والا سمجھ جائے گا کہ مقصود تکلیف نہ دینا ہے، لہذا تکلیف نہ دینا یہ اف نہ کہنے کا لازمی معنی ہے، اب اس لازمی معنی میں جتنی صورتیں ہوں گی سب داخل ہو جائیں گی، جیسے مارنا، گالی دینا وغیرہ تو یہ مارنا گالی دینا وغیرہ کی حرمت دلالت النص سے ثابت ہوئی، اور اف نہ کہنے کی حرمت عبارة النص سے ثابت ہوئی۔ مصنف اللہ کہتے ہیں کہ دلالت النص بھی اشارۃ النص کی طرح قطعی ہے، اسی لئے دلالۃ النص سے حدود و کفارات ثابت ہو جاتے ہیں۔ حدود کی مثال :- حضرت ماعز اسلمی رضی اللہ عنہ کا قصہ کہ وہ آئے اور کہنے لگے یا رسول اللہ مجھ سے زنا ہو گیا، آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے رخ پھیر لیا، پھر وہ دوسری طرف سے آئے، آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے رخ پھیر لیا، غرض چار مرتبہ آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے رخ پھیر لیا، چاروں مرتبہ وہ یہی کہتے رہے، چوتھی مرتبہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے ان کے رجم کا فیصلہ کیا تو اس حدیث سے حضرت ماعز رضی اللہ عنہ کا رجم تو عبارۃ النص سے ثابت ہوا البتہ دلالت النص سے یہ بات معلوم ہوگی کہ جو حضرت ماعز کی طرح ہوگا، اس کو بھی رجم کیا جائے گا، حضرت ماعز پر رجم اس لئے ہوا کہ وہ محصن (شادی شدہ) تھے اور زنا کیا لہذا جو شادی شدہ زنا کرے گا تو رجم ثابت ہوگا۔ دوسروں کے لئے رجم دلالت النص سے ثابت ہوا۔ کفارہ کی مثال :- حضرت ابو ہریرہ رضی اللہ عنہ کہتے ہیں کہ ہم حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے پاس بیٹھے ہوئے تھے، ایک آدمی آیا اس نے کہا یا رسول اللہ ہلاک ہو گیا، آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے پوچھا کیا ہوا؟ اس نے کہا روزہ کی حالت میں بیوی سے جماع کیا، حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا غلام آزاد کر سکتا ہے، اس نے کہا نہیں، پھر دو مہینہ پے در پے روزے رکھ سکتا ہے۔
And as for dalaalah al-nass, it is that which is established by the linguistic meaning of the text, not by deduction through opinion, like the prohibition of saying 'uff' informs the prohibition of hitting without the mediation of reflection and ijtihaad. And that which is established by dalaalah al-nass is like that established by ishaarah, so much so that it is valid to establish hadd punishments and kaffaraat by dalaalaat al-nusoos, except in case of conflict, where it is lower than ishaarah. Explanation: Dalaalah al-nass is when a ruling is established from the meaning of the text linguistically, i.e., not the literal meaning but the necessary meaning. For example, Allah says 'Do not say to them uff'. The literal meaning is 'do not say uff', established by 'ibaarah. But every Arabic speaker immediately understands that the intent is not to cause them discomfort; therefore, causing discomfort is the necessary meaning. All forms of causing discomfort, like hitting, cursing, etc., are prohibited by dalaalah al-nass. The author says that dalaalah al-nass is also qat'i like ishaarah, so hadd and kaffarah can be established by it. Example of hadd: The story of Ma'iz al-Aslami (RA) who came and said he committed zina. The Prophet (PBUH) turned away, and after four times, he ordered his stoning. His stoning was established by 'ibaarah al-nass, but by dalaalah al-nass it is understood that anyone like Ma'iz (i.e., muhsin) who commits zina is also stoned. Example of kaffarah: The hadith of Abu Hurairah (RA) about a man who had intercourse with his wife while fasting; the Prophet (PBUH) asked if he could free a slave, and when he said no, ordered two consecutive months of fasting.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines dalaalah al-nass as the necessary linguistic implication of a text—something any native speaker understands without reflection. Unlike 'ibaarah (explicit literal meaning) and ishaarah (alluded meaning), dalaalah is a logical consequence. The example of 'Uff' (Quran 17:23) illustrates: the explicit command is not to say 'uff', but the necessary implication is not to harm parents in any way. This ruling is derived by dalaalah. The text asserts that dalaalah is equal in authority to ishaarah: both are qat'i and can establish even severe rulings like hudud and kaffarah. However, in case of conflict, dalaalah is considered weaker than ishaarah. The examples from Ma'iz and the fasting man show how dalaalah extends the ruling to similar cases. This principle is crucial in Hanafi usul for deriving rulings beyond the literal text without resorting to qiyas. The authority weight here is from the commentary tradition (sharh), but the underlying matn likely from al-Husami or similar work; the rules themselves are from the established Hanafi usul corpus.

Key Terms
دلالة النص (dalālah al-nass): A ruling established from the necessary linguistic meaning of the text, understood immediately by native speakers.
إشارة النص (ishārah al-nass): A meaning that is alluded to by the text but not its primary intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Ishaarah is stronger than dalaalah in case of conflict.
C) Both are equally strong in all cases.
D) Neither is used for hudud.

Key Takeaway
Dalaalah al-nass is a definitive linguistic implication that allows derivation of rulings without ijtihaad.

Definition and Authority of Dalalat al-Nass
Defining dalaalat al-nass as necessary textual implication with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dalaalat al-nass, distinguish it from 'ibaraat al-nass, and apply it to derive rulings from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Dalalat al-nass derives a binding ruling from necessary linguistic implication, understood immediately without reasoning.

In this lesson, we explore dalaalat al-nass, one of the four types of textual indication in Hanafi usul. Unlike literal meaning ('ibaraat al-nass), dalaalat al-nass extracts a ruling from what the text necessarily implies. We will see how this definitive proof operates in the Quran and hadith, and why it differs from the Shafi'i approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify key components of dalaalat al-nass
Break down the definition: a ruling derived from the necessary (lazimi) meaning of a text, understood immediately by a native Arabic speaker without reflection or deduction.
Necessary meaning is distinct from literal meaning.
2
Distinguish dalaalat al-nass from 'ibaraat al-nass
Contrast the two modes using the Quranic verse 'Do not say uff': the literal prohibition is 'ibaraat al-nass; the necessary implication (prohibition of all harm) is dalaalat al-nass.
3
Analyze the linguistic necessity
Determine why the prohibition of saying 'uff' necessarily implies prohibition of all forms of disrespect. Focus on the immediate understanding of an Arabic speaker: if even the slightest expression of annoyance is forbidden, then stronger forms are clearly included.
This necessity is linguistic, not logical deduction.
4
Evaluate the evidentiary value
Assess why dalaalat al-nass is considered qat'i (definitive) in Hanafi usul, similar to ishaarat al-nass, and how it thus establishes hadd and kaffarah. Compare with the Shafi'i view that such implications are zanni.
This difference has practical consequences for establishing severe penalties.
5
Apply to a hadith
Apply the same logic to the hadith of the Bedouin who broke his fast: the ruling of expiation for him individually is 'ibaraat al-nass, but by dalaalat al-nass it applies to anyone who deliberately breaks a Ramadan fast.
The contextual elements (e.g., the man's poverty) do not affect the implication.

أَفْقَرَ مِنِّي يَارَسُوْلَ اللهِ ؟ یا رسول اللہ میرے سے زیادہ محتاج پر؟ وَاللَّهِ مَا بَيْنَ لَابَتَيْهَا أَهْلُ بَيْتٍ أَفْقَرَ مِنْ أَهْلِ بَيْتِی -- مدینہ کے دو پہاڑوں کے بیچ میں میرے سے زیادہ کوئی گھرانہ ضرورت مند نہیں ، فَضَحِكَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ حَتَّى بَدَتْ أَنْيَابُهُ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم اس کی بات سن کر ہنس پڑے حتی کہ آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے انیاب دانت ظاہر ہو گئے۔۔ یہ حدیث خاص اس دیہاتی کے بارے میں بیان ہوئی ہے لہذا روزه جان بوجھ کر توڑنے کی وجہ سے اس اعرابی کے لئے کفارہ کا ثبوت تو عبارت النص سے ہوا۔ مگر اس حدیث کے دلالت النص سے یہ ثابت ہوا کہ جو کوئی رمضان میں جان بوجھ کر روزہ توڑے گا اس پر کفارہ واجب ہو گا۔
Establishing expiations from the indications of texts, but at the time of contradiction, dalaalat al-nass is lower in rank than ishaarat al-nass. Explanation: Dalaalat al-nass is defined as when a ruling is established from the meaning of the text linguistically, meaning the intended meaning from the text is not its literal and original meaning, but rather its necessary meaning. If the literal meaning were intended, it would be 'ibaraat al-nass. Any ruling established from the necessary meaning of the text, which a speaker of Arabic immediately understands along with the original meaning without needing reflection or deduction, that necessary meaning which is understood linguistically is called dalaalat al-nass. Understand by example: Allah's command 'Do not say 'uff' to them.' The literal meaning is not saying 'uff'—that is established by 'ibaraat al-nass. From this speech, every Arabic speaker understands that the intent is not to cause hardship. Therefore, not causing hardship is the necessary meaning of not saying 'uff'. Now all forms included in this necessary meaning, like hitting, cursing, etc., their prohibition is established by dalaalat al-nass, while the prohibition of saying 'uff' is established by 'ibaraat al-nass. The author, may Allah have mercy on him, says that dalaalat al-nass is also definitive like ishaarat al-nass, therefore hadd punishments and expiations are established by dalaalat al-nass. Example of hadd: The story of Ma'iz al-Aslami, may Allah be pleased with him, that he came and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, I have committed zina.' The Prophet turned his face away. Then he came from another side, the Prophet turned his face away. In short, four times the Prophet turned his face away, and each time he said the same. On the fourth time, the Prophet decreed his stoning. From this hadith, the stoning of Ma'iz is established by 'ibaraat al-nass, but from dalaalat al-nass it is understood that whoever is like Ma'iz will also be stoned. Ma'iz was stoned because he was muhsan (married) and committed zina; so whoever is married and commits zina, stoning is established. For others, stoning is established by dalaalat al-nass. Example of expiation: Abu Hurairah, may Allah be pleased with him, said: We were sitting with the Prophet when a man came and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, I am destroyed.' The Prophet asked, 'What happened?' He said, 'I had intercourse with my wife while fasting.' The Prophet said, 'Can you free a slave?' He said, 'No.' 'Then can you fast two consecutive months?' He said, 'No, I am old.' 'Then can you feed sixty poor people?' He said, 'No, O Messenger of Allah, I am a very poor man.' The Prophet said, 'Sit down.' After a while, some dates etc. were brought as charity to the Prophet. So the Prophet asked, 'Where is the questioner?' He came, and the Prophet said, 'Give this as charity.' The man said, 'To someone needier than me, O Messenger of Allah? By Allah, there is no household between the two lava fields of Medina needier than my household.' The Prophet laughed until his canine teeth appeared. This hadith was narrated specifically about this Bedouin; therefore, the obligation of expiation for this Bedouin due to deliberately breaking the fast is established by 'ibaraat al-nass. But from the dalaalat al-nass of this hadith, it is established that whoever deliberately breaks the fast in Ramadan, expiation becomes obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines dalaalat al-nass, one of the four types of textual indication in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author explains that dalaalat al-nass derives legal rulings from the necessary implication (lazimi) of the text, not the literal meaning. This implication is immediately understood by a native Arabic speaker without reflection. The author then provides three examples: first, the Quranic verse 'Do not say uff to them' (17:23) — the literal ruling is prohibition of saying 'uff', but the necessary implication extends to any form of disrespect like hitting or cursing. Second, the hadith of Ma'iz establishes stoning for him by literal wording, but by dalaalat al-nass the ruling applies to any married person who commits zina. Third, the hadith of the Bedouin who broke his fast — the literal ruling applies to him, but by dalaalat al-nass it extends to anyone who deliberately breaks a Ramadan fast. The author emphasizes that dalaalat al-nass is qati (definitive) like ishaarat al-nass, so hadd punishments and kaffarat are established through it.

Key Terms
دلالت النص (dalaalat al-nass): A ruling established from the necessary (lazimi) meaning of a text, understood immediately by a native Arabic speaker.
عبارت النص ('ibaraat al-nasṣ): A ruling established from the literal, original meaning of the text.
قطعي (qaṭ‘ī): Definitive; providing certainty in both transmission and indication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal status (hukm) of dalaalat al-nass?
A) It is a speculative (zanni) proof like qiyas.
B) It is a definitive (qat'i) proof similar to ishaarat al-nass.
C) It is weaker than 'ibaraat al-nass in all cases.
D) It only applies to Quranic verses, not hadith.

Key Takeaway
Dalalat al-nass is a definitive proof that derives rulings from the necessary linguistic implication of a text, and it is distinct from both literal meaning and analogical reasoning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

6 min
Muqtada al-Nass Does Not Admit Takhsis
Stating the rule that muqtada al-nass does not admit takhsis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why muqtada al-nass does not admit takhsis in Hanafi usul and distinguish the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Muqtada al-nass has no generality, so it cannot be specified — takhsis applies only to 'amm.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rule that rulings derived from muqtada al-nass (the implied meaning necessitated by the text) cannot be specified. Unlike general expressions (amm), muqtada carries no generality to narrow down. We will explore the reasoning and the practical difference with Imam al-Shafi'i's view.

Guided Path
1
Define muqtada al-nass
Identify muqtada as the implicit meaning necessarily required by the text for the ruling to be valid. Note that it is not an explicit general term.
Muqtada is established by Sharia, not by linguistic generality.
2
Understand the principle
The Hanafi rule: muqtada has no 'umum (generality). Therefore, it cannot be specified (takhsis) because specification presupposes generality.
Takhsis is an exception to a general rule; no general rule exists here.
3
Apply to the oath example
If someone swears 'I will not drink' and intends only a specific drink, his intention does not restrict the oath. The oath applies to all drinks because the muqtada of 'drink' is any liquid consumption.
The ruling is derived from the necessity of the text, not from the swearer's intention.
4
Compare with Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that muqtada can be specified, allowing the intention to limit the oath. The Hanafi response: specification only applies to terms that have the potential for generality, which muqtada lacks.
This is a fundamental usul disagreement on the nature of implied meanings.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the oath example, Hanafi rules the oath covers all drinks; Shafi'i may restrict it to the intended drink.
The khilaf affects many similar cases involving implicit rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الثَّابِتُ بِمُقْتَضَى النَّصِّ لَا يَحْتَمِلُ التَّخْصِيْصَ حَتَّى لَوْحَلَفَ لَا يَشْرَبْ وَنَوَىٰ شَرَابَاً دُوْنَ شَرَابٍ لَا تُعْمَلُ نِيَتُهُ لِاَنَّ الْمُقْتَضَى لَا عُمُومَ لَهُ خِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِيِّ وَالتَّخْصِيْصُ فِيْمَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْعُمُوْمَ۔ ترجمہ: پھر وہ حکم جو ثابت ہوتا ہے مقتضی النص سے وہ تخصیص کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے، یہاں تک کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ وہ نہیں پئے گا اور اس نے نیت کی ایک مشروب کی نہ کہ دوسرے کی، تو اس کی نیت پر عمل نہیں کیا جائے گا، اس لئے کہ مقتضی کے لئے عموم نہیں ہے امام شافعی کے خلاف، اور تخصیص اس چیز میں ہوتی ہے جو عموم کا احتمال رکھتی ہے۔
Then the ruling established by muqtada al-nass does not admit specification (takhsis), so that if someone swears 'I will not drink' and intends a specific drink to the exclusion of another, his intention is not given effect, because muqtada has no generality, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And specification occurs only in what admits generality. Translation: Then the ruling established by muqtada al-nass does not admit specification. So if someone swears that he will not drink and intends a specific drink and not another, his intention is not acted upon, because muqtada has no generality, contrary to Imam Shafi'i. And specification applies only to that which admits generality.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents a clear Hanafi rule: the ruling derived from muqtada al-nass (the implied meaning necessitated by the text) cannot be specified (takhsis) because it has no general applicability ('umum) in the first place. The example given is an oath: a person swears 'I will not drink' and privately intends only a particular drink (e.g., milk) and not another (e.g., water). According to the Hanafis, his intention does not restrict the oath because the ruling from muqtada of the phrase 'I will not drink' is that any liquid consumption is included, and there is no general wording to specify. This is contrary to al-Shafi'i, who might allow such specification. The basic principle: specification is only applicable to general terms that already have the potential for generality. Since muqtada does not express generality, it cannot be narrowed down.

Key Terms
مقتضى النص (muqtada al-nass): The meaning that is necessarily implied by the text for the ruling to be valid; it is established by Sharia.
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification: narrowing the scope of a general term to exclude some of its members.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person swears 'I will not drink' and intends only milk, what is the ruling?
A) His intention is valid and the oath applies only to milk
B) His intention is not valid and the oath applies to all drinks
C) The oath is invalid due to the intention
D) The matter is left to the qadi's discretion

Key Takeaway
Muqtada al-nass carries no generality, so it cannot be specified — a key distinction between implicit meanings and general expressions.

Muqtada and Mahdhuf: Why Implied Meaning Has No Generality
Usul rule distinguishing muqtada and mahdhuf and the ruling on takhsis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muqtada and mahdhuf and apply the Hanafi rule that muqtada does not accept takhsis in deriving rulings from texts.

Core Idea
Muqtada is meaning, not wording; meaning has no generality, so it cannot be specified.

This lesson from the commentary on Al-Husami clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi usul: the difference between muqtada (implied meaning) and mahdhuf (omitted word). Understanding this distinction determines whether a ruling derived from a text can be limited by intention or specification. We will see why, according to the Hanafi school, muqtada lacks generality (umum) and therefore cannot accept takhsis, contrary to the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
First, recognize that muqtada is a necessary implication of meaning for the ruling to be valid, while mahdhuf is a word omitted from the text that can be restored without distorting meaning.
Example: 'Ask the village' – 'people' is mahdhuf; 'Free a believing slave' – 'owned' is muqtada.
2
Analyze the difference using the marker test
The text proposes a marker: restoring mahdhuf changes the wording/grammar, while restoring muqtada does not. Evaluate this marker with provided exceptions.
The author shows counterexamples and resolves them by saying the true difference is that muqtada is established by shariah necessity, mahdhuf by linguistic convention.
3
Understand the rule: muqtada has no generality
Generality and specificity are properties of words, not meanings. Since muqtada is meaning, it cannot have generality, and thus cannot be specified (takhsis).
This is a foundational Hanafi usul principle.
4
Apply the rule to an oath example
If someone swears 'I will not drink', the object 'drink' is muqtada. According to Hanafis, any drink breaks the oath because the reality of drinking is present, not because of generality. The swearer cannot specify a particular drink by intention.
This contrasts with Shafi'i, who treat muqtada like wording and allow specification.
5
Distinguish between dalalat al-nass and isharat al-nass
Similarly, rulings established by dalalat al-nass (meaning as illa) do not accept takhsis, while those from isharat al-nass (based on wording) can be general and specified.
The passage begins this distinction but is cut off; the key point is that meaning-based derivations are not subject to specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفيض الحجازي۔ شرح اردو المنتخب الحسامي۔ ہیں کہ مقتضی کو اگر کلام میں ظاہر کر دو تو کلام میں کوئی تبدیلی نہیں آئیگی، کلام اپنی حالت پر رہے گا، اور مخذوف کو اگر ظاہر کر دو تو کلام میں تبدیلی آجائیگی، جس چیز کا تعلق پہلے مذکور سے تھا اب مخذوف سے ہو جائے گا جیسے وَاسْئَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ میں اہل محذوف ہے اگر اس کو ظاہر کیا جائے تو وَاسْئَلُ أَهْلَ الْقَرْيَةِ ہوگا تبدیلی آگئی، پہلے سوال قریہ سے تھا اب اہل سے ہو گیا، نیز پہلے قریہ مفعول بہ ہونے کی وجہ سے منصوب تھا اب مضاف الیہ کی وجہ سے مجرور ہو گیا، تو لفظا اور معنی کلام میں تغیر آ گیا، یہ مخذوف کی پہچان ہے، اور مقتضی سے کلام میں تبدیلی نہیں آئے گی جیسے فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ میں مقتضی ہے مملوکة، اگر اس کو ظاہر کر دیا جائے تو فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ میں کوئی فرق نہیں آئے گا، جیسے فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ مَمْلُوْكَةٍ، مگر مصنف نے جو پہچان بتلائی وہ قابل غور ہے، اس لئے کہ بہت سی مرتبہ اس کے برعکس فقلنا اضرب بعصاك الحجر فانفجرت منه اثنتا عشرة عينا، یہاں بیچ میں فَضَرَبَ فَانْشَقَ الْحَجَر محذوف ہے اس کو ذکرنے سے کلام میں کوئی تبدیلی نہیں آئی۔ حالانکہ یہ محذوف ہے، اس طرح اعتق عبدک عنی بالف، اس میں بیج کو مقدر مانا گیا ہے جو مقتضی ہے، اور مقتضی کو جب ظاہر کر دیا جائے اور یوں کہے بع عبدک عنى بالف ثم كن وكيلى بالاعتقاق تو کلام میں تبدیلی آجاتی ہے، کیونکہ پہلے وہ اپنے غلام کو آزاد کرنے کا مامور تھا اور اب وہ آمر کے غلام کو آزاد کرنے کا مامور ہوگا، تو دونوں مثالوں میں وہ علامت و پہچان ٹوٹ گئی۔ بعض حضرات نے جواب دیا ہے کہ یہ پہچان و علامت اکثری ہے، کلی نہیں ہے بھی اس کا تخلف بھی ہوسکتا ہے مگر یہ جواب تکلف سے خالی نہیں، لہذا صاف ستھری بات یہ ہے کہ مقتضی اور مخذوف میں بس یہی فرق ہے کہ مقتضی کلام میں شرعاً ثابت ہوتا ہے اور محذوف لغۃً اور یہ پہچان و علامت میں تکلف کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ ثُمَّ الثَّابِتُ بِمُقْتَضَى النَّصِّ لَا يَحْتَمِلُ التَّخْصِيْصَ حَتَّى لَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يَشْرَبْ وَنَوَىٰ شَرَابًا دُونَ شَرَابٍ لَا تُعْمَلُ نِيَّتُهُ لِأَنَّ الْمُقْتَضَى لَا عُمُومَ لَهُ خِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِيِّ وَالتَّخْصِيْصُ فِيمَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْعُمُومَ۔ ترجمہ:۔ پھر وہ حکم جو ثابت ہوتا ہے مقتضی النص سے وہ تخصیص کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے، یہاں تک کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ وہ نہیں پئے گا اور اس نے نیت کی ایک مشروب کی نہ کہ دوسرے کی، تو اس کی نیت پر عمل نہیں کیا جائے گا، اس لئے کہ مقتضی کے لئے عموم نہیں ہے امام شافعی کے خلاف، اور تخصیص اس چیز میں ہوتی ہے جو عموم کا احتمال رکھتی ہے۔ تشریح:- مصنف مقتضی اور محذوف میں دوسرا فرق بیان کر رہے ہیں اس سے پہلے یہ بات ذہن میں رکھیں کہ عموم و خصوص الفاظ کے عوارض میں سے ہے، نہ کہ معنی کے یعنی معنی میں عموم خصوص نہیں ہوتا ہے، الفاظ میں ہوتا ہے۔ دوسری بات جہاں عموم ہوتا ہے وہاں تخصیص بھی ہو سکتی ہے، اور جہاں عموم ہی نہ ہو وہاں تخصیص کا سوال ہی پیدا نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ تیسری بات محذوف الفاظ کے قبیل سے ہے جیسا کہ ظاہر ہے، اور مقتضی معنی کے قبیل سے ہے، تو معلوم ہوا کہ محذوف میں عموم ہوسکتا ہے، کیونکہ یہ الفاظ کے قبیل سے ہے، اور جب عموم ہو سکتا ہے تو تخصیص بھی ہوسکتی ہے اور مقتضی چونکہ معنی کے قبیل سے ہے، اس لئے اس میں عموم نہیں ہوسکتا ہے، تو جب عموم نہیں ہو سکتا ہے، تو تخصیص بھی نہیں ہو سکتی ہے، لہذا اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی لا یشرب وہ نہیں پئے گا تو یہاں مشروب کو بطریق اقتضاء مقدر مانیں گے کیونکہ شراب (پینا) بغیر مشروب کے متحقق نہیں ہو سکتا ہے، لہذا اقتضاء مشروب مقدر ہو گا تو مشروب مقتضی ہوا اور مقتضی میں عموم نہیں ہوتا ہے، لہذا تخصیص بھی نہیں ہوگی تو حالف جو چیز بھی پئے گا حانث ہو جائے گا، اگر وہ کہے کہ میری مراد فلاں مشروب تھی نہ کہ فلاں تو اس کی نیت کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ نیت سے وہ تخصیص کر رہا ہے، اور مقتضی میں عموم نہیں ہے، اس لئے تخصیص بھی نہیں ہے، البتہ امام شافعی مقتضی میں عموم و خصوص کے قائل ہیں، وہ اس کو محذوف کی طرح مانتے ہیں، لہذا امام شافعی کے نزدیک اگر حالف نے مخصوص شراب کی نیت کی تو اس کی نیت معتبر ہوگی۔ یہاں ایک طالب علمانہ سوال ہو سکتا ہے کہ حالف جو بھی پئے گا حانث ہو جائے گا تو مشروب میں عموم ہوا حالانکہ مقتضی میں عموم نہیں ہوتا ہے، جیسا کہ آپ نے فرمایا۔ جواب ہر چیز کے پینے سے حانث ہونا اس لئے نہیں ہے کہ عموم ہے بلکہ اس لئے ہے کہ ہر مشروب کے پینے میں شراب (پینے) کی ماہیت موجود ہے پس ماہیت شراب یعنی محلوف علیہ کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے حانث ہو جائے گا نہ کہ مشروب کے عام ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ وَكَذَلِكَ الثَّابِتُ بِدَلَالَةِ النَّصِّ لَا يَحْتَمِلُ التَّخْصِيْصَ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَى النَّصِّ إِذَا ثَبَتَ كَوْنُهُ عِلَّةً لَا يَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ غَيْرَ عِلَّةٍ وَأَمَّا الثَّابِتُ بِإِشَارَةِ النَّصِّ فَيَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ عَامًا يُخَصَّصُ لِأَنَّهُ ثَابِتٌ بِصِيغَةِ الْكَلَامِ وَالْعُمُومُ بِاعْتِبَارِ الصِّيغَةِ۔ ترجمہ:۔ اور اسی طرح وہ حکم جو دلالت النص سے ثابت ہوتا ہے تخصیص کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے، اس لئے کہ نص
Then the ruling established by the implication of the text (muqtada al-nass) does not accept specification (takhsis), to the extent that if someone swears 'I will not drink' and intends a specific drink and not another, his intention is not acted upon, because muqtada has no generality, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And specification is only in that which admits generality. Commentary: The author is explaining a second distinction between muqtada and mahdhuf. Keep in mind that generality and specificity are accidents of words, not of meaning; meaning does not have generality or specificity, only words do. Second: where there is generality, specification can occur; where there is no generality, specification is not possible. Third: mahdhuf belongs to the category of words, while muqtada belongs to the category of meaning. So mahdhuf can have generality, and thus can be specified, while muqtada, being meaning, cannot have generality, and thus cannot be specified. If someone swears 'I will not drink', the drink is implied by necessity (muqtada), because drinking cannot occur without a drink. The drink is muqtada, and muqtada has no generality, so no specification. Therefore, whatever he drinks, he breaks his oath; his intention to specify a certain drink is not valid. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that muqtada does have generality and specificity, treating it like mahdhuf, so his intention would matter. Objection: If he breaks the oath by drinking anything, does that imply generality? Answer: No, the oath is broken because every drink partakes of the reality of 'drinking' (the subject of the oath), not because of generality of the drink. Similarly, what is established by dalalat al-nass does not accept specification, because when the meaning of the text is established as an illa (cause), it cannot be other than a cause. As for what is established by isharat al-nass, it may be general and subject to specification, because it is established by the wording and generality is a matter of wording.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on Al-Husami, explaining two key usul concepts: muqtada (the implied but necessary meaning) and mahdhuf (the elliptically omitted word), and then the rule that the ruling derived from muqtada does not accept takhsis (specification). The author first distinguishes them: if you restore a mahdhuf, it changes the wording and sometimes the meaning; if you spell out muqtada, it does not change the wording. The examples: 'Ask the village' (Quran) — the word 'people' is mahdhuf; restoring it changes the grammatical structure. 'Freeing a believing slave' (Quran) — the implied 'owned' (mamluka) is muqtada; adding it does not change the sense. However, the author notes exceptions to this marker, like in the Quranic story of Moses striking the rock (where an omitted phrase 'he struck' is mahdhuf but restoring it doesn't change the sentence), and the example of someone saying 'Free your slave from me for a thousand' (where the implied purchase is muqtada but restoring it changes the command). The author resolves this by saying the real difference is that muqtada is established by shariah (subsidiary meaning necessary for the ruling) while mahdhuf is purely linguistic omission. Then the rule: what is established by muqtada al-nass has no generality, so it cannot be specified. For example, if one swears 'I will not drink,' the object 'drink' is implied necessarily (muqtada); since muqtada has no generality, the swearer cannot specify a particular drink by intention — any drink breaks the oath. This contradicts Imam al-Shafi'i, who treats muqtada like mahdhuf (which can be general and thus specified). The commentator explains that generality/specificity belong to words, not meanings; muqtada is meaning, so no generality. A student might object that if any drink breaks the oath, that seems like generality. The answer: the breaking is because each drink partakes of the reality of 'drinking' (the subject of the oath), not because of generality of the word 'drink'. Finally, the text begins to distinguish between dalalat al-nass (indication of the text) and isharat al-nass (pointer of the text): what is established by dalalat (like an illa) is also not subject to takhsis because it is a meaning; what is established by isharat is based on wording, so it can be general and specified. The passage cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
مقتضی (muqtada): The meaning that is necessarily implied by the text for the existence or validity of the ruling, without being explicitly stated.
محذوف (mahdhuf): A word that is elliptically omitted from the text for brevity, but can be restored without distorting the intended meaning.
تخصیص (takhsis): The specification of a general term to exclude some of its potential referents.
دلالة النص (dalalat al-nass): The indication of the text that establishes a meaning as a cause (illa) for a ruling.
إشارة النص (isharat al-nass): The pointer of the text that derives a ruling from the wording (sighah) and can be general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this lesson, if someone swears 'I will not drink' and later drinks water, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because water is a drink and the muqtada has no generality.
B) No, because water is not explicitly mentioned.
C) Yes, but only if he had no specific intention.
D) No, unless he drinks a prohibited beverage.

Key Takeaway
Muqtada (implied meaning) does not have generality and thus cannot be specified; rulings derived from muqtada apply universally to all instances that fulfill the necessary meaning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Muqtada vs Mahzuf: Where 'Umum Applies in Words, Not Meanings
Commentary distinguishing muqtada and mahzuf regarding 'umum and takhsis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a ruling derived from muqtada or mahzuf admits 'umum and takhsis, and apply the Hanafi position to an oath scenario.

Core Idea
Muqtada is meaning, not word — so it cannot be general or specified.

In Hanafi usul, 'umum (generality) and takhsis (specification) are properties of words, not of meanings. This lesson focuses on the distinction between muqtada (the necessary implied meaning) and mahzuf (the deleted word). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from implied elements in legal texts, especially in oaths and vows.

Guided Path
1
Classify the implied element
Determine whether the text implies a word (mahzuf) or a meaning (muqtada). Mahzuf is an omitted word that can be restored in the sentence; muqtada is a necessary meaning that is not expressed by any word.
For example, in 'la yashrab' (he will not drink), the object (mashrub) is not stated but is necessarily implied — this is muqtada.
2
Apply the rule of 'umum
Since 'umum is an attribute of words, mahzuf can be general and thus can be specified. Muqtada, being meaning, cannot be general and therefore cannot be specified.
This means that if muqtada is used, every instance that fulfills the meaning triggers the ruling.
3
Test the rule with the oath example
Take the oath 'la yashrab' (I will not drink). The implied drink is muqtada. According to the Hanafi view, any drink breaks the oath because each drink contains the essence of the thing sworn. The swearer cannot specify a particular drink.
The Shafi'i view differs: they treat muqtada like mahzuf, so the swearer's intention can specify, and only that specific drink breaks the oath.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Hanafi position, drinking any liquid that counts as drinkable breaks the oath; under the Shafi'i position, only the intended drink breaks it.
This affects fatwa in cases of ambiguous oaths.

شراب یعنی محلوف علیہ کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے حانث ہو جائے گا نہ کہ مشروب کے عام ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ وَكَذَلِكَ الثَّابِتُ بِدَلَالَةِ النَّضِ لَا يَحْتَمِلُ التَّخْصِيْصَ لِاَنَّ مَعْنَى النَّضِ إِذَا علت ہونے کا ، اور بہر حال وہ حکم جو ثابت ہوتا ہے اشارۃ النص سے وہ احتمال رکھتا ہے یہ کہ عام ہو جس میں تخصیص کی جائے ، اس لئے کہ یہ ( اشارۃ النص سے ثابت شدہ حکم ) ثابت ہے کلام کے صیغہ سے اور عموم صیغہ وہاں حرمت ثابت ہوگی ، اب اگر ہم تخصیص کے قائل ہوں یعنی ایذاء کی بعض صورتوں کو حرام کہیں اور بعض کو حرام نہ کہیں تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ بعض جگہ ایذاء ( علت ) موجود ہو اور حرمت (حکم ) نہ ہو ، تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ ایذ اعلت نہیں ہے، حالانکہ اس کا علت ہونا ثابت ہو چکا ہے، بہر حال تخصیص ثابت کرنے کی صورت میں بعض صورتوں میں جہاں تخصیص کریں گے ، علت کا غیر علت ہونا لازم آتا ہے اور یہ درست نہیں ہے، لہذا ثابت بدلالۃ النص میں عموم نہیں ہے، کیونکہ یہ معنی کے قبیل سے ہے اور عموم الفاظ کے عوارض میں سے ہے، اور جب عموم نہیں تو تخصیص بھی نہیں ہوگی ، لہذا جہاں بھی ایذا ( علت ) پائی جائے گی وہاں حرمت (حکم پایا جائے گا۔ یہ خیال رہے کہ اس جگہ علت (ایذا) کے عام ہونے کی وجہ سے حکیم ( حرمت) میں عموم آیا ہے، حکم (حرمت) کے عام ہونے کی وجہ سے عموم نہیں آیا ہے، لہذا اس طرح کے عموم کو عموم اور تعمیم نہیں کہا جاتا ہے اب رہا اشارۃ النص کا مسئلہ تو اشارة النص چونکہ عبارة النص کی طرح نظم اور صیغہ سے ثابت ہوتا ہے، اور عموم بھی صیغہ اور لفظ کے عوارض میں سے ہے، لہذا عبارة النص کی طرح اشارۃ النص میں بھی عموم و خصوص جاری ہوگا۔ جیسے مثال لا تقولوا لمن يقتل في سبيل الله اموات بل احياء ولكن لا تشعرون، اس آیت کا عبارة النص تو شہداء کا بلند درجہ بیان کرنا ہے، اور اشارۃ النص سے معلوم ہوا کہ شہداء پر نماز جنازہ نہیں پڑھی جائے گی ، اس لئے کہ اللہ نے ان کو احیاء کہا ہے جیسا کہ امام شافعی ، کا مذہب ہے ، اور نماز جنازہ نہ پڑھے جانے کا حکم عام ہے، لیکن اس میں حضرت حمزہ جی کی تخصیص کی گئی کیونکہ حضرت حمزہ جن پر حضور صلی یا تم نے ستر مرتبہ نماز جنازہ پڑھی الہ ہے، یہ مثال تو امام شافعی الہی کے مسلک پر ہوئی۔
Explanation: The author is explaining the second difference between muqtada and mahzuf. First, keep in mind that 'umum and takhsis are attributes of words, not meanings. Second, where there is 'umum, takhsis is possible; where there is no 'umum, takhsis is not possible. Third, mahzuf belongs to words, muqtada belongs to meaning. Thus mahzuf can have 'umum, muqtada cannot. If someone swears 'la yashrab' (he will not drink), the drink (mashrub) is implied by muqtada because drinking cannot occur without something drunk. The implied drink (muqtada) does not have 'umum, so no takhsis; the swearer breaks his oath whatever he drinks, and his intention to specify a particular drink is not considered. Imam Shafi'i holds that muqtada can have 'umum and takhsis like mahzuf. A student might ask: if the swearer breaks oath by any drink, that implies 'umum in the drink, contradicting the rule. The answer: he breaks oath not because the drink is general, but because every drink contains the essence of the prohibited drink (i.e., the substance of the thing sworn). Similarly, what is established by dalalat al-nass does not admit takhsis, because when the meaning of the text is established as a 'illa, it cannot be other than a 'illa. But what is established by isharat al-nass admits possibility of being general and specified, because it is established by the wording of the speech, and 'umum is considered from the wording. Explanation: The author says just as muqtada does not have 'umum, similarly a ruling established by dalalat al-nass does not have 'umum, so no takhsis. Because dalalat al-nass is a necessary meaning of the intended meaning, not a word; e.g., 'la taqul lahuma uff'—the intended meaning is prohibition of saying 'uff', and its necessary meaning is prohibition of causing harm. Harm is the 'illa. If we specify some harms as not prohibited, that would imply the 'illa is not operative, which is invalid. Thus no 'umum, no takhsis. Note: here the ruling (prohibition) is general because the 'illa (harm) is general, not because the ruling itself is general. As for isharat al-nass, since it is established by the wording and structure like 'ibarat al-nass, 'umum and takhsis apply. Example: 'la taquloo liman yuqtal fee sabeel illahi amwatun bal ahyaun'—the clear meaning ('ibara) is to elevate the status of martyrs, but the indication (ishara) is that funeral prayer should not be performed for martyrs because Allah calls them living. Imam Shafi'i holds this as general, but he specifies it by excluding Hamza (RA) because the Prophet prayed seventy janazah prayers over him.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that clarifies two important Usul distinctions: first, between muqtada (implied meaning) and mahzuf (deleted word), and second, between dalalat al-nass (textual implication) and isharat al-nass (textual indication). The commentator emphasizes that 'umum (generality) and takhsis (specification) are properties of words, not of meanings. Muqtada, being a meaning, cannot be general or specified, whereas mahzuf, being a word, can. This has practical effect in oaths: if someone swears 'I will not drink,' the implied object (drink) is muqtada, so any drink breaks the oath. Imam Shafi'i disagrees and treats muqtada like mahzuf, allowing specification by intention. The second part extends this logic to dalalat al-nass: it is based on the necessary meaning of the text (like the 'illa), not wording, so it cannot be specified. Isharat al-nass, however, is based on wording and can be general or specific. The example of martyrs and janazah prayer illustrates isharat al-nass: the indication that martyrs are alive leads to the ruling that janazah prayer is not performed for them, but this ruling can be specified (e.g., for Hamza). The commentary stays within Hanafi usul, presenting the Shafi'i position only for contrast.

Key Terms
مقتضی (muqtada): The necessary meaning implied by the wording, not explicitly stated; belongs to meaning, not words.
محذوف (mahzuf): A deleted word that is understood from context; belongs to words, so can have 'umum and takhsis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi usul, what is the status of 'umum and takhsis for muqtada (implied meaning)?
A) Muqtada has full 'umum and takhsis like mahzuf.
B) Muqtada has no 'umum, so no takhsis is possible.
C) Muqtada has 'umum but no takhsis.
D) Muqtada has takhsis but no 'umum.

Key Takeaway
Muqtada, as a necessary meaning, cannot be general or specified; only words (like mahzuf) admit 'umum and takhsis.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Dalalat al-Nass vs Isharat al-Nass: Generality in Meaning vs Wording
Commentary on 'umum and takhsis in dalalat al-nass and isharat al-nass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a ruling established by dalalat al-nass (which cannot be specified) and one established by isharat al-nass (which can), and explain why using the 'uff verse example.

Core Idea
Dalalat al-nass (textual implication) is meaning-based and immune to takhsis; isharat al-nass (textual indication) is wording-based and admits specification.

Just as muqtada cannot have 'umum or takhsis because it is meaning, the same applies to dalalat al-nass — a ruling derived from the necessary meaning (the 'illa) of a text. In contrast, isharat al-nass is derived from the wording and structure, so it shares the properties of 'ibarat al-nass, including the possibility of 'umum and takhsis. This lesson explores this distinction using Quranic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of derivation
Determine whether the ruling comes from the necessary logical implication of the text (dalalat al-nass) or from an indication inferred from the wording (isharat al-nass).
Dalalat al-nass is based on the 'illa (cause) that the text points to. Isharat al-nass is based on the structure and phrasing.
2
Classify the ruling as meaning-based or wording-based
If the ruling is the necessary meaning of the text's intended meaning, it is meaning-based (dalalat al-nass). If it is a secondary meaning derived from the wording, it is wording-based (isharat al-nass).
Example: 'La taqul lahuma uff' — the prohibition of saying 'uff' implies the prohibition of causing any harm to parents; this is dalalat al-nass.
3
Apply the rule on 'umum and takhsis
Dalalat al-nass, being meaning-based, cannot have 'umum or takhsis. Isharat al-nass, being wording-based, can have 'umum and takhsis like 'ibarat al-nass.
Specifying a ruling from dalalat al-nass would require denying the 'illa in some cases, which is contradictory.
4
Analyze the Quranic examples
For the 'uff verse: the 'illa is causing harm; harm is universal, so the prohibition applies to all harm. For the martyr verse: the indication (ishara) that martyrs are alive leads to the ruling of no janazah prayer; this ruling is general but can be specified (e.g., Hamza).
The Hanafi view on the martyr example is not given, but the text uses it to show that isharat al-nass admits takhsis.

نہیں پڑھی جائے گی ، اس لئے کہ اللہ نے ان کو احیاء کہا ہے جیسا کہ امام شافعی ، کا مذہب ہے ، اور نماز جنازہ نہ پڑھے جانے کا حکم عام ہے، لیکن اس میں حضرت حمزہ جی کی تخصیص کی گئی کیونکہ حضرت حمزہ جن پر حضور صلی یا تم نے ستر مرتبہ نماز جنازہ پڑھی الہ ہے، یہ مثال تو امام شافعی الہی کے مسلک پر ہوئی۔
Explanation: The author is explaining the second difference between muqtada and mahzuf. First, keep in mind that 'umum and takhsis are attributes of words, not meanings. Second, where there is 'umum, takhsis is possible; where there is no 'umum, takhsis is not possible. Third, mahzuf belongs to words, muqtada belongs to meaning. Thus mahzuf can have 'umum, muqtada cannot. If someone swears 'la yashrab' (he will not drink), the drink (mashrub) is implied by muqtada because drinking cannot occur without something drunk. The implied drink (muqtada) does not have 'umum, so no takhsis; the swearer breaks his oath whatever he drinks, and his intention to specify a particular drink is not considered. Imam Shafi'i holds that muqtada can have 'umum and takhsis like mahzuf. A student might ask: if the swearer breaks oath by any drink, that implies 'umum in the drink, contradicting the rule. The answer: he breaks oath not because the drink is general, but because every drink contains the essence of the prohibited drink (i.e., the substance of the thing sworn). Similarly, what is established by dalalat al-nass does not admit takhsis, because when the meaning of the text is established as a 'illa, it cannot be other than a 'illa. But what is established by isharat al-nass admits possibility of being general and specified, because it is established by the wording of the speech, and 'umum is considered from the wording. Explanation: The author says just as muqtada does not have 'umum, similarly a ruling established by dalalat al-nass does not have 'umum, so no takhsis. Because dalalat al-nass is a necessary meaning of the intended meaning, not a word; e.g., 'la taqul lahuma uff'—the intended meaning is prohibition of saying 'uff', and its necessary meaning is prohibition of causing harm. Harm is the 'illa. If we specify some harms as not prohibited, that would imply the 'illa is not operative, which is invalid. Thus no 'umum, no takhsis. Note: here the ruling (prohibition) is general because the 'illa (harm) is general, not because the ruling itself is general. As for isharat al-nass, since it is established by the wording and structure like 'ibarat al-nass, 'umum and takhsis apply. Example: 'la taquloo liman yuqtal fee sabeel illahi amwatun bal ahyaun'—the clear meaning ('ibara) is to elevate the status of martyrs, but the indication (ishara) is that funeral prayer should not be performed for martyrs because Allah calls them living. Imam Shafi'i holds this as general, but he specifies it by excluding Hamza (RA) because the Prophet prayed seventy janazah prayers over him.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that clarifies two important Usul distinctions: first, between muqtada (implied meaning) and mahzuf (deleted word), and second, between dalalat al-nass (textual implication) and isharat al-nass (textual indication). The commentator emphasizes that 'umum (generality) and takhsis (specification) are properties of words, not of meanings. Muqtada, being a meaning, cannot be general or specified, whereas mahzuf, being a word, can. This has practical effect in oaths: if someone swears 'I will not drink,' the implied object (drink) is muqtada, so any drink breaks the oath. Imam Shafi'i disagrees and treats muqtada like mahzuf, allowing specification by intention. The second part extends this logic to dalalat al-nass: it is based on the necessary meaning of the text (like the 'illa), not wording, so it cannot be specified. Isharat al-nass, however, is based on wording and can be general or specific. The example of martyrs and janazah prayer illustrates isharat al-nass: the indication that martyrs are alive leads to the ruling that janazah prayer is not performed for them, but this ruling can be specified (e.g., for Hamza). The commentary stays within Hanafi usul, presenting the Shafi'i position only for contrast.

Key Terms
دلالة النص (dalalat al-nass): Textual implication: a necessary meaning derived from the text's intended meaning (the 'illa or cause).
إشارة النص (isharat al-nass): Textual indication: a secondary meaning inferred from the wording and structure, like 'ibarat al-nass.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A ruling derived from dalalat al-nass (textual implication) in Hanafi usul:
A) Can be general and specified.
B) Cannot be general, but can be specified.
C) Can be general, but cannot be specified.
D) Cannot be general or specified.

Key Takeaway
Dalalat al-nass (meaning-based) cannot be general or specified; isharat al-nass (wording-based) can be general and admits specification.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Dalalah al-Nass vs Isharah al-Nass on Generality
Explaining why dalalah al-nass lacks generality while isharah al-nass has it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dalalah al-nass and isharah al-nass, and explain why the former lacks generality while the latter carries it.

Core Idea
Dalalah al-nass is meaning-based and cannot be general; isharah al-nass is wording-based and carries generality.

In Hanafi usul, understanding how rulings are extracted from texts requires careful attention to the type of implication. This lesson explores two key types: dalalah al-nass (implied meaning from necessary consequence) and isharah al-nass (meaning indicated by wording). The critical difference lies in whether generality applies—a distinction that impacts how far a ruling extends.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of implication
Distinguish between 'ibarah al-nass (explicit statement), isharah al-nass (indicated meaning), and dalalah al-nass (implied meaning). Focus on the latter two.
Only isharah and dalalah are compared here.
2
Understand the basis of generlity
Recognize that generlity ('umum) is an attribute of wording (sighat), not of meaning. Rulings derived from meaning alone do not have generlity.
3
Apply to the Quranic example of parents
Analyze verse 17:23: 'Do not say to them 'uff'.' The literal ruling forbids saying 'uff'; the necessary meaning (dalalah al-nass) forbids all harm to parents. Since this is based on the meaning, not wording, it cannot be specified (takhsis).
The cause (harm) is general, but the ruling is not general in wording.
4
Contrast with isharah al-nass
Isharah al-nass, like 'ibarah, is based on wording and structure, so it carries generlity and can be specified. Example: the verse on martyrs (2:154) indicates no funeral prayer for martyrs, but it was specified for Hamzah.
This illustrates how isharah al-nass is treated similarly to explicit text.
5
Consolidate the principle
Conclude: dalalah al-nass is meaning-based, so no generlity/takhsis; isharah al-nass is wording-based, so generlity/takhsis apply.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi usul.

کے معنی جب کہ ثابت ہو گیا اس کا علت ہونا تو وہ احتمال نہیں رکھے گا غیر علت ہونے کا ، اور بہر حال وہ حکم جو ثابت ہوتا ہے اشارۃ النص سے وہ احتمال رکھتا ہے یہ کہ عام ہو جس میں تخصیص کی جائے ، اس لئے کہ یہ ( اشارۃ النص سے ثابت شدہ حکم ) ثابت ہے کلام کے صیغہ سے اور عموم صیغہ کے اعتبار سے ہوتا ہے۔

یہ خیال رہے کہ اس جگہ علت (ایذا) کے عام ہونے کی وجہ سے حکیم ( حرمت) میں عموم آیا ہے، حکم (حرمت) کے عام ہونے کی وجہ سے عموم نہیں آیا ہے، لہذا اس طرح کے عموم کو عموم اور تعمیم نہیں کہا جاتا ہے اب رہا اشارۃ النص کا مسئلہ تو اشارة النص چونکہ عبارة النص کی طرح نظم اور صیغہ سے ثابت ہوتا ہے، اور عموم بھی صیغہ اور لفظ کے عوارض میں سے ہے، لہذا عبارة النص کی طرح اشارۃ النص میں بھی عموم و خصوص جاری ہوگا۔ جیسے مثال لا تقولوا لمن يقتل في سبيل الله اموات بل احياء ولكن لا تشعرون، اس آیت کا عبارة النص تو شہداء کا بلند درجہ بیان کرنا ہے، اور اشارۃ النص سے معلوم ہوا کہ شہداء پر نماز جنازہ نہیں پڑھی جائے گی ، اس لئے کہ اللہ نے ان کو احیاء کہا ہے جیسا کہ امام شافعی کا مذہب ہے ، اور نماز جنازہ نہ پڑھے جانے کا حکم عام ہے، لیکن اس میں حضرت حمزہ کی تخصیص کی گئی کیونکہ حضرت حمزہ پر حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے ستر مرتبہ نماز جنازہ پڑھی، یہ مثال تو امام شافعی کے مسلک پر ہوئی۔ مسلک احناف پر مثال على المولود له رزقهن عبارت النص سے تو باپ پر نفقہ کا ثبوت معلوم ہوا مگر اشارۃ النص سے یہ معلوم ہوا کہ باپ کو بیٹے کے تمام مال میں تصرف کا حق ہے، اور یہ حکم عام ہے مگر اس سے بیٹے کی باندی سے وطی کی تخصیص کی گئی، کہ یہ باپ کے لئے جائز نہیں ہے، اگر باپ نے بیٹے کی باندی سے وطی کر لی تو اس کی قیمت واجب ہوگی۔
When its being a cause is established, it will not have the possibility of not being a cause. And in any case, the ruling that is established from isharah al-nass has the possibility that it is general and subject to specification, because it (the ruling established from isharah al-nass) is established from the wording of the speech, and generality occurs by virtue of the wording. Explanation: The author (may Allah have mercy on him) says: Just as muqtada does not have generality or specificity, similarly the ruling established from dalalah al-nass does not have generality. And when there is no generality, there will be no specification, because specificity and generality are among the accidents of words. And the ruling from dalalah al-nass is a necessary meaning of the subject matter, not of the word; that is, dalalah al-nass is of the category of meaning, not of the category of wording. Because the ruling from dalalah al-nass is a necessary meaning of the subject matter, like 'Do not say to them 'uff.' Here, the subject matter is the prohibition of saying 'uff,' and its necessary meaning is the prohibition of causing harm. And this prohibition of harm is the cause of the prohibition of 'uff. Therefore, wherever this cause exists, the prohibition will be established. Now if we subscribe to specification, meaning we declare some forms of harm as forbidden and others as not forbidden, that would mean that in some places the cause (harm) exists but the ruling (prohibition) does not. That would imply that harm is not a cause, even though its being a cause has been established. In any case, if specification were affirmed, it would necessitate that the cause becomes non-cause in some cases, which is incorrect. Therefore, there is no generality in what is established by dalalah al-nass, because it belongs to the category of meaning, and generality is among the accidents of words. And when there is no generality, there will be no specification. So wherever harm (the cause) is found, there the prohibition (the ruling) will be found. Note: Here, due to the generality of the cause (harm), generality has come in the ruling (prohibition); it is not that generality came because the ruling is general. Therefore, this kind of generality is not called 'umum or ta'mim. Now, regarding the issue of isharah al-nass: because isharah al-nass is established from the arrangement and wording, just like 'ibarah al-nass, and generality is also among the accidents of wording and speech, therefore generality and specificity will apply in isharah al-nass just as in 'ibarah al-nass. For example, the verse 'Do not say about those who are killed in the way of Allah, 'They are dead.' Rather, they are living, but you perceive [it] not.' The 'ibarah al-nass of this verse is to express the high status of martyrs. And from isharah al-nass, it is understood that the funeral prayer is not performed for martyrs, because Allah called them living, as is the madhhab of Imam al-Shafi'i. And the ruling of not performing funeral prayer is general, but it was specified for Hadrat Hamzah (may Allah be pleased with him) because the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed the funeral prayer over him seventy times. This example was according to the madhhab of Imam al-Shafi'i. According to the Hanafi madhhab, for the example 'And upon the father is their provision,' from 'ibarah al-nass the obligation of maintenance on the father is understood, but from isharah al-nass it is understood that the father has the right to dispose of all of the son's property. And this ruling is general, but from it the father's intercourse with the son's slave-girl was specified, that it is not permissible for the father. If the father has intercourse with the son's slave-girl, then its value becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains a key distinction in Hanafi usul between two types of textual implication: dalalah al-nass (implied meaning) and isharah al-nass (indicated meaning). The author clarifies that dalalah al-nass does not carry generality ('umum) because it is derived from the necessary meaning of the subject matter, not from the wording itself. Since generality is an attribute of wording, rulings from dalalah al-nass apply wherever the cause ('illah) exists, without being subject to specification (takhsis). The example given is the verse 'Do not say to them 'uff' (Quran 17:23). The literal subject is prohibition of saying 'uff', but its necessary meaning is prohibition of any form of harm to parents. This prohibition of harm is general in reality but not in wording, so it cannot be specified to exclude some forms of harm. In contrast, isharah al-nass is based on the wording and structure of the text, so like 'ibarah al-nass (explicit statement), it can be general and can be specified. Two examples are provided: according to Imam Shafi'i, the verse about martyrs (Quran 2:154) indicates that funeral prayer is not performed for them, a general ruling that was specified for Hamzah; according to the Hanafi madhhab, the verse 'And upon the father is their provision' (Quran 2:233) indicates the father's general right over the son's property, but that is specified to exclude intercourse with the son's slave-girl.

Key Terms
دلالۃ النص (dalalah al-nass): The implied meaning derived from the necessary consequence of the subject matter, not from the wording.
اشارۃ النص (isharah al-nass): The meaning indicated by the wording and structure, similar to 'ibarah al-nass in carrying generality.
عموم ('umum): Generality that arises from the wording of the text; an accident of words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does dalalah al-nass not have generality?
A) Because it is based on wording, which does not carry generality.
B) Because it is derived from the necessary meaning of the subject matter, not from wording.
C) Because it can be specified like 'ibarah al-nass.
D) Because it is a weak type of evidence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, dalalah al-nass is meaning-based and lacks generality; isharah al-nass is wording-based and carries generality, allowing for specification.

Invalid Method: Specification by Proper Name
Refuting the claim that naming something excludes all others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the claim that specifying something by name negates the ruling from others is invalid, and apply this critique to similar arguments.

Core Idea
A text's silence on an unmentioned case cannot prove negation or affirmation—only valid inference can extend a ruling.

Some scholars argue that if a ruling is explicitly attached to a proper name or common noun, it automatically excludes all other cases. This seems intuitive, but it is a flawed method of inference. The Hanafi school demonstrates that such a claim puts words into the text's mouth: since the text does not address those other cases, it cannot be used to prove anything about them. In this lesson, we will dissect this argument, examine the example of the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'', and understand why silence does not equal negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opposing Claim
State the claim that explicit mention of a case by name (ism 'alam) necessitates the ruling being exclusive to that case and negated from all others.
This claim is attributed to some Hanabila, Ash'ariyya, Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq, and Abu Hamid al-Ghazali.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Refutation
Understand the key argument: the text does not encompass (lam yatanawalu) the unmentioned case, so it cannot establish a ruling—positive or negative—for it.
The text is silent on the unmentioned case; silence cannot be interpreted as negation.
3
Examine the Example
Consider the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' where ghusl is required for semen. The opposing view says this negates ghusl for other cases. The Hanafi response: the hadith does not address those cases; qiyas or other evidence must be used.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is seen in cases like penetration without ejaculation (iksal).
4
Apply the Principle
Test the principle: if a Quranic verse says 'Cut off the hand of a thief', does it automatically negate other punishments for theft? According to Hanafi usul, other punishments can be established by separate evidence.
This step reinforces that ruling on unmentioned cases requires independent proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ وَمِنَ النَّاسِ مَنْ عَمِلَ فِى النُّصُوْصِ بِوُجُوْهِ أَخَرَهِيَ فَاسِدَةٌ عِنْدَنَا مِنْهَا مَا قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ إِنَّ التَّنْصِيْصَ عَلَى الشَّئ بِاسْمِهِ الْعَلَم يُوجِبُ التَّخْصِيْصَ وَنَفْيَ الْحُكْمِ عَمَّا عَدَاهُ وَهَذَا فَاسِدٌ لِاَنَّ النَّصَّ لَمْ يَتَنَاوَلُهُ فَكَيْفَ يُوجِبُ الْحُكْمَ فِيْهِ نَفْيَا أَوْاِثْبَاتًا
ترجمہ : ۔ یہ فصل ہے ( وجوہ فاسدہ کے بیان میں ) اور لوگوں میں سے بعض وہ ہیں جو نصوص میں عمل کرتے ہیں دوسری وجوہ سے جو ہمارے نزدیک فاسد ہیں ، ان وجوہ فاسدہ میں سے وہ ہے جو بعض لوگوں نے کہا کہ کسی بھی کی اس کے اسم علم کے ساتھ صراحت کرنا تخصیص کو واجب کرتا ہے اور حکم کی نفی کو واجب کرتا ہے اس کے علاوہ سے، اور یہ وجہ فاسد ہے، اس لئے کہ نص اس (مسکوت عنہ ) کو شامل ہی نہیں ہے تو اس کے اندر حکم کو نفی یا اثبات کے طور پر ثابت کرے گی۔
تشریح:- مصنف انہی مذکورہ چار طریقوں کے علاوہ سے استدلال کے طرق بیان کر رہے ہیں ، جو ہمارے نزد یک فاسد ہیں بعض حضرات ان سے استدلال کرتے ہیں بعض سے مراد بعض حنابلہ ، بعض اشعریہ ابوبکر دقاق اور ابوحامد غزالی ہیں، اور یہاں علم سے مراد صفت پر دلالت کرنے والا لفظ نہ ہو، چاہے علم ہو یا اسم جنس ہو۔ بہر حال پہلی فاسد وجہ جس کے قائل بعض لوگ ہیں ، وہ یہ ہے کہ جب کوئی حکم کسی علم پر منصوص طریقہ پر لگایا جائے تو یہ صراحت کے ساتھ علم پر حکم لگانا یہ دلالت کرے گا اس بات پر کہ یہ حکم اس اسم علم کے ساتھ خاص ہے،اس کے علاوہ سے یہ حکم منتفی ہے، جیسے مسلم شریف کی حدیث الماء من الماء پہلے ماء سے مراد نسل اور دوسرے ماء سے مراد منی ہے، تو اس حدیث میں اسم جنس یعنی ماء ثانی (منی) پر غسل کا حکم لگایا گیا ہے، لہذا غسل کا وجوب منی کے ساتھ خاص ہوگا اور منی کے علاوہ سے حکم مشتفی ہوگا، لہذا اگر منی نہ نکلے جیسے اکسال (اکسال کے معنی اخراج الذکر من القبل قبل الانزال ) کی صورت میں تو اس سے غسل واجب نہ ہوگا، جیسا کہ بعض انصار مدینہ نے اس حدیث سے یہی سمجھا ہے جب کہ وہ اہل زبان ہیں ،معلوم ہوا کہ اسم علم پر صراحت کے ساتھ حکم لگانا اس علم کے ماعدا سے حکم کی نفی کرتا ہے
Section: And among people are those who work with texts in other ways that are invalid according to us. Among them is what some said: that specifying a thing by its proper name necessitates specification and negation of the ruling from other than it. And this is invalid, because the text does not encompass that (other thing), so how can it establish a ruling for it, either negative or affirmative? Translation: This is a section (on invalid ways). And among people are those who work with texts in other ways that are invalid according to us. Among these invalid ways is the one some people said: that specifying something by its proper name necessitates specification and necessitates negation of the ruling from other than it. And this is an invalid reason, because the text does not include that (unmentioned thing), so it can establish a ruling for it, whether negative or affirmative. Explanation: The author is stating ways of inference other than the mentioned four, which are invalid according to us. Some people use them. 'Some' refers to some Hanabila, some Ash'ariyya, Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq, and Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. And here 'name' does not mean a word indicating a quality; whether it is a proper noun or a common noun. In any case, the first invalid reason, held by some people, is that when a ruling is applied to a name in a specified manner, this explicit application of the ruling to the name indicates that this ruling is specific to that name, and the ruling is negated from other than it. For example, the hadith in Sahih Muslim: 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' (water from water). The first 'water' means the child, and the second 'water' means semen. So in this hadith, the ruling of ghusl is applied to the common noun 'water' (semen). Therefore, the obligation of ghusl is specific to semen, and the ruling is negated from other than semen. So if semen is not emitted, as in the case of iksal (meaning withdrawal of the penis from the vulva before ejaculation), then ghusl is not obligatory, as some of the Ansar of Madinah understood from this hadith, even though they are native speakers. Thus, applying a ruling explicitly to a name negates the ruling from other than that name.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This section introduces a fasl (chapter) on invalid methods of textual inference. The first invalid method is the claim that when a ruling is explicitly attached to a specific name (proper or common noun), it necessitates that the ruling is exclusive to that named entity and negated from everything else. The author refutes this by arguing that the text does not address the unmentioned cases at all, so it cannot be used to affirm or negate a ruling for them. The example given is the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'', which some understood to mean that ghusl is only required when semen is emitted, not in cases like penetration without ejaculation. The Hanafi position is that this inference is invalid; the ruling is not necessarily exclusive to the named cause. The text criticizes this as a false analogy and a misunderstanding of how texts work. The correct approach is to consider other principles like qiyas or istihsan.

Key Terms
اسم علم (ism 'alam): A proper name or common noun used to specify a particular entity or kind.
تخصیص (takhsis): Specification of a ruling to a particular case, excluding others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi school, why is the claim that specifying a thing by name necessitates negation of the ruling from others invalid?
A) Because the text does not encompass the other cases, so it cannot prove anything about them.
B) Because the ruling is always general unless specified otherwise.
C) Because proper names cannot carry rulings.
D) Because the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma' is weak.

Key Takeaway
Explicit mention of a case does not automatically negate the ruling from other cases; silence on a matter is not evidence of negation.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

First Invalid Method: Specific Naming Requires Exclusivity
Explains and refutes the claim that naming something implies exclusivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the invalid method of deducing exclusivity from explicit naming, apply the Hanafi principle that a text only governs what it covers, and distinguish between lam al-istighraq and the false principle of exclusivity.

Core Idea
Explicit naming affirms the ruling only for the named; it neither negates nor affirms for the unnamed.

In this lesson, we examine a common but invalid method of interpreting legal texts: claiming that when a ruling is explicitly attached to a specific name (e.g., 'Zaid' or 'semen'), that ruling is exclusive to that name and negated from all else. The Hanafi school rejects this reasoning, arguing that a text simply does not address what it does not mention. We will walk through the refutation, the counterargument from the Ansar's understanding of a hadith, and the Hanafi responses including abrogation and legal estimation.

Guided Path
1
State the invalid method and the Hanafi position
Identify the claim that explicit naming (التنصيص) necessitates exclusivity and negation from others. Then state the Hanafi position: the text only covers the named; it gives no ruling for the unnamed because it does not include them.
The Hanafi position is based on the principle that silence is not a source of law.
2
Analyze the opposing proof
Opponents cite the Ansar's understanding of the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma' to argue that naming semen implies ghusl is only required when semen is emitted. Understand their reasoning: because the hadith explicitly mentions semen, they believe it negates the obligation in other cases.
The Ansar's interpretation is historically significant but methodologically flawed in Hanafi view.
3
Apply the Hanafi refutation using the 'Zaid came' analogy
Use the example 'Zaid came' to show that stating Zaid's arrival does not affirm or deny anyone else's arrival. Extend to a dangerous case: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' would imply no other messengers, which is absurd. This demonstrates the invalidity of the method.
The reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid) is a common Hanafi argument.
4
Reinterpret the Ansar's understanding
The Hanafi response is that the Ansar did not rely on the naming principle but on lam al-istighraq (the 'al' for totality). They interpreted 'al-ma' as 'all instances of semen', meaning only seminal emission triggers ghusl. This is not the same as exclusivity from naming.
This distinction shows that the same textual result can arise from different linguistic mechanisms.
5
Provide alternative Hanafi arguments for ghusl
Even if the hadith were taken as evidence, Hanafis offer two defenses: (a) the ruling was abrogated (naskh) later, making ghusl obligatory upon insertion regardless of emission; (b) even without abrogation, the meeting of circumcised parts (iltaqa al-khitanan) is legally equivalent to seminal emission via taqdir (estimation), similar to travel being estimated as hardship.
This shows the Hanafi school's ability to harmonize apparent contradictions through usul principles.
